PREFACE
This report is the result of a master thesis in MSc Architectural Engineering 
at the building department of the Technical University of Denmark (DTU). 
The master thesis has had a duration of five months starting on the 26th of 
January 2015 and with a final deadline on the 26th of June 2015. 
We are two master students, Christina Erikshøj and Mathilde Landgren 
involved in the project, both writing for 30 ECTS points, resulting in a total of 
60 ECTS points for the thesis. 
We wish to thank the people who have been involved in our thesis during the 
process. First of all a thank you to our supervisor Toke Rammer Nielsen from 
DTU, who have supported us with guidance and advice. 
A great thanks to Jørgen Schultz from Steensen Varming, who has been a 
great support as well. It has been a pleasure for us to work with a real case 
and gaining experience from this.
Furthermore we appreciate the coorporation with Henrik Schmidt and 
Robert Janson from Lundgaard & Traanberg Architects and for involving us 
in their project T30 and by showing interest in our project and contributions 
through the process. 
At last but not at least we would like to say thanks to the intervieewed 
architects and engineers for spending time and energy on us and our project. 
Signatures:

II
RESUME
Glashuse har været omdebatteret indenfor byggebranchen gennem de senere 
år, da fokus på energioptimering og energieffektive byggerier er intensiveret. 
Denne fokus har ledt til højere krav til bygninger i brugstiden, herunder er 
klimaskærmen en vigtig parameter. For glasbyggerier afstedkommer dette 
flere udfordringer i forhold til indeklima og dermed også energi. 
Indeværende kandidat speciale ønsker, at undersøge perspektiver på 
glashuset, med fokus på facaden, både i forhold til fordele og ulemper.
Flere perspektiver på glashuset er blevet tilgået med en holistisk vinkel 
gennem et omfattende litteraturstudie, og der er også samlet viden via flere 
interviews af både arkitekter og ingeniører. Ved at følge et konkret projekt, 
er betydningen af de tæt samarbejde mellem arkitekter og ingeniører blevet 
understreget. Gennem de to forløb er flere konkrete problemstillinger blevet 
identificeret og sideløbende løst i den tekniske del af specialet. 
De tekniske undersøgelser har taget udgangspunkt i dagslyset i byggeriet, 
da de foretagne interviews og projektmøder lagde stor vægt på dagslys 
og transparens som en afgørende arkitektonisk værdi. Ved metodisk at 
graduere glasfordelingen i facaden ud fra den lokale kontekst har det været 
muligt at reducere glasarealet uden at forringe dagslyset. Dagslys Faktoren 
ses som en god indikator for  dagslysforholdene i de tidlige designfaser, hvor 
Useful Daylight Illuminance (UDI) er yderst anvendelig gennem hele design 
processen, fordi den både kan give et billede af områder med overbelysning 
såvel som underbelysning. Dagslysundersøgelserne er dermed brugbare til 
yderligere at kunne definere zoneinddeling af kunstig belysning med henblik 
på beregning af energiforbrug samt at optimere solafskærmningen, hvor der 
er behov. 
Tekniske undersøgelser relateret til udformningen af facaden i forhold til 
glasarealer, placering af disse og brug af solafskærmning er blevet lavet 
for at kunne vurdere betydningen af disse tiltag, uafhængigt af hinanden. 
Undersøgelserne viser, at det ikke er lige så effektivt at reducere glasarealet 
som det er at bruge en solafskærmning. For at opnå den samme effekt 
skal glasarealet reduceres til omkring 40 %. Yderligere viste det sig stadig 
problematisk at overholde selv energirammen for BR10, på trods af at 
tiltagene også blev kombineret. Undersøgelser tyder altså på, at det ikke er 
via modificeringer af selve facaden, at de store energibesparelser ligger, så 
glasarealet er ikke af så afgørende betydning, hvilket giver en større design 
frihed og mulighed for arkitektonisk kvalitet. 
==PAGE:5==
III
ABSTRACT
Glasshouses has been subject to discussions within the building industry  in 
recent years, as the focus on energy optimization and energy efficient building 
design has increased. This focus has resulted in more comprehensive demands 
to the buildings during the occupied hours, and the building envelope is a 
significant parameter. For glasshouses this gives rise to challenges concerning 
indoor climate and thereby also the energy consumption. 
This thesis has investigated the potential of glasshouses with focus on the 
facade, both in regard to beneficial qualities and disadvantages.
 
The perspectives of the glasshouse have been addressed from a holistic 
approach and comprises a wide literature study, and knowledge has also been 
gathered from interviews with both architects and engineers. In addition to 
this we have followed a real design project, which has made us realize that a 
smooth and close cooperation between architects and engineers is vital for 
a design project.
The technical studies are based on the daylight in the building, as the 
statements during the interviews and the project meetings placed great 
emphasis on daylight and transparency as vital issues for the architectural 
value. During methodical grading of the glass distribution in the facade seen 
in the local context it proved to be possible to reduce the glazing area without 
reducing the daylight. The Daylight Factor is considered a good indicator for 
the daylight conditions in the early phases of the design project, whereas 
the Useful Daylight Illuminance (UDI) is a very useful parameter throughout 
the complete process, because this parameter can serve as illustration both 
for areas with too high and too low levels of light. The daylight investigations 
are thereby useful to be able to define the division in zones with the purpose 
of calculation of the energy consumption and optimization of solar shading, 
where needed.
The technical investigations related to the design of the facade regarding 
the glazing areas, the distribution of these and the use of solar shading has 
been performed to be able to evaluate the impact of these approaches, 
independently of each other. The investigations show that it is not as efficient 
to reduce the glazing area as it is to use solar shading. In order to achieve 
the same effect the glazing area must be reduced to approximately 40 %. 
Furthermore it proved to be problematic to fulfil even the energy performance 
framework of Building Class 2010, despite the fact that the approaches also 
were combined. Thereby the investigations indicate that significant savings 
cannot be achieved through such adjustments of the facade design, so the 
glazing area is not of that great importance, which brings more freedom and 
better possibilities for the architectural quality.

1. INTRODUCTION
==PAGE:10==

1. INTRODUCTION
1.1 BACKGROUND
The glasshouse is a debated typology, as the benefits of the design also 
causes side-effects concerning the visual comfort, thermal indoor climate 
and energy consumption. 
The energy use for buildings amounts to 30-40% of the total energy 
consumption in Denmark [SBi 2013-05]. As the building mass represents a 
major share of the total energy consumption, an increased focus has been to 
try to limit the energy consumption from the buildings. During the past years 
the building requirements for energy and indoor climate have been tightened 
to decrease the energy consumption, and this has led to an increased focus 
on glasshouses. 
Despite of the increased focus on energy, there is generally still a high 
percentage of glazing in new buildings. The inconsistency of an increased 
focus on energy efficiency and the high amount of new high glazed buildings 
is an interesting development. 
The concerns about glass buildings are often based upon the increased 
energy consumption due to the high percentage of glass in the facade, as it 
will lead to higher energy consumption for heating and cooling to stabilize 
the indoor climate compared to other building types. These higher energy 
demands are mainly caused by the high U-values of the glass and  frames 
compared to the insulated facades. Therefore it can be difficult to fulfill the 
energy performance framework for the latest energy demands. 
It can therefore be questioned whether it is possible to have an totally 
glazed facade at all, and how much the percentage of glass would have to be 
decreased to fulfill the energy demands.  
Looking at the problematics, energy is required to accommodate indoor 
environment demands, and at the same time regulations are set to limit the 
energy use, which pull the demands in two seperate directions, whether to 
prioritise the indoor environment demands or the energy demands. These 
inconsistenties makes building design in glass sensible, as there are challenges 
both according to the indoor environment and the energy efficiency.  
The high amount of glass can be explained by the related advantages, for 
instance free heatgain from the solar radiation, which can at some times of 
the year decrease the energy demand for heating. Furthermore the increased 
daylight has the advantage of a decreased energy consumption for artificial 
light.
Daylight is in general evaluated as one of the biggest advantages, when 
having a high percentage of glass in the facade, as it results in a high Daylight 
Factor (DF) in the building. The recent years the demands for the daylight in 
a building has increased, so there is an inconsistency of whether to prioritise 
the indoor environment demands related to the daylight or the energy 
demands. 
As it appears there are variuos opinions, whether it is a good idea to build 
with high percentage of glass in the facade, as the material properties of 
glass have considerable benefits but also considerable disadvantages which 
==PAGE:11==
3
1. INTRODUCTION
influence on each other and makes it a complex question. 
The relations between the facade design, the indoor environment and 
the energy consumption for glasshouses is very challenging but also very 
complex. This will therefor be the base for further investigations...
==PAGE:12==
4
1. INTRODUCTION
1.2 PROBLEM STATEMENT
Glass is a material widely used in building envelopes, but the material is not 
unproblematic in relation to indoor environment and energy consumption 
therefore issues regarding these aspects are often seen in glass buildings. 
The thesis seeks to cover and investigate the qualities and issues when 
building in glass from a holistic perspective. Therefore three approaches to 
cover the subject will be taken. 
Firstly the subject will be studied through literature to gain background 
knowledge and identify the problems related to indoor environment and 
energy. A study to get a holistic perspective of current problematics will be 
made by conducting interviews with both architects and engineers from the 
business world and thereby clarifying the qualities and issues seen from both 
professions and discuss the cooperation as a mean, to make more optimised 
solutions.  
Secondly the thesis will follow the process of a specific case in the finishing 
stage of a design concept for a glass building with an engineering firm and an 
architectural firm. Following a process, the intention is to clarify specific issues 
encountered during the process in regard to daylight, indoor environment 
and energy consumption.
Thirdly, the desire is to implement the gathered knowledge from the study 
and process in relation to problem areas for glass buildings into technical 
investigations.
The desire is to make investigations relating to the building envelope in the 
perspectives of daylight, indoor envrionment and energy consumption. More 
precise the effect on these three perspectives, when changes are made for 
the building envelope. The changes, which is wanted to be studied, is the 
glass area and distribution to clarify how big an impact these changes have 
on optimising the building envelope. To be able to make these investigations 
a dynamic building energy software will be used for providing knowledge 
on the different subjects within the indoor environment and energy, while 
daylight simulations tools will be used to document the daylight conditions 
in the building. 
Through the process and the gained knowledge within the three approaches 
parallels will be drawn to see how the different aspects influence on each 
other and if general conclusions can be made.  
==PAGE:13==
5
1. INTRODUCTION
1.3 INTRODUCTION TO REPORT
The final product of the master thesis consists of this main report and an 
attached appendix collection. First there are some overall prerequisites, 
which are important to mention before introducing the detailed format of 
the report.
The focus for the project is concerning buildings with a high percentage of 
glass in the thermal envelope, and does not include double skin facades. 
Mainly the facade design, and its impact on the daylight, the indoor climate 
and the energy consumption, will be evaluated. 
To be able to get some qualified results, the project is based upon a real 
project case “T30”. The responsible architects are Lundgaard & Tranberg, 
who assigned the engineering company Steensen Varming to assist with 
technical knowledge and documentation. As the developer has an agenda 
for publishing the project, there will be a certain degree of anonymization in 
the report. 
The project “T30” is in the design phase, where the inputs from the engineers 
still can have an influence on the facade design and layout of the plans. Having 
a real case as base for the project gives a direct insight in the coorporation 
between the architects and the engineers, and how big an influence the 
engineers can have at the development of the design.
The technical investigations have their base in the Danish building 
requirements and regulations, and the climatic data for Stockholm in Sweden, 
as this is the local context for the real case project, T30. 
The format of the report is overall divided into the following three chapters: 
	
Literature Studies
	
Process in Praxis
	
Technical Investigations
The three chapters can be read independently, but to give the overall picture 
they have to be considered together, as the thesis has been developed in 
in a synergy between the processes. Through the literature studies and 
the process in praxis parallels will be linked together and to the thechnical 
investigations.

2. METHOD
2.2 USED PROGRAMS
Through the process different simulation programs have been used, which 
will be described in this section. 
Pilkington Spectrum: 	Used to investigate the construction of the glazing, 
	
	
	
further descriptions are available in “Appendix B - 
	
	
	
Pilkington Spectrum”.
VDV: 	 	
	
“Velux Daylight Visualizer”,  used for daylight 
	
	
	
simulations.
DaySim:	
	
Used for further daylight simulations. 
IESVE: 		
	
“Integrated Environmental Solutions – Virtual 
	
	
	
Environment” a dynamic simulation tool, used for the 
	
	
	
overall simulations in the technical investigations.
2.2.1 VELUX DAYLIGHT VISUALIZER
One of the main focuses in the report is the daylight in glasshouses. The 
simulation program “Velux Daylight Visualizer” (VDV) is used to make the 
main daylight simulations. The program has been chosen because of the 
good illustrative outputs, and as it is proved to be good for simulations in 
different settings compared to other programs. Figure 2.2 shows the results 
for a test for several available daylight simulation tools, done by the State 
Building institute, SBi, in Denmark [SBi 2013:20]. 
Figure 2.2: Different programs ability of calculating the daylight factor in 5 different room types [SBi 
2013:20].
VDV is a simulation tool intended to ease the documentation and calculation 
of daylight for professionals. The program is developed by Luxion in 
collaboration with the Velux Group, a big Danish developer of skylights 
[Velux].
IMPORT FROM SKETCHUP
There are two ways of modelling in Velux Daylight Visualizer, either by 
modelling directly in the program, or by modelling in SketchUp and then 
import it. In this project the models have been developed in Sketchup, as it 
is a good modelling tool for a complex design.
==PAGE:18==
10
2. METHOD
When developing the model in SketchUp with the purpose to import the 
model to VDV, there are some important factors to take into account. At first 
it is important that all rooms are closed, with no small openings, as these 
can not be calculated. Furthermore all surfaces have to be defined by a 
material on both sides of the pane. This is important as these materials will 
be imported as well and all material properties have to be assigned to these, 
to be able to change the transmittances of the glass and the reflectances of 
the other surfaces. 
2.2.2 IESVE
“Integrated Environmental Solutions – Virtual Environment” (IESVE) is an 
international dynamic simulation software. The software consists of several 
integrated modules to simulate the indoor climate of a building, such as 
the daylight, temperatures, ventilation and the energy consumption of the 
building. The modules used in this report are: ModelIT, SunCast, ApacheSim, 
ApPro, RadianceIES and MacroFlo [IES, ApacheSim]. 
MODELIT
The modelling of the building can either be made by importing a model from 
e.g. SketchUp, or by modelling it directly in ModelIT. Import can result in 
minor mistakes, which can complicate the process [IES, ModelIT].
SUNCAST
The Suncast simulation has to be done prior to any thermal simulation 
or daylight simulation to make sure that the shade from the surrounding 
buildings are included [IES, Suncast].
Suncast is also used to perform an analysis of the solar irradiation on the 
facades. The Solar Energy Analysis displays the results in kWh/m2 and the 
cumulative % availability. The outputs are graphically displayed with colour 
indications on the facades [IES, Suncast].
APACHESIM
ApacheSim is the module calculating the thermal analysis based on 
mathematical modelling of the heat transfer processes in the building, 
related to the inputs from the other modules. Construction templates and 
thermal templates are assigned to the model in this module. The profiles to 
control the thermal consitions are defined in ApPro [IES, ApacheSim].
 
MACROFLO
Macroflo is the module used to analyse the air and thermal exchanges of the 
building. MacroFlo simulates the airflow through all openings in a building. 
The opening types consider all kinds of openings, from smaller cracks, holes, 
windows and doors. MacroFlo is therefore interesting, when considering the 
infiltration, natural ventilation and the hybrid ventilation in a building. The 
templates are assigned to specific constructions [IES, Macroflo].
RADIANCEIES
RadianceIES is based on Radiance, which is developed by the Lighting Systems 
Research Group at Lawrence Berkley Laboratory in California. Radiance 
is a software package, developed as a research tool at first, which can be 
==PAGE:19==
11
2. METHOD
used for calculating the daylight in a room with high accuracy. Radiance 
uses a 3-dimensional geometrical model, based on which it produces a 
photorealistic image showing the radiance values. Radiance is a sort of ray-
tracing algorithm, which traces the solar rays backward, which means that 
it is traced from the sensor point  to the scene in focus. Radiance has two 
main outputs, the “illuminance output”, from where Daylight Factor, lux and 
contours can be seen, and the “luminance output” from where photorealistic 
colour images and glare analysis can be made [IES, RadianceIES].    
VISTA
Vista is used to review all the results from the dynamic thermal simulations 
made in ApacheSim. Results for the entire building, a group of rooms or one 
room can be seen here. The results can be illustrated by graphs or in tables, 
selected for specific dates or a year [IES, Vista].
2.2.3 DAYSIM
DaySim is an educational programme, developed by the National Research 
Council of Canada Institute, and it is available for free download online. 
Daylight simulations can, as described earlier, either be static or dynamic, 
depending on the sky conditions and other parameters. DaySim can both 
manage the static and the dynamic simulations, but is mainly developed for 
the purpose of the dynamic simulations [Reinhart, 2010].
DaySim is a Radiance-based software, based on backward ray-tracing like 
explained for RadianceIES. DaySim analyses the annual daylight conditions 
inside and outside the buildings. The program can include solar shading 
defined by the users, and need of electric light with dimming profiles in the 
calculations. The program has mainly been developed with the purpose 
of integrating the daylight conditions and user behaviour with the need 
for artificial light. Furhtermore the effects of the energy consumption 
for artificial light has become a part of the program [Reinhart, 2010]. The 
simulation outputs are climate-based daylight metrics, among the outputs 
are the Daylight Autonomy (DA) and the Useful Daylight Illuminance (UDI) 
as well as the risk of glare and the energy use of the needed electrical light 
[Reinhart, 2015].
The Daylight Factor (DF) is also an output from DaySim, as a more simple 
analysis, based on a CIE overcast sky, compared to the DA and UDI, which are 
based on all sky conditions over a reference year. The needed weather file 
has to be loaded to use the local geographical reference year [MicroShade, 
2013].
The building can be modelled in 3D in eg. SketchUp and afterwards exported 
to DaySim to do the daylight simulations. Afterwards the results can be 
analysed by visualizing the results in SketchUp, by importing the results back 
to Sketchup from DaySim, using the plugin Su2ds [MicroShade, 2013].

3. LITERATURE STUDY
3.1 INTRODUCTION
This chapter has focus on the process in the literature studies. To get an 
overview of the literature study it is divided into two phases of approaching 
the knowledge related to glasshouses, which is illustrated in figure 3.1; 
where the literature study and interview study follows the previous chapter 
“2. Method”.

Figure 3.1: Interaction between the method, literature study, interviews and the thesis.
The first phase is the literature study, which contains the history of the 
glasshouse, a litterature study of perspectives of the glasshouse and a study 
of the general knowledge. These parameters are investigated to have a 
common base for understanding the development of the glasshouse and 
what led to how it is today and the related problems and qualities. The general 
knowledge study gives a common background for how glass influence the 
indoor climate and energy in general. 
The second phase of the literature studies is based on several interviews. 
The interviews are done by both architects and engineers separately to give 
a holistic indication of the problems and qualities in the glasshouses as it 
is today. The interviews also focus at the importance of the cooperation 
between the architects and the engineers to improve the architecture and 
the building performance. 
The main points of the qualities and problems related to the glasshouse are 
finally summed up in a sub-conclusion having highlighted the main aspects 
to further analysis in the techincal investigation. The interaction between the 
litterature study and the technincal investigations is illustrated in the following 
figure 3.1, where the entire process of the thesis is build up diagrammatic.

3. LITERATURE STUDY
3.2 HISTORY OF THE GLASSHOUSE
The use of glass in construction can be traced back centuries. Over time 
the use and thereby technical capability has increased. Several factors have 
influenced this, but they all take their starting point in the desired quality of 
glass; to let in light.
 
In churches, coloured or stained glass was used in the gothic architecture 
(1200-1600) and played an important role in giving the interior special 
lighting effects [Eekhout, 1990] [Weller et al., 2009].
The glasshouse originated in constructions, which were built to protect 
or grow plants. At first it was a construction, an orange house or a plant 
house, which could be assembled in winter and disassembled in summer. 
These had been used in Germany since the middle of the sixteenth century. 
In order to reduce the process of assembly and disassembly the orangery 
had 3 permanent walls and the south wall and roof were made movable. The 
permanent orangery appeared, when the plants rather than the orangery 
were made movable. This type was developed to its full potential between 
1700 and 1730 [Kohlmaier et al., 1991]. Making the orangery permanent 
also had the benefit of making the heating easier, because the joints could 
be sealed properly, which reduced leaks, and the heating source could be 
installed permanently [Kohlmaier et al., 1991].
The glasshouse originated from social purposes and evolved to a greenhouse 
or a winter garden. It was a way of capturing nature indoor, so the concept of 
the glasshouse was still connected to the basic principles of the plant houses 
[Kohlmaier et al., 1991]. The sizes of the plant houses were, however, not 
sufficient anymore for the growing concept of the winter gardens. They were 
not only to capture some nature but to capture a landscape and bacame a 
part of the lifestyle of the nobility [Kohlmaier et al., 1991]. The interest and 
expansion of the winter garden also have to be seen in the light of a growing 
interest for exotic plants, which started in the beginning of the 19th century, 
and was caused by the colonialism and the expansion of international trade 
[Kohlmaier et al., 1991]. 
At the start of the 19th century the winter gardens were combined with the 
living accommodations and by that became integrated in the daily life instead 
of being a place for festive events only. The nobility still held the privilege to 
have winter gardens, which was a sign of their position in society, because it 
was very expensive to build, maintain and to heat [Kohlmaier et al., 1991]. 
The middle class, however, also gained capital due to the industrialization 
and could compete with the nobility in an expensive lifestyle, so they started 
to build their own smaller plant houses, which spread out to the cities and 
became more and more common for every villa to have [Kohlmaier et al., 
1991]. 
The industrialization had an important role in the development and spreading 
of the glasshouse. The industrial period provided the technology and 
capability to build all glazed houses. The production of glass was improved 
making it possible to produce glass in bigger sizes, which led to a decrease in 
the price [Eekhout, 1990]. Iron structures were as well developed  and used 
==PAGE:24==
16
3. LITERATURE STUDY
in buildings in combination with glass (in the 19th century) which together 
opened for new possibilities in enclosing space. Out of this a new glass-and-
iron architecture arose, which had its peak, when the famous Crystal Palace 
was built in 1851 in London. The palace was special because it was a product 
of modular construction  and prefabrication, which was made possible by the 
industrialization [Eekhout, 1990]. The industrial growth reduced the nature 
in the cities and increased the need for green areas. Public winter gardens 
therefore became popular and were very positively received. Here women 
and men could gather, protected from unpleasant weather. The first large 
public winter garden was built between 1842 and 1846 in London [Kohlmaier 
et al., 1991]. 
With the construction of the Crystal Palace (Figure 3.2) and the modular 
construction an epidemic for similar glass buildings swept through Europe 
resulting in a large number of exhibition buildings, glass arcades, market 
coverings and winter gardens to be built. Besides the benefits developed on 
the constructive part in the industrial revolution, progress was also made in 
solving heating, ventilation and sun shading to control the environment in 
the glass buildings [Eekhout, 1990]. The Crystal Palace could be considered 
to be the starting point for modernism’s occupation with the ‘lightness of 
construction’ but it also awoke another architectural direction of ‘constructing 
with light’ [Richards, 2006].
The tendency to build glasshouses stopped when the First World War  broke 
out [Eekhout, 1990]. However, the alternative direction of ‘constructing with 
light’ started to evolve in a short period from 1914 to 1932. Among others 
the Glass Pavilion by Bruno Taut in Cologne, Germany, in 1914. Mies van der 
Rohe also made a design for an unbuilt project in Berlin in 1922, but it was 
in Pierre Chareau’s Maison de Verre in Paris in 1931 that a new architectural 
language, which had not been seen before, emerged [Richards, 2006]. The 
Glass Pavilion was considered the breakthrough for a new architectural way of 
thinking, where focus was on how design could add to the quality of life. The 
potential of the qualities of glass began to emerge with light, transparency, 
reflection, translucency and opacity which changed the perception of space 
in architecture. 
Mies van der Rohe was also convinced that it was time to find new ways with 
the use of glass. He designed skyscrapers in Berlin in 1921-1922 where he 
explored the idea of constructing the walls entirely of glass. He saw the glass 
as a skin, which would be slipped over the steel structure. This would have 
the effect that the glass would reflect and mirror the structure and by that be 
an integrated part of the structure, rather than hiding it. 
Frank Lloyd Wright was also working in the United States with the idea of 
open up space and eliminate the division between inside and outside. This 
was also done by Le Corbusier in Paris. Le Corbusier was the first to attempt 
making an integrated curtain wall system, but it failed. The technology 
wasn’t far enough in solving the environmental challenges, the curtain wall 
presented, and it took a another 50 years to control the impact of the solar 
gain by tinting, air-conditioning, integral multilayered facades and shading 
devices. 
Over the next 50 years the main development potential of glass was focused 
Figure 3.2: Crystal Palace, A cast-iron 
and plate-glass building erected in Hyde 
Park, London for the Great Exhibition of 
1851 [leasliecreasey].
==PAGE:25==
17
3. LITERATURE STUDY
on the curtain wall and the needed technology. Glass was a symbol of 
progress and became a material that symbolized the modern architecture. In 
this process the material properties were overlooked, and it was more seen 
as a cladding material. 
Onwards the crossing between ‘lightness in construction’ and ‘construction 
with light’ kept on being a dilemma for the glass architecture to really emerge. 
The focus was kept on the building envelope, and not the structure of space 
and awareness of how transparency can contribute to the architecture. 
It was in fact artists and not architects, who contributed to change the 
perception of glass and light. Artists started their experimentation in the 
mid 1960s, and experimented with light and dark, sunlight and shadow, 
time and space, sound and silence using different materials related to glass 
such as plexiglass, fibreglass etc. Other artists experimented with industrial 
materials such as steel, aluminium, fluorescent light and mirrors to express 
particular phenomena. They created illusions on water and projected and 
reflected light. It took decades, before the ‘light artists’ and evolving glass 
architects fully crossed boundaries, and it had an effect in ‘making light 
material’ [Richards, 2006].
The first building to explore the new transparency was the Willis Faber 
& Dumas offices in Ipswich (Figure 3.3), England in 1971-75, by Foster 
Associates (Norman Foster). The building has a curtain wall facade, which 
follows the curvy form of the building. The glass is butt-jointed (placed 
together side by side) with silicone joints to maintain visual continuity. The 
facade uses Pilkington single-glazed toughened solar tinted glass. This causes 
a shimmering effect, which results in external reflections, while the interior 
provides internal reflections. At night lighting levels within the building 
causes a reversed daily situation and brings the building to life reducing the 
material boundaries between spaces. 
During the 1980s progress was made in the glass industry, which included 
the evolution of hard and soft coatings on the inner surface of the glass 
envelope, double and triple skins, solar-control glasses, K-insulation glass, 
integral blinds and shading devices. The multi-layered facade could begin to 
accommodate integrated climatic control as thermal regulation and energy 
recovery. Imroving the function of separate layers with different functions 
contributing to the overall united facade expression, and this opened up for 
new possibilities in lightness and spaces [Richards, 2006].
This new progress affected the 1990s, which represents a decade with more 
experimentation, because the new progress allowed it. There was a wider 
availability of glass and materials, technology could resolve climatic issues, 
and there was an interest to experiment with transparency, space and light. 
This resulted in a decade, which represents experimentation with glass 
architecture and demonstration of glass technology [Richards, 2006].
Figure 3.3: Headquarters of Willis Faber 
& Dumas, by Foster Associates (Norman 
Foster), built from 1971-1975, Ipswick 
(England) [nfartchitecture].

3. LITERATURE STUDY
3.3 PERSPECTIVES OF THE GLASSHOUSE
As a part of the literature studies several articles are selected with some 
interesting views upon different qualities and problems related to the 
glasshouse. In this section an overview of some of these views will be 
described to give a general idea of the various perspectives on qualities and 
problems related to glasshouses. 
The article “Seduced by the view” by the U.S. Green Building Council [Urban 
Green, 2013] is based upon a field study of several glasshouses in New York 
City. The analysis indicates that an average of 59% of the window area was 
covered, by either blinds or shades, which decreased the possibility to look 
out. This includes both residential and office buildings. The investigation also 
indicated that more than 75% of the buildings had more than half of the 
windows covered by solar shading. The article concludes that the high use 
of blinds or shades mainly was due to the need of privacy in the residential 
buildings, and not because of the direct sunlight, and in the office buildings it 
was mainly because of the glare, overheating etc. [Urban Green, 2013]. Figure 
3.4 shows the problematic of the high use of curtains in an office building. In 
the article they also state that “.. the bigger the windows, the easier it is to 
rent or sell an apartment or office space” [Urban Green, 2013: p. 3], which 
indicates how big an influence the developer has to say in the the process of 
constructing and how big a factor economy is. The related problems to glass 
facades such as glare, noise, overheating and cold facades in winter times 
are according to this author often overlooked and the article states, “from 
an environmental perspective, glass buildings are a major step backwards” 
[Urban Green, 2013: p. 3]. The windows insulate not as efficiently as regular 
walls filled with insulation. When looking at the lifetime of the building, 
the glass facade will last for 50 years or longer, whereas the technical parts 
will be changed for more efficient solutions more often. In the long run the 
facade will therefore be the most inefficient part of the building, with the 
biggest energy consumption, so why use glass facades at all? It is not only the 
tenants in the glass buildings paying the costs of higher energy consumption, 
but the entire society, which has to power the buildings and thereby increase 
the related pollution in the cities and globally [Urban Green, 2013].
There are many interesting statements in the article like; why glasshouses so 
often are built, when there are so many related problems, it seems to be for 
the economical and architectural benefits. When looking at the userbehaviour 
shading of different kind is used most of the time and thereby covering the 
view, which for residential buildings can be seen as a reaction for claiming 
the privacy back due to the increased transparency. For office buildings, the 
shading is used to limit glare, overheating and big light contrasts, but again 
the blinds and shades are covering the view. The view can be considered 
as the most important advantage of the glasshouse, and therefore it is a 
big consern when it is blocked. Overall the article certainly points out some 
important issues to consider in relation to view, shading and user behaviour 
in a glasshouse. 
This leads to the very subjective approach from the article, “Can an All-Glass 
Office Building Really Be Considered Green?”, by Lloyd Alter von Treehugger 
Figure 3.4: Glass facade of office build­
ing, showing internal curtains [photog­
raphersdirect].
==PAGE:27==
19
3. LITERATURE STUDY
[Alter, 2014], where the main question is, why so many glass buildings 
are being built, when so many problems are related to this typology. One 
concern regards the daylight in the buildings and the thought of having glass 
from floor to ceiling, as the light under table-height is not defined as daylight 
anyway, and as the view from the lower part of the window is only useful for 
the people nearby the facade, and not the people further back in the room. 
Another concern mentioned regarding the big glass facades is from the 
company’s point of view. Companies are often attracted to the transparency 
of the glass facades to show an open, welcoming and thereby attractive 
company, but often it is forgotten that glass is not always transparent. 
Glass can have the opposite effect of mirroring its surroundings due to the 
orientation and effect of light, thereby the transparancy is eliminated, and so 
is the idea of a transparent company. The overall opinion in this article is that 
curtain walls and glass facades are only built for increased investment for the 
developer and promotion of the companies, and not for the architectural 
value. The if there are problems related to the glass facades conserning 
indoor climate and high energy consumption when in use, it is often the 
tenants, who have to pay, and not the developer. The energy problems are 
caused by an increased energy consumption for glass buildings compared to 
other building typologies [Alter, 2014].
Alsoin this article it is the quality of daylight, transparency of the facade and 
the related economics, which are mentioned as important factors related to 
using glass facades. 
The article “Useful daylight illuminances: A replacement for daylight factors”, 
by Nabil et al. [Nabil et al., 2006], mainly focus on the daylight, and how the 
daylight is considered according to the building regulations. Many building 
regulations require simulations to document the daylight factor (DF) in the 
rooms, which are differentiated for resident and office buildings respectively. 
The DF is, however, as they underline in the article, not necessarily an indicator 
of the quality of the light in the room. The article suggest to be more aware 
of the useful daylight illuminance (UDI) instead of the DF, as the UDI is more 
detailed with time-varying sky and sun conditions, instead of the DF, where 
only an overcast sky is used. The UDI include the risk of occupant discomfort 
and unwanted solar gain, which are associated with high levels of daylight as 
well as the useful levels of daylight illuminance [Nabil et al., 2006].
Previous studies by the Danish SBi, SBi-318, “Windows and daylight - a field 
investigation in office buildings” [SBi 318, 1999], also indicates the problems 
between the useful daylight and the requirements. The report had the goal 
to point out, that the building requirements related to daylight and windows, 
should be made considering the size, shape and placement of the windows 
in office buildings to ensure a good and satisfying visual and thermal indoor 
climate. The studies indicate that the biggest inconveniences due to the 
windows are the risk of glare caused by the direct light and high differences 
in the luminance. Furthermore slim vertical windows were considered as 
an nuicance for the employees, who would prefer wider windows to have 
a better view out. The same studies showed that the main part of the 
employees valued the view out, the cycle of the weather, and to ventilate 
the room manually higher than the daylight conditions in the room [SBi 318, 
1999].
==PAGE:28==
20
3. LITERATURE STUDY
Here is a study, which focuses on office buildings and the working 
environment for the employees, but with an output, which might be different 
than expected. This is very relevant to consider in the process of designing a 
building, where glass has a big impact. It is even more important to be aware 
of that it is not the quantity of glass but the quality and placement of glass, 
which ought to have the main focus.
When discussing view out and the quality of daylight it is also important to 
consider the solar shading, which will most likely be needed caused by both 
visual and thermal discomfort. In the article “Indoor thermal environmental 
conditions near glazed facades with shading devices - Part 1: Experiments and 
building thermal model”, by Bessoudo et al., investigations of the thermal 
effect near glass facades, when adding solar shading have been made. The 
investigations show the relation between the addition of shading systems 
and an improved operative temperature and radiant asymmetry during cold 
sunny days. These relations also depend on the properties of the windows as 
well as the tilt angle of the window. Even for cloudy days the solar shading 
had an effect, and the heat loss was decreased a bit. The interior surface 
of glass can be very warm during warm sunny days and the opposite very 
cold during cold days. As the article states, the primary goal of a building 
is to provide comfort, shelter and space for the people working or living in 
the building, and the energy consumption should be seen as a secondary 
goal. Therefore they claim that a building can be described as a success, 
if a comfortable indoor climate is achieved without increasing the energy 
consumption of the building [Bessoudo et al., 2010]. 
The investigation proves that external solar shading has some good qualities 
regarding the thermal indoor climate and not only the negative relations like 
closing off the view and limiting the daylight. 
Generally the above-mentioned side effects express a common understanding 
of the related problems and qualities when designing glass facades. They all 
agree that a glass facade has to be designed with consideration to limit the 
following related problems. It is an open question, why so many buildings 
with glass facades are built, when they are consuming more energy than 
other types of facades, and have a lot of related problems, which in many 
cases are a disadvantage and takes the focus from the architectural qualities.

3. LITERATURE STUDY
3.4 GENERAL KNOWLEDGE
This section focuses on the basic features of glass and solar radiation to get 
an overview of the aspects which affects the daylight, indoor climate and 
energy performance of a building. The notions are selected to give a brief 
overview of glass in the building industry today and the related material 
parameters conserning energy and indoor climate. This section forms the 
basis of the general knowledge used for the technical investigations.
3.4.1 GLASS IN BUILDINGS
During the past years the use of glass in  buildings has been increased rather 
rapidly, but the knowledge and the quality of the glass have had difficulties to 
develop accordingly. Therefore the market has a great amount of specialised 
glazings developed for specific projects, which later have been put on the 
market. Many scientists are working on analyzing and developing the glass 
to fulfill the needed requirements, and secure that the glazing on the market 
have qualities, which meet the requirements [SBI-192, 1999].
Glass is a unique material and as it is said “Glass is like a diva among building 
materials” [Weller et al., 2009]. The reason for this statement is the unique 
material properties for glass. Glass is a very sensitive and brittle material, 
which reacts immediately at improper use, as its elastic properties are subject 
to regular temperature conditions. The brittle material properties and high 
risk of breaking without warning is due to the high content of silicate, which 
limits the elastic behaviour of the material, but this is at the same time the 
reason for the hardness and the strength of the glass. Plastic behaviour occurs 
when the glass is heated up and floats [SBi 192, 1999]. Minor scratches and 
mistakes in the surface are, however, enough to increase the risk of breaks, 
since it limits the strength of the glass considerably [Weller et al., 2009: 12].
Another unique quality of glass in the building industry is the transparency, 
which is due to the atomic structure. Glass is in scientific sense a frozen liquid, 
which hardens without crystallisation, and thereby the material becomes 
transparent. The main part of glass in the building industry is soda-lime 
silicate glass. The atomic structure of glass has a lack of boundary surfaces, 
which prevents reflection of light in the range of visible light and long wave 
UV-light, and instead it lets the light through the pane, whereas the short-
waved UV-B and UV-C light is absorbed in the pane [Weller et al., 2009].
FLOAT GLASS
The float glass method was invented in 1952-59 by Pilkington company from 
the UK. This method made the production of high optical quality sheet glass 
easier, and it became a commonly used production method [Weller et al., 
2009]. The method is performed by continuously casting and floatation of 
the liquid on a metal bath, which gives the advantage of a flat, parallel and 
polished surface already from production, and therefore further handling 
after this process is limited [SBI 192, 1999] [DS EN 572-1, 2012]. The benefit 
of float glass is that it is transparent, has a smooth surface and no optical 
distortions, which makes it optimal to look through [Pilkington, 2014].
Figure 3.5: Float glass at the bottom 
and low-iron glass at the top [Byg­
gnadsglas].  
==PAGE:30==
22
3. LITERATURE STUDY
LOW-IRON GLASS
Regular float glass has a greenish colour, which is increased depending on 
the thickness. It is the content of iron oxide that naturally gives the colour 
[Pilkington, 2014]. The colour is mostly visible along the edges of the glass. 
To make the glass more transparent and without colour the content of iron is 
reduced. This type of glass is known as low-iron and extra-clear glass [Weller 
et al., 2009]. 
TOUGHENED GLASS
Some of the disadvantages for glass as a building material are the brittle 
fracture behaviour, and its low tensile strength, which causes risk for 
damage. To compensate for these disadvantages, the glass can be thermally 
toughened, which means that the glass is prestressed in the factory to 
overcome higher tensile stresses without any damages. The prestress in the 
glass gives the glass a higher tensile bending strength, which is better than 
float glass [Weller et al., 2009]. Due to the prestress, toughened glass has a 
high content of energy stored in the glass, which upon fracture will result in 
the glass to shatter into a lot of small pieces. This type of glass minimises the 
risk for being injuried due to glass splinter when breaking, and is therefore 
very commonly used for rooflights in buildings [Pilkington, 2014].
3.4.2 SOLAR RADIATION
RADIATION
The solar spectrum constitutes a small part of the spectrum of electromagnetic 
waves. Solar radiation is overall divided into three kinds of radiation; the 
ultraviolet radiation (280-380 nm), the visible light (380-780 nm) and the 
infrared radiation (780-2500 nm) [AGC]. The ultraviolet radiation, UV-
radiation, is electromagnetic radiation with a high energy intensity and 
thereby also high temperature. The visible light is seen in the colour spectrum 
from violet for the low values to red for the high values, with indigo, green, 
yellow and orange in between.
Heat radiation constitutes a bigger spectrum of the electromagnetic waves. 
It can overall be divided in two categories; heat radiation from the solar 
spectrum constituted by the previous three described kinds of radiation; 
ultraviolet radiation, light and short infrared waves. The other category is 
heat emitted from objects in the form of long infrared waves (above 2500nm) 
[AGC]. Whenever a temperature difference occurs, heat will be transferred 
from the cold point to the warmer point. Heat is transferred in three ways: 
[AGC] [Rode et al., 2010]: 
Conduction: 	 Heat is transferred from molecule to molecule when they 
	
	
collide with each other. 
Convection: 	 Differences in temperature causes difference in density and 	
	
	
thereby movement of the heat through a liquid or a gas.
Radiation: 	
The heat is transferred in the form of electromagnetic 
	
	
radiation. 
EMISSIVITY
The emissivity factor, ε, is the ability of a body to emit energy in the form of 
Figure 3.6: Toughened glass, which 
shatters into a lot of small pieces when 
breaking [Elite Art Glass]. 
Figure 3.7: Illustration of the solar spec­
trum [Batterixlcom, 2015].
==PAGE:31==
23
3. LITERATURE STUDY
radiation. It is related to the surface characteristic of the body. Low emissivity 
equals a low heat transfer. Glass is impermeable to long-wave radiation, 
but will absorb the longwave infrared radiation, which then easily can be 
re-emitted as heat, which gives glass a high emissivity [Weller et al., 2009]. 
Therefore a glass will emit a certain percentage of energy, for a glass with no 
coating, ε = 0.8, this will be around 80 %, where a coated glass with ε = 0.05 
only will emit 5%. This difference in the emissivity means that the energy 
will stay in the glass and heat it up. Energy glass is generally defined with an 
emissivity ε ≤ 0.20, which is quite low [Pilkington, 2014].
G-VALUE
The solar energy transmittance through the glass is defined as the g-value. 
This value describes the ability of glass to transmit the radiation as heat to the 
interior. The value is a percentage of the radiation hitting the glass outside, 
which is transmitted through the glass. The transmittance can be directly and 
as the secondary heat, which is absorbed by the material at first, and then re-
emitted into the interior [Weller et al., 2009]. This will be further described 
later in the report. The greater the value is, the more heat is transmitted to 
the room [Pilkington, 2014]. This is desirable in the wintertime to get free 
heat and thereby reduce the need for heating, but can be more critical in the 
summertime, if the internal gains are high.
When describing the qualities for glass it is important to differentiate 
between the visible radiation, the sunlight, the heat radiation and the energy 
transmittance passing through the surface [SBi 219, 2008].
U-VALUE
The U-value (thermal transmittance) describes the heat loss through a 
building component and material from the internal surface to the external 
surface through conduction, convection and radiation. The U-value is defined 
as the reciprocal value of the total resistances (Rtot) including the surface 
resistances as seen in the following equation: 
	
U=1/Rtot [W/(m2K)]
For a good insulated material, the heat transmission is low, which is defined 
through a low U-value. When referring to glass the low heat transmission will 
result in a high surface temperature on the inside of the glass. In winter time 
this will reduce the temperature drop and draft from the windows, and it will 
be the opposite situation during summertime with lower surface temperature 
on the inside of the glass [Pilkington, 2014]. If the U-value is very low, even 
lower than 1.1, there will be a high risk of condensation on the external side 
of the glass. This will mainly be in the spring and autumn periods, where the 
condensation will happen, when the temperature increases in the morning 
after a cold and clear night, and thereby the humidity will increase as well 
[SBi 219, 2008].
MULTI-PANE AND COATED GLASS
Nowadays the building industry has a big focus on minimising the energy 
consumption and thereby optimizing the thermal properties for the building 
envelope. To ensure good thermal properties for the glass as well as the 
other facade components in the construction, multi-pane glass is used. Multi-
==PAGE:32==
24
3. LITERATURE STUDY
pane glass consists of at least two panes of glass, which are joined along 
their perimeters by a shear-resistant connection. The joined edges create a 
hermetically sealed cavity filled with air or gas, which improves the thermal 
insulation compared to a single pane [Weller et al., 2009]. 
Glass does not transmit long wave infrared radiation, which therefore will be 
absorbed by the glass, and then be emitted to the room as heat. Normally the 
heat transfer goes from cold to warm, and therefore the low emission (Low E) 
coating is designed to increase reflection of the heat absorbed by the glazing 
to the inside and not the outside, so the heat is retained inside and enhances 
the thermal comfort during cold wintertimes [AGC]. During summer, the 
process is reverse, and the Low E coating reflects the longwave radiation 
from the sun outwards to limit the heat gain from the sun [Pilkington, 2014].
Low E coating consists of a very thin film of a metal or a metal oxide, which is 
either added during or after production. The coating made during production 
is very durable and has a hard and strong surface, which is not vulnerable 
to scratches, whereas the coating made after production is less resistant 
to environmental impacts and therefore placed on the inside of the multi-
pane glass [Weller et al., 2009]. An important feature of the coating is the 
possibility to decrease the amount of infrared and ultraviolet radiation, which 
passes through the glass, but at the same time lets the visible light through. 
The ultraviolet light causes fading of interior materials. The infrared light is 
often referred to as the short wave from the sun, and heat radiation from 
warm surfaces is referred to as longwave infrared [Glass Education Center].
Coatings can be used as solar shading as well, but here the coated pane is 
the outer pane of the multi-pane glass. Hereby the solar energy is reduced, 
which means the g-value is rather low. This also results in a reduced light 
transmittance. The solar shading can be adjusted to the given building and 
its needs, by using different coatings. The coatings can either increase or 
decrease the reflectance of the facade, or give the glass a tint of a colour, 
most commonly in the grey, green and blue scale. If using an antireflection, 
coating it will seem as if it has a greater transparency [Weller et al., 2009].
GLASS AND THERMAL COMFORT
Glazing has a higher thermal transmittance compared to ‘regular’ external 
walls, where insulation lowers the thermal transmittance. A high thermal 
transmittance equals more heat gain and loss, which evidently affects the air 
temperature and the indoor environment. 
All surfaces emit long wave radiation, including people through the skin, 
so a radiant heat exchange is occurring between all the surfaces. Glass is a 
material with good properties for absorbing and transmitting solar radiation. 
When absorbing the radiation the inside surface temperature is affected, 
because the temperature is increasing [Stampe, 2001]. This affects the long 
wave radiation exchange between the surfaces and the occupant. For the 
heat balance of the occupant the relation between radiation emitted to the 
surroundings and the energy received from the surroundings are important 
for the comfort. In summertimes the inside surface temperature of windows 
can increase significantly and lead to discomfort due to long wave heat 
exchange between the surface and the occupant leading to a heat gain and 
Figure 3.8: Illustration of the concept for 
Low E coating.
==PAGE:33==
25
3. LITERATURE STUDY
a warm discomfort. In wintertimes the surface temperature can be quite 
low also compared to the other surfaces in the indoor environment and the 
occupant [Hoffmann et al., 2012]. In cold wintertimes the windows can be 
really cold, and the radiant heat exchange between the occupant and the 
surface will lead to a cold discomfort due to heat loss for the occupant. The 
heat exchange and discomfort is dependent of the geometry of the interior 
and placement of the occupant in relation to this. In summertimes the 
discomfort in terms of a warm feeling is reinforced by the transmitted solar 
radiation or short wave radiation. The short wave radiation is absorbed by 
the outer surface, clothes or skin and leads to a temperature increase. In 
cold winter times the effect of the short wave radiation can have a different 
influence, where it might feel pleasant if you’re feeling cold and get warmer 
by the absorbing the radiation. The transmitted solar radiation can be in two 
forms: direct or diffuse. The direct solar radiation has higher intensity and 
depending of the amount of exposure, can raise the skin temperature locally, 
if only few body parts are exposed [Huizenga et al., 2006] [Poirazis, 2008] 
[Hoffmann et al., 2012]. 
In winter, the inside surface temperature is often several degrees below the 
room air temperature. This can cause a cold down draft, because the air 
temperature, moving along the cold surface, will be cooled down and seek 
down towards the floor evidently leading to cold feet [Hoffmann et al., 2012]. 
It can be concluded that glazed surfaces affect the thermal comfort in overall 
3 ways: [Hoffmann et al., 2012]
-	
Long wave radiation from the cold or warm interior glass surface to 	
	
the occupant.
- 	
Solar radiation transmitted directly through the window producing a 	
	
heat load on the occupant. 
- 	
Increased convection by the window, caused by the difference 	
	
between the glass surface temperature and the ambient air 
	
temperature.
MEAN RADIANT TEMPERATURE
As described all bodies exchange thermal radiation with their surroundings. 
The mean radiant temperature describes the average temperature of the 
surrounding sufaces, which radiates heat. 
Low mean radiant temperatures affect the energy consumption because they 
increase the need for a higher air temperature. This is because of the radiant 
heat exchange occurring between all surfaces, including the occupants. A 
low mean radiant temperature of the surroundings causes the occupants to 
loose heat, and therefore a higher air temperature is needed to compensate 
[Madsen, 1998]. 
RADIANT TEMPERATURE ASYMMETRY 
A very specific measure of the effect on thermal comfort of the longwave 
radiation is the radiant asymmetry. Here the effect of surfaces with 
temperatures different than the air temperature are evaluated. 
The radiant asymmetry is defined as the difference between the plane 
radiant temoerature ∆tpr of two opposite sides of a plane through the persons 
==PAGE:34==
26
3. LITERATURE STUDY
center of gravity. That can be either cold or warm thermal radiation from the 
horizontal surfaces or the vertical surfaces. It is seen that occupants are less 
tolerant towards cold thermal radiation from a vertical surface (must not 
exceed 10 0C) and less tolerant towards warm thermal radiation from the 
ceiling (must not exceed 5 0C) [Madsen, 1998] [DS 474] [DS 469].
To calculate the radiant temperature asymmetry the exchange of radiation 
between two surfaces can be calculated, taking into account the emissivity 
of the surfaces and the angle factor. The angle factor describes the relation of 
the quantity of energy radiated from one surface 1 to another surface 2, and 
the total quantity radiated towards a hemisphere over surface 1. Therefore 
the angle factor takes into account the internal placement of the surfaces in 
relation to each other and their radiation. The following formula desribes the 
relation [Rode et al., 2010]:
Φ1-2 = F1-2 · A1 · ε1 · ε2 · σs (T1
4 - T2
4)
3.4.3 DAYLIGHT
ANGLE OF INCIDENCE
The angle of incidence is the angle between the light ray on the surface of 
the glass and a line perpendicular to the glass. The angle will differ depending 
on the time of the day, the time of the year and the geographical location. 
The angle of incidence of the sun decides the amount of light transmitted 
through the glass, and therefore there is a difference depending on the time 
of the year. This means that the amount of sunlight is greater during winter 
than summer at a southern oriented glass as the angle of incidence is big, 
whereas it is smaller in summer at eastern and western facades as the angle 
of incidence is big and the sun is low on the sky as seen in figure 3.10 [SBI 
192, 1999].
TRANSMISSION, ABSORPTION AND REFLECTION
Regular glass allows solar radiation in the area between 300 to 3000 nm to 
pass through. When the light from the sun reaches the glass it is not just 
penetrated directly through. The light will be divided into three; some 
light will be reflected (R), some absorbed (A) in the glass, and some will be 
transmitted (LT) through the glass. The total radiation hitting the glass is 
always 100% of the sum of the three parameters,  as seen in the following 
equation [SBi 219, 2008]: 
	
I = LT + R + A 
The specific division of these three parameters depends on the type and 
thickness of the glass, the type of gas in the cavity and the type and placement 
of the coating. Besides these physical parameters of the glass, the angle 
of incidence of the sun on the surface is very important, as this angle also 
defines how much light will be reflected and transmitted through the glass, 
which again makes the work with glass very complex and dynamic [Weller 
et al., 2009]. A part of the radiation will be reflected for every transmission 
from glass to gas etc., and therefore several reflections between the panes 
can occur depending of the amount of panes and surface treatments [SBi 
219, 2008]. The transmitted light is defined as LT, which is the capability 
Angle of 
Incidence
Figure 3.10: Angle of incidence: the an­
gle between the light ray on the surface 
and a line perpendicular to the surface.
Figure 3.9: Radiant asymmetry from a 
cold window [Madsen, 1998: Fig. 6].
==PAGE:35==
27
3. LITERATURE STUDY
of the glass to transmit the visible sunlight, and therefore the higher light 
transmittance the more sunlight is let through the glass [Pilkington, 2014].  
SURFACE REFLECTANCES
Surface reflectances are equally important as daylight in a room, since it 
softens the direct light and creates a better visual comfort in the room. As the 
direct light enters the room, it will be reflected at all surfaces and led further 
into the room, and therefore most of the brightness in the back of a room is 
mainly consisting of reflected light. In general surfaces with high reflectances 
are often used for ceilings, while surfaces with the low reflectances often 
are used for flooring [SBi 219, 2008]. As general rule of thumb the Danish 
Building Regulation, SBi 230, recommends a value of 0.7 for the ceiling, 0.1 
for the floor and 0.4 for the internal walls [SBi 230, 2014: Chap 6.5.2 sect. 
1]. These recommendations have to be revalued due to the furnishing of the 
specific room, since the furnishing has a big influence on the reflectances. 
ILLUMINANCE AND DAYLIGHT FACTOR
Illuminance defines the amount of light at a horizontal plane at a specific 
sensor point indoor compared to a horizontal plane at a specific sensor point 
outdoor. The illuminance is measured in lux, which is lumen per squaremeter 
(lm/m2). The illuminance level consists of several contributions of the 
transmitted light through the glass, which is defined by the direct radiation 
from the sky, the diffuse reflected radiation from the exterior surfaces and 
the diffuse reflected radiation from internal surfaces [SBi 219, 2008].
The illuminance level in a room is highest close to the window and decreases 
rapidly, when the distance to the window increases. When evaluating the 
illuminance level, the lux-level will be measured at a height above floor level 
at 850 mm, which corresponds to the surface of a desktop. The illuminance 
in a room will vary depending on the illuminance outdoor and therefore big 
variations will occur in the room. Due to this, it is useful to look at the daylight 
factor (DF), which gives a better indication of the daylight in the room [SBi 
219, 2008]. The daylight factor is a percentage, defined by the ratio between 
the interior illuminance at a sensor point and the exterior illuminance at an 
overcast sky, as seen in the following equation: 
	
DF = Ein/Eext·100 
where Ein is the internal luminance level (lux) and Eext is the external luminance 
level (lux) [Fontoynont et al., 2004].
The daylight factor as described here, is based on the sky conditions of a CIE 
overcast sky,  a definition of an equal overcast sky, which is mixed by an overcast 
sky, partly clouded sky and a clear sky without sun. CIE is the shortening of the 
Commission Internationale de l’Eclairage. The recommended daylight factor 
at a workstation is 2% and can be reached by having an illuminance level at 
10,000 lux outdoor, which is a general definition of a CIE overcast sky, and 
200 lux indoor at the horizontal sensor point, as seen in figure 3.11. The DF is 
in general calculated in a grid with a distance from the wall and a grid at 0,5 
m [Fontoynont et al., 2004] [SBi 219, 2008]. The DF has some limitations as 
the CIE overcast sky often underestimates the luminances near the horizon, 
Figure 3.11: The illuminance level out­
doors and the preferred lux-level at a 
workplace [SBi 2013:20: Fig. 2].
==PAGE:36==
28
3. LITERATURE STUDY
which has the consequence of under and overlit spaces [Reinhart, 2010].
LUMINANCE AND GLARE
Luminance defines the amount of light radiated from a surface towards 
the eye and is measured in candela per m2 (cd/m2). When direct sunlight is 
reflected from a surface there is a high risk of glare due to high luminances. 
This is a limitation of the direct sunlight and is one of the main reasons for the 
need of shading devices. The big differences of the intensity of the direct and 
diffuse light can be decreased by reflecting some of the direct light further 
into the room and thereby decrease the big luminance differences in the 
room [SBi 219, 2008]. 
Glare is one of the main reasons for visual discomfort and can either be 
caused by high luminances, high luminance differences within the visual 
area, or by high light sources close to the eye. Two types of glare are mainly 
used: 
Vision limited glare 	 occurs when there is a big difference in luminance of 	
	
	
	
the focus of the eye and the surroundings.
Uncomfortable glare 	 occurs when a small area in the edge of the 	area of 
	
	
	
vision has very high luminance compared to the area 
	
	
	
of vision. 
In general the human eye has the capacity of a broad interval of luminances, 
but the degree of glare is often defined by how well the eye adapts to the 
luminance difference [SBi 219, 2008].
The preferred luminance level at a work plane is 45-105 cd/m2. Screens often 
have a luminance at 50-100 cd/m2 and a wall has a preferred luminance level 
at 30-60 cd/m2. To evaluate the contrasts in the field of view, the preferred 
ratios of the task (Lt), working plane (Lwp) and the wall illuminance (Lw) are 
defined as Lt:Lwp:Lw = 10:4:3 [van Ooyen et al., 1987], where Lw is mainly 
considered for the parapets in the atrium since a glass building. 
The following luminances are the maximum recommended values to avoid 
discomfort glare, when doing tasks involving a computer screen with an 
average luminance at 85 cd/m2 [Ruck, 2000]. 
Max. 300 cd/m2	
for surfaces in the field of view within the immediate 
	
	
	
background.
Max. 850 cd/m2 	
for surfaces in the field of view within the general 
	
	
	
background. 
Max. 850 cd/m2 	
for any surfaces at 0.6 x 0.6 m within the field of view.
UDI AND DA
The overall task of the daylight simulation is to predict, with a high certainty, 
illuminance and luminance level at a specific point, considering time, 
3d-modelling and dynamic sky conditions [Reinhart, 2010]. In many ways 
the average daylight factor is a limited definition of the daylight conditions 
in a room, as it only considers the simple conditions of a CIE overcast 
sky. If considering the variation of daylight in a room the Useful Daylight 
Illuminance (UDI) has become a more used definition. The UDI was proposed 
==PAGE:37==
29
3. LITERATURE STUDY
by Mardaljevic and Nabil in 2005 as a dynamic simulation tool [Reinhart, 
2010]. The UDI can, as well as the DF, describe the level of sufficient daylight 
at a workstation during the year. The difference from the DF to the UDI is 
that the UDI is based on a climatic-based analysis over a year, and thereby 
time-varying sky and sun conditions, which predicts the hourly levels of the 
absolute daylight illuminance instead of only looking at the CIE overcast sky. 
The UDI includes the risk of occupant discomfort and unwanted solar gain, 
which are associated with high levels of daylight as well as the useful levels 
of daylight illuminance. The occurrence of daylight illuminances has been 
divided into three groups in the UDI, as follows [Nabil et al., 2006].
	
< 100 lux 	
below the level of useful light
	
100-2000 lux 	 within the range of useful light
	
> 2000 lux 	
above the level of useful light
 
The Daylight Autonomy (DA) is the amount of time, where the daylight is at 
the preferred level, and there is no need of artificial lighting, which means 
that there will be either 0% or 100% artificial light. The preferred level of DA 
is between 50-70%, were below 50% is too low and above 70% is too high 
[Reinhart, 2012]. The DA is just as the UDI based on a climatic-based analysis 
over a year [Reinhart, 2010]. Continuous Daylight Autonomy, (DAcont) is the 
DA where dimming is added to the light, which will result in some energy 
savings for the artificial light, since the light will be matched with the daylight 
in the room and partly covering of daylight will count as well instead of only 
full hours like for DA. The Maximum Daylight Autonomy (DAmax) indicates the 
risk of too high illuminance levels, which will result in glare. In DaySim the 
DAmax is defined as 10 times the desired minimum illuminance level [Reinhart, 
2012].
The UDI and DA can both be calculated by using different simulation tools, 
which was described in the chapter “2.3 Method”. 
ARTIFICIAL LIGHT
The design of artificial light has become an important design task, together 
with the interior design and the daylight design. 
The design of the artificial light has become a part of the interior design since 
the second world war, due to the increased use of electricity for light. This 
led to an increased interest in artificial light, and research in old materials 
along with new materials were made to improve the quality of light. Well 
designed interiors are often aligned with an efficient and functional lighting 
and is becoming more integrated within the architecture. The light is now 
a big part of the architectural value, if it is worked with by sensitiveness 
and adjusted to the purpose, and not only by technical appearance. As for 
daylight the artificial light has to be considered with care, to avoid glare, big 
disturbing contrasts, odd shadows or false colours in the light. Designing the 
artificial light is an art in itself, dependent on a close cooperation between 
architects and electrical engineers. Often it is seen that an approximation 
of daylight in colour of the artificial light is preferable as well as some 
differences in intensity and shadows, to achieve as natural and living interior 
light environment as possible [Read et al., 1933] [SBi 230, 2014].
==PAGE:38==
30
3. LITERATURE STUDY
3.5 INTERVIEWS
INTRODUCTION
This section contains research performed in collaboration with the business 
world. Interviews with architects and engineers are performed to get a 
holistic and more practical aspect on the perspectives of glasshouses. The 
interviewed architects and engineers are from different companies and the 
selection is based on their experience and field of work. They have through 
their work a lot of experience and knowledge from a more realistic and 
practical perspective. They have participated in projects from the beginning 
till the end, so they know the dilemmas and challenges in the process and 
can describe these from an architectural and an engineering point of view. 
There is generally various opinions about glasshouses, and an often used 
statement is that engineers are usually very negative about glasshouses, while 
the architects are very positive. This is put to a test, when each profesion 
is questioned about the qualities and problematic issues when building in 
glass. Getting an understanding about the perspective from each profession 
sets a foundation for a mutual understanding, and which will contribute to 
achieving more optimised solutions. Furthermore specific relevant issues 
can be pointed out for further investigations.
The cooperation between architects and engineers is questioned to hear 
how the cooperation is experienced, and what could be done to improve 
the cooperation in the future. 3-4 interviews have been performed of 
respectively the architects and engineers to get different perspectives and 
views on the subjects. 
The interviews are performed as a mix of questions and conversation. 
This means that a few general questions have been on the agenda for all 
interviews, and during the interviews more specific questions have occurred, 
based on the course of the interview and the opinions and exoeriences of 
the interviewed person. The general questions are listed below.  
	
- Why is glass used so much in buildings?
	
- What are the qualities of glasshouses?
	
- What are the problematic aspects of glasshouses?
	
- How is the cooperation between architects and engineers?
	
- What could be done to improve the cooperation?
After the interviews, they have been transcribed and processed in relation to 
the main subjects of: 
Pros: 	 Architectural value, daylight, thermal indoor climate, energy, 
	
cooperation, statics.
Cons: 	 Daylight, thermal indoor climate, energy, cooperation, statics.
General: Own opinion, technical description. 
Each subject has been provided with a colour, so relevant information in the 
interviews have been assigned a colour and main points have been drawn out 
and rewritten if necessary to the most important essence. The transcribed 
==PAGE:39==
31
3. LITERATURE STUDY
and processed interviews can be seen in “Appendix A1-A7”.
In this chapter an analysis of the interviews will be made seperately for the 
architects and the engineers. The analysis will be divided into the subjects 
as previous described and further summed up to give an onverview of the 
outputs separately for the architects and the engineers. For both analysis 
some key points are gathered as important aspects for further investigation 
in the technical investigations. Finally the two analysis will be summed up to 
make a common sub-conclusion pointing out differences and similarities of 
the key aspects between architects and engineers.
