3. LITERATURE STUDY
3.5.1 INTERVIEWS WITH ARCHITECTS
The following section and exposition are made based on the interviews 
of the architects. Key points in relation to the questions are divided in the 
subjects of architectural quality, atriums, the facade, daylight, thermal indoor 
environment, energy and cooperation and the subjects will be described. 
Below the participants in the interviews are listed: 
	
Lundgaard & Tranberg Architects:
	
Henrik Schmidt, Architect M.A.A., partner.
	
Robert Janson, Architect M.A.A.
	
Mikkelsen Architects:
	
Stig Mikkelsen, Architect M.A.A.
ACHITECTURAL QUALITY
During the interviews the interviewees directly and indirectly expressed the 
architectural qualities of glasshouses. A general point of view is relating to 
the transparency, which bring two desired qualities; the potential of having a 
lot of daylight and having visual connections.
Visual connections are important both in regards to external and internal 
views. In relation to having the visual connection to the outside, Henrik 
Schmidt states: “The big benefits of glasshouses are, that you can make the 
most out of a location” [Schmidt, 2015: 11.34-13.42]. Robert supports this 
aspect by describing that having a nice view add one more dimension to the 
architecture, which make glass important [Janson, 2015]. It is, however, not 
only the aspect of connection to the outside visually, but also the feeling of 
connection to the outside, because it would give a feeling of disconnection 
to the outside world, if it was not possible to sense the weather and the 
time of day [Schmidt, 2015]. Furthermore the connection to the urban life 
in the world outside contributes with an energy to the indoor environment 
[Mikkelsen, 2015]. Glass enables an openness in the building, which affects 
the experience of the indoor environment, because it provides internal views. 
It is possible to look through the building, which also creates interesting 
spatial experiences of the interior, intensified by the quality of glass to reflect 
the surroundings [Schmidt, 2015] [Mikkelsen, 2015]. Furthermore the fact 
that you can see the working life in the house will also participate to this 
feeling or energy of ‘the pulse’ in the building [Schmidt, 2015].
 
ATRIUMS
Places which often have an excessive amount of glazing are atrium areas etc. 
Henrik describes these as being examples on contexts, where glass can be 
used, because you want to have the outside-feeling but it’s not warm enough 
all year long to be outside [Schmidt, 2015]. The atriums can then create this 
middle- climate zone, where you get the feeling of being outside, but you 
are independent of rainy and windy weather. Henrik finds the way atriums 
can bring nature into the building fascinating, by means of either local or 
more exotic plants. This brings something extra, other experiences, which is 
only possible with glass [Schmidt, 2015]. Robert also finds that glass can give 
==PAGE:41==
33
3. LITERATURE STUDY
some qualities, which can be better exploited by making rooms with double 
room height or atriums etc [Janson, 2015].
FACADE
All glazed facades have to some extend the same qualities as atriums, when 
standing right next to the internal facade, but in some cases one can feel 
exhibited, so it depends on the environment outside the facade [Janson, 
2015 ] [Schmidt, 2015]. Henrik Schmidt points out that the facade design 
should also depend on the function inside. If people are sitting right next to 
the busy city life, and they are working, it can feel wrong because you are 
sitting in a social context but you are not social, to this issue Henrik Schmidt 
states: “In my opinion good architecture is a matter of identifying yourself 
with the architecture and how it feels to be in it, both inside and outside” 
[Schmidt, 2015: 00.57-03.35].
The design of the facade has to be processed carefully to create good 
architecture. Henrik Schmidt also states that it is the subdivision of the 
windows, which make the glass facade interesting. This means, how you both 
from the inside and outside look through the grid, created by the division 
of the windows [Schmidt, 2015]. If this is successful: “..then you can form 
the light to some extent and in particular create a graphical experience in 
the light, which can be really interesting, and in here a lot of architecture is 
hiding” [Schmidt, 2015: 19.23-20.48]. When working with the grid it is a part 
of working with the degree of detailing on several levels, which is important 
to create a scale and make the facade more interesting and by this also create 
an interaction of light and shadow [Janson, 2015].
The facades can be uninteresting if they are all smooth and uniform. Examples 
of this are the curtain-wall and when structural glazing has been used for the 
facade [Janson, 2015] [Schmidt, 2015]. To create some variety and a more 
interesting look, closed squares can be used in the design [Schmidt, 2015] 
and by this also have the possibility to use a mix of materials to create an 
interesting design [Janson, 2015]. Stig Mikkelsen describes the transition 
between open and closed facade to be the big challenge architecturally, 
because it can be too messy, if there are too many transitions in a too small 
scale, and here a total glazed facade outside can create a whole context in the 
expression [Mikkelsen, 2015]. Having closed squares can be problematic, if 
the transition between open and closed is not desired. Examples can be seen, 
where the closed squares are covered with glazing, which make it difficult to 
identify the open areas from the closed ones [Janson, 2015] [Schmidt, 2015]. 
What to use is a question of the desired expression, but if the glass covering 
the closed areas are replaced by another material, it can be discussed if the 
glasshouse then still can be perceived as a glasshouse, because there is no 
precise definition of that [Schmidt, 23.20-25.47]. Robert Janson suggests: 
“..it is probably the criteria that you experience it as transparent” [Janson, 
2015: 13.52-14.31].
Stig Mikkelsen also expresses his passion for glass as linked to the potential 
of creating simple, elegant detail, construction wise. Glass can be used for 
edges and endings and give a nice finish like no other material can do in the 
same way, but a lot of work lies in designing this kind of details [Mikkelsen, 
2015].
==PAGE:42==
34
3. LITERATURE STUDY
Robert Janson finds the disadvantages of using glass in the fact that the 
material doesn’t have any texture and that it doesn’t really age [Janson, 
2015]. Robert Janson states: “Glass has no character and will therefore not 
get prettier over time, in best case it will be unchanged and in worst case it 
will get uglier over time” [Janson, 2015: 10.10-10.34].
Other disadvantages are related to the transparency, which can bring with 
it undesired qualities, if they are not taken into consideration in the design. 
The general perception is often that glass is invisible, and here the mistake 
is, because glass can be untransparent under many lighting conditions. Seen 
from the outside in daylight the glass can appear dark and not transparent 
at all [Janson, 2015]. If you on the other hand consider the glass at night, the 
situation has turned, and the glass is transparent from the outside, while it’s 
not from the inside, which can be a bit scary [Schmidt, 2015] and give the 
feeling of being exposed, because people can look at you [Janson, 2015]. 
 
DAYLIGHT
The benefit of using glass is the daylight you get in the room, which allows 
you to work without turning on the electrical lighting [Janson, 2015].
The disadvantages are over illumination and contrasts. Big, uninterrupted 
glass facades where the light is just pouring in, can be uninteresting because 
the light isn’t graded [Janson, 2015], which can cause rough contrasts. In 
older buildings with thick walls, there is a softer light because there is more 
reflection and the light is more formed into the building [Schmidt, 2015]. 
In classical buildings the light will be reflected further into the room, so it’s 
important to be aware of the difference [Schmidt, 2015]. The function is also 
a factor to consider in the design of the facade, where a facade with too 
much changing between open and closed area for example won’t provide the 
equal distribution of daylight, which is desired in offices [Mikkelsen, 2015].
Having a big amount of glazing it often has to be glass with coatings, which 
affects the light and makes it more boring, even though technology is 
improving. So the quality of the light is problematic, when using a lot of glass 
[Schmidt, 2015].
 
THERMAL INDOOR ENVIRONMENT
Closed areas are also to be considered from an indoor climate perspective, 
where they can be beneficial to have [Schmidt, 2015].
In relation to sitting close to the facade there is also the side-aspects of down 
draft and thermal radiation, besides the architectural qualities of connection 
to the outside, light and view [Schmidt, 2015].
 
ENERGY
It is a challenge to fulfill the requirements for daylight and energy. To fulfill 
the daylight requirements big glazing areas are needed, but this isn’t in 
accordance with the energy requirements. There are so many possibilities for 
optimization that they are beginning to work against each other [Mikkelsen, 
2015]. It is a struggle to make it all come together. The requirements can be 
a limiting factor in the design, but it is not controlling the design, and if it is 
the desire to work with contrasts and transparency, then it is not limiting 
[Janson, 2015]. A limiting factor from Robert Janson’s point of view can be 
the sunshade and the coating on the glass, which are sometimes necessary 
and make the glass less transparent than desired [Janson, 2015].
==PAGE:43==
35
3. LITERATURE STUDY
The energy requirements have caused the windows to increase in layers and 
have framing systems, to get a better performance, which according to Stig 
Mikkelsen influence on the design. The framing system is pretty massive 
so to balance it, the windows need to have an appropriate size [Mikkelsen, 
2015]. Daylight brings a lot of quality to the building, which can not all be 
measured, but glazing is still considered to be one of the biggest challenges 
in relation to energy. It should be considered to be more ambitious in other 
aspects, so the daylight and the qualities from this were not compromised 
[Mikkelsen, 2015].
 
COOPERATION
A mutual understanding is important in the cooperation process between 
engineers and architects. Then you can establish a creative environment 
and challenge the requirements, which can be too ambitious sometimes 
[Schmidt, 2015].
The architects express that they are aware of the practical aspect in concern 
to the rules and regulations, and that they want to be in a position, where 
they can cooperate [Schmidt, 2015] [Mikkelsen, 2015]. Several aspects are 
influenced by the design, and they are trying to integrate solutions, which 
are not only considered from a design point of view [Mikkelsen, 2015]. The 
architects also want the projects to be realized, so it’s important that the 
engineers participate from an early stage in the process [Mikkelsen, 2015], 
also to confirm that the project is on the right track and can be realized, 
otherwise it’s just an unrealistic idea [Mikkelsen, 2015].
To have a productive cooperation and a positive attitude is therefore 
necessary, and it requires people to be committed and understand the 
challenges from both sides [Mikkelsen 2015]. It can be difficult to describe 
the cooperation between engineers and architects, because it’s different 
from project to project, and it’s very dependent on the persons involved 
[Mikkelsen, 2015]. Sometimes engineers can have a negative attitude and do 
not even want to explore the ideas from the architects, which is very difficult 
to work with [Schmidt, 2015].
The lack of cooperation can result in a lot of unused potential, because 
every profession puts in these safety margins to be sure on their side. 
If the professions also cooperated in deciding the safety margins, the full 
potential would be used, and more optimized solutions could be found. A 
third participant in the cooperation to optimize the solutions is the external 
suppliers, who should also be iparticipating in the cooperation, as they have 
a more specific practical knowledge, which is required in the cooperation 
[Mikkelsen, 2015]. 
SUM UP FOR INTERVIEWS WITH ARCHITECTS
The visual connections glass creates is important. This is to be understood as 
the visual connection towards the outside, where you can exploit the possible 
view, as well as the emotional connection to what is happening in the world 
outside. The visual connection is also important in relation to internal views, 
which brings an openness to the environment and the reflecting glass creates 
spatial experiences. 
Glass can be used to simulate an outside feeling, without being dependent 
on outdoors cold temperatures and bad weather, this is particular put into 
practice in atriums etc. 
==PAGE:44==
36
3. LITERATURE STUDY
A smooth, uniform glass facade can be uninteresting, therefore it is important 
to work with the degree of detailing on several levels for example the framing 
systems of the windows, and the edges of the floors.  
A glasshouse doesn’t necessarily need to have an all glazed facade, closed 
areas can be integrated as well and create a more interesting design, but how 
the transition between open and closed areas and the related effects hereof 
needs to be considered in the design. In here lies the dilemma of when the 
glasshouse is not considered to be a glasshouse any more, which does not 
have any specific guidelines. Therefore the division between closed and open 
areas and the material used for the closed areas should be considered, if the 
desire is to create a glasshouse. 
Mutual understanding for each other is very important in the cooperation 
between architects and engineers. The architects are aware that the design 
influence many aspects and are aware of the associated problems in relation 
to indoor climate and energy consumption. They are positive towards the 
cooperation with the engineers in creating better solutions and values a 
mutual understanding to find and explore new solutions. At the same time 
they are also very aware of the significant role the engineers possess in 
confirming the project and the principles so it can be realized. 
The architects agree on the dilemma and challenge between fulfilling the 
daylight requirements and the regulations concerning energy. One of the 
benefits in using a lot of glass is the amount of daylight which is important 
in relation to work, but also brings other qualities to the interior, which is 
difficult to measure. Glass is considered to be a sinner in relation to energy, 
but it should be considered to be more ambitious in other aspects in relation 
to the regulation.
Furthermore it is suggested that suppliers should be included in the 
cooperation because they hold specific practical knowledge, which could be 
beneficial in creating more optimised solutions together.  
Based on the exposition of the interviews a list of relevant subjects which 
could be investigated further is described below:
	
Facade design -  Investigation of the placement of windows. 
	
Daylight - Investigations of the daylight through different methods, 	
	
as daylight brings a lot of qualities, which are not always optimal to 
	
describe using the daylight factor.

3.5.2 INTERVIEWS WITH ENGINEERS
The following section and exposition are made based on the interviews 
of the engineers. Key points in relation to the questions are divided in the 
subjects of architectural quality, daylight, thermal indoor climate, energy and 
cooperation and will be described. 
	
COWI:
	
Michael Jørgensen, MSc. Architectural Engineering, Section - Energy, 	
	
Indoor climate and sustainability 
	
DTU:
	
Jens Henrik Nielsen, Associate Professor, Department of Civil

Grontmij: 
	
Ole Teisen, Chief Advisor, Planning & Design, Facilities Management
	
Jonas Vendel Jensen, MSc. Energy Engineering, Mechanical & Energy
 	
Engineering, Planning & Design
	
Mikkelsen Architects:
	
Philip Hørmann, Construction Architect, Cand. Scient. Arch.
ARCHITECTURAL VALUE
The biggest advantage of the glass facade is the architectural value [Teisen, 
2015]. The most important architectural values of the glass facade, mentioned 
of the engineers, are the transparency and the view out, as Michael 
Jørgensen says, “It can give a good contact to the exterior, therefore a good 
contact to the outdoor climate and the outdoor environment and wipe out 
the borders between outdoors and indoors” [Jørgensen, 2015: 06.03-07.14]. 
When working with glass it is possible to work in several layers of the building 
envelope, and as Philip Hørmann says “there is something about glass you 
add outside, it has the transformation between outside and inside and what 
is in between” [Hørmann, 2015: 05.16-05.45]. The prestigeous qualities of 
glass is the possibility to enjoy the good view and sunlight indoors, while it 
protects against the unwanted rain and wind [Nielsen, 2015]. 
It is a general impression that the glass facade has some architectural 
qualities, which are valued higher than the price of the construction [Jensen, 
2015]. The architectural values for glasshouses also include the materiality 
and the possibilities for details and connections. Philip Hørmann values the 
slim details related to the design with glass, when describing the architectural 
value of glass [Hørmann, 2015]. These details can for instance be seen, 
when pulling back the deck from the facade and let the glass facade cover it. 
Thereby an homogeneous facade is ensured and only a slim connection of the 
glass facade is visible. By solving the facade connection like just described, 
the glass percentage of the facade increases by around 30%. According to 
the engineers this solution has only an architectural quality and no further 
engineering qualities [Jørgensen, 2015].
==PAGE:46==
38
3. LITERATURE STUDY
DAYLIGHT
For the engineering point of view there are mainly two obvious qualities for 
glass buildings, which is view and daylight. What makes glasshouses unique 
is the way you can get a shower of light indoor just like outdoor [Jørgensen, 
2015]. The glass division of the facade has to be arranged according to where 
there is a need of daylight. 
When defining the problems relating to the daylight in glasshouses the list 
is longer from the engineering point of view. In modern office buildings, 
the client often prioritise many employees in the room, and for example to 
accommodate the potential of three rows of workplaces in the depth, with 
optimal daylight conditions, big windows are needed [Jørgensen, 2015]. 
With the big windows a long list of problems follows, for instance the issue 
of big contrasts in the light, which results in high risk of glare. Glare is also 
a result of overexposure of light, caused from the direct light and reflected 
light from the interior surfaces. The shower of light can therefore also be a 
disadvantage [Jensen, 2015].
In Denmark the daylight conditions in a room is often only defined by the 
daylight factor (DF), which is based upon an overcast sky, and does not take the 
variating light intensity outside into account. If instead looking at the Useful 
Daylight Index (UDI), the span of useful daylight will be illustrated instead of 
just an average. Good daylight is therefore not just a synonym of 100% glass 
[Jensen, 2015]. To accommodate the risk of glare and overexposure of light, 
the glass is either treated with a coating or a solar shading is installed. By 
using coating the light transmittance is reduced, which results in a dimmed 
daylight and smaller change of the colour of the light, and the quality of the 
daylight will therefore be reduced [Jørgensen, 2015]. When using controlled 
external solar shading, it will be in use in the summertime and thereby shade 
for the view and in wintertime it is often not in use, even though there is still 
a risk of glare [Jensen, 2015]. In the Danish Building Regulation the daylight 
is measured in an average daylight factor, which in the 2020 requirements 
can not even be fulfilled with a dense building with 100 % glass in the facade. 
The strict requirement for energy and indoor climate in 2020 can therefore 
be complicated to achieve and at the same time reach the preferred daylight 
[Hørmann, 2015]. To accommodate this problem, it could be efficient to 
work with the UDI instead of only the DF [Jensen, 2015]. If the areas of the 
windows are decreased remarkably, the users can feel completely detached 
from the surrounding world [Jørgensen, 2015].
THERMAL INDOOR ENVIRONMENT
Common for all interviewed is the opinion that there are no qualities related to 
the thermal indoor climate in glasshouses. The general problems mentioned 
regarding the thermal indoor climate is the risk of overheating due to the 
heat gain from the big glass facade. This overheat results in an increased use 
of the solar shading, which as well will limit the view out. Furthermore the 
increase of ventilation can cause draft, which is a problem in office spaces 
which have strict requirements to wind speeds to avoid discomfort and flying 
papers. This is not the case in the atrium where a bit of draft due to the natural 
ventilation would not be considered as discomfort, as it is a recreative area, 
and some outdoor conditions are expected [Teisen, 2015]. All in all there are 
several risks for increased discomfort due to the big glass facades. 
==PAGE:47==
39
3. LITERATURE STUDY
There is a tendency and rumor which says that engineers are skeptical 
towards glasshouses, and as Michael Jørgensen says “... it is because 
engineers traditionally have the responsibility for the indoor climate, and for 
the solution to work when the building is put in service. So it’s us they call if 
there is overheating or if it is too cold or too warm right? Or if there is draft” 
[Jørgensen, 2015: 20.49-03.25]. This tendency therefore is highly related to 
the disadvantages related to glasshouses. 
ENERGY
A general attitude from the engineering point of view, concerning to the 
relation between energy and glasshouses is clearly described by Jonas Vendel 
Jensen as he says: “Now that I am an energy engineer, and both struggles 
with the energy and indoor climate, and if I had to compare a glasshouse 
with other solutions it doesn’t have many qualities measured in energy and 
indoor climate” [Jensen, 2015: 07.15-07.39]. One of the problems with glass 
facade energy is the risk of local temperature drop in wintertimes due to the 
cold bridges along the window frames in the facades. These local weaknesses 
are often missed when working with big buildings, since the focus of work 
is only considering the average U-value of the facade which can result in 
an underdimensioned heating system [Jensen, 2015]. In relation to this Ole 
Teisen states that the glass facade is included “.. as a part of the indoor-
controlled system and therefore one must take it in oath, because there are 
so terrible loads of watt in it, if it suddenly goes crazy” [Jensen, 2015: 48.20-
50.00]. Therefore the glass facades can be very expensive in the long run, if 
they are not designed properly to avoid the heat load to enter, and if it is not 
integrated in the total indoor-controlled system of the building to efficient 
get rid of the heat. But if the decision has been made it is important to be 
aware of the resulting challenges [Jensen, 2015].
STATICS
Another reason for the great use of glass can also be explained by the 
advantageous material properties, since glass almost does not change during 
the years and the maintenance is minimal. The only change over the years is 
the scratches, which will occur in the surface over time, and thereby reduce 
the strength and transparency [Nielsen, 2015]. As Jens Henrik Nielsen states, 
“... structural wise the paramount disadvantage is that it is a brittle material, 
which means high risk of a breach having catastrophic consequences, if 
the design is not done properly. Because a small mistake in the surface can 
reduce the strength a lot since it is such a brittle material and it is not possible 
to avoid mistakes in the material” [Nielsen, 2015: 03.45-05.10]. Even though 
there are both pros and cons for glass as building material, the unique quality 
of transparency and the robustness towards external impacts will keep it in 
the building industry in the future as well [Jensen, 2015].
COOPERATION
The interviews prove that the general attitude towards the cooperation 
between engineers and architects are positive, and both parts show interest 
and respect for each professional field. Accompanied with the common 
interest in the project, a good dialogue and an integrated design process 
will occur and the final results will be optimised. This improvement of the 
cooperation between engineers and architects have progressed in the recent 
==PAGE:48==
40
3. LITERATURE STUDY
years in parallel to the Architectural Engineering field has grown in Denmark, a 
profession with focus on the teamwork and how the engineers can challenge 
the solutions of the architects [Teisen, 2015]. As Jonas Vendel Jensen says 
“That is among other things what Architectural Engineering is about, to see 
the possibilities and see what we can do instead of just shoot the ideas down” 
[Jensen, 2015: 28.58-30.28]. In general it is mainly about communication, 
whether the engineers get to be heard as Michael Jørgensen says: “Well 
it is very important, that we as engineers communicate on the architect’s 
premises (…) if you present it so they understand, at the receivers conditions, 
they can easily understand it but also take it into account in their design” 
[Jørgensen, 2015: 33.18-34.06]. In general it requires about 20 % of the time 
for a task to do the communicative part, where as the remaining 80 % is 
time used for the task in itself [Jørgensen, 2015]. A good foundation for an 
improved communication is also the architects intention of function-based 
design, where the close relation to the engineering field is very important 
to achieve a holistic solution [Jensen, 2015]. As Ole Teisen says, “When 
Architectural Engineering is going well, the solutions achieved have an inner 
beauty because the function in itself is beautiful” [Teisen, 2015: 27.58-28.58]. 
After all the architects do the shape and design and the engineers accomplish 
with the calculations and ideas for optimisations, and as Ole Teisen says: “The 
happiness is that some architects can sell the client some dreams, because 
we can’t” [Teisen, 2015: 38.24-38.42]. 
Even though the cooperation is in progress there are still examples of the 
classical gap between architects and engineers, where architects design on 
their own premises, and then expect the engineers to solve the technical 
parts afterwards, or engineers who only deliver the usual calculations without 
looking open minded at the possibilities. In relation to the engineers there 
are a tendency in looking at maximum work done to the minimum time, 
which minimises their focus of communicating the calculations and thereby 
limits their possibilities to get as much influence in the early phases of the 
design [Jørgensen, 2015]. As for the architects they just design and expect 
the engineers to annul the law of gravity to be able to solve the potential 
problems related to the chosen design [Teisen, 2015].
In some cases the miscommunication is not among the architects and 
engineers, but in relation to the suppliers, who claim to do the best, and in 
the end they can not do it, as Jonas Vendel Jensen says “.. there are some, who 
are pirates” [Jensen, 2015: 31.30-32.31]. Meaning they promise something 
they know they cannot keep. Luckily not all suppliers are like pirates and are 
willing to cooperate with the architects and engineers to optimise the future 
buildings [Jensen, 2015].
To meet the complications related to the cooperation between architects and 
engineers, different tools have to be implemented. As a tool to improve the 
dialogue between the parties in the beginning of the design phase, where 
the concept are set and thereby the functions are optimised regarding the 
climate-study, Michael Jørgensen developed a graph as a communication 
tool. The graph showed the needed climate-system in relation to the 
people density, percentage floor area and percentage window area. Hereby 
discussions can be taken up-front, whether the expensive systems are the 
way to solve the design related issues for instance. It’s a static relation with 
==PAGE:49==
41
3. LITERATURE STUDY
assumptions about the solar radiation on the facade, external solar shading, 
people load and equipment load. It shows a relation between a high people 
load in a south oriented room as more expensive, which requires more 
complicated solutions [Jørgensen, 2015].
At Henning Larsen Architects a new department in sustainability has been 
developed during the recent years, which makes it possible for the architects 
to be up-front with their design proposals, when taking the first dialogues with 
the engineers. They do daylight simulations and energy frame calculations in 
some projects, which can be a bit of a challenge as well, as it is more difficult 
as an engineer to change the setpoints and values in the calculations to be 
verified [Jørgensen, 2015].
TECHNICAL POINTS
During the interviews with the engineers several technical aspects related to 
glasshouses came up, among the general main topics were the pros and cons 
of solar shading including the costs. 
As described earlier the glass facades accommodate a high risk of 
overheating, and to avoid this, external solar shading is a typical solution. 
According to studies performed, this results in 50 % use in the occupant 
hours from May to August, which influences the view out, but limits the risk 
of glare [Jørgensen, 2015]. Mainly three types of solar shading was discussed 
in the interviews: the fixed solar shading, the mechanical blinds and the 
screens. The fixed blinds only consider the direct sun from specific angles, 
whereas the mechanical blinds can be angled according to the sun and be 
more efficient in the long run. To avoid the direct sun by using mechanical 
blinds, glare and view out are completely closed off, external screens are 
used instead. The critical feature with the screens is the fragility towards 
wind. They are expensive, and as Ole Teisen says “ you don’t have a reference 
year for how windy it is, and you don’t have any, even though you could get 
a number from DMI for when it will be windy and the sun will shine at the 
same time, so it’s not even related it’s just guessing” [Teisen, 2015: 22.21-
26.32]. If a reference year indication for wind was available, more accurate 
simulations could be made. At the moment the screens are working due to 
local measurements of wind speed on the roof, which defines when the wind 
speed is too high, and the screens have to get off. This is not an optimal 
solution, as the measured wind speed is measured at the roof, but it is not 
the same wind speed occurring on the facade, due to smaller turbulence in 
the air around the building facade [Teisen, 2015].
All interviewed concluded that using glass facades would result in high 
expenses compared to the more traditional types of facades. So if this 
type of facade is wanted, it has to be prioritised in the budget from the 
very beginning. Total economy has been a requirement in buildings since 
1997, but now the facade is added as a section in the building regulations to 
accommodate unexpected costs due to the design late in the process. Glass 
facades can cost around 25 % of the total economy of the project [Jensen, 
2015]. Where the total economy of the project is the sum of the total building 
costs and the operating costs [Teisen, 2015].
==PAGE:50==
42
3. LITERATURE STUDY
SUM UP FOR INTERVIEWS WITH ENGINEERS
To sum the interviews with the engineers up, they expect glasshouses have 
come to stay due to the qualities related to the material properties regarding 
transparency and robustness. The quality of having a view out and a relation 
to the exterior urban environment has a big influence on the working 
environment and the indoor comfort. As the engineers underline, the only 
benefit glass facades accommodate, are the increased possibilities for 
daylight, otherwise it is both indoor climate and energy-wise not considered 
as an advantageous solution. 
Related issues that should be further investigated and integrated in the 
work with glasshouses is first and foremost the quality of the daylight and 
therefore the UDI should be more used compared to the DF. As a result of 
overheating there is a higher risk of draft due to increased use of cooling, 
which is a critical discomfort for the working environment. Another critical 
parameter is the risk of local thermal discomfort near the facade. During 
winter there is a risk of cold down draft and during summer there is a risk 
of increased heat radiation from the facade, defined as radian asymmetry. 
Therefore a bigger focus when designing new buildings should be at the 
smaller scale and not only at the total building scale. In relation to this, the 
communication with the producers has to be improved to make sure that the 
adequate products are available and in the needed qualities. 
When looking at the cooperation between engineers and architects, the 
engineering point of view is in general positive as long as both parts have a 
common interest and curiosity for a common optimised result. To bridge the 
two professions the architectural engineers are playing an important role by 
seeing possibilities instead of limitations from both point of view. 
Overall the glasshouse has a lot of challenges in the field of engineering, 
which makes it a less prefered typology even though there are important 
qualities as well and the cooperation between the parties is improving.
The following list gives an overview of main points for further analysis:
	
Daylight  - To get an overall evaluation of the daylight conditions in 
	
the building it could be an idea to calculate the Usefull Daylight 
	
Illuminance as an addition to the Daylight Factor.
	
Solar shading - The solar shading has some limitations when 
	
considereing the user behaviour and the mechanical control, as the 
	
behaviuor of the users are difficult to predict, but has a great deal 		
	
to say energy-wise.
	
Thermal discomfort - Glass facades acommodate thermal 
	
discomfort in terms of overheating and local discomfort caused by 
	
radiant asymmetry.
	
==PAGE:51==
43
3. LITERATURE STUDY
3.5.3 SUM UP - INTERVIEWS
A brief sub-conclusion for all the interviews is made to give an overview of 
the differences and similarities related to the questions about glasshouses 
and cooperation. 
In general the interviews showed a common understanding from both 
the architects and engineers that glass has an architectural value due to 
the transparent material properties, which provides fine possibilities for 
daylight and view out. Regarding the daylight both parts see it as a quality, 
even though it is often accompanied by risk of glare and high contrasts of 
illuminance level. To avoid the glare and high contrasts coatings and solar 
shading are often used, and this also gives some limitations, as it also has 
an impact on the qualities of the light transmitted and the view out. Both 
architects and engineers are aware of the limitations, but for the architects it 
is considered a bigger limitation.
When discussing the indoor climate in relation to the glass facades the 
engineers had the focus on all the related problems; overheating, draft from 
increased ventilation and downdraft from the windows. The architects were 
aware of the problems as well, but only briefly mentioned them. For the 
energy both parts see a mis-match between the daylight requirements and 
the new energy requirements, as the requirements doesn’t take into account 
the quality of the light only the quantity. 
The architects points out a wish for improving the cooperation with the 
suppliers and see this as a problem, since it accommodates an unutilized 
potential for more optimised solutions. The engineers indirectly indicate 
the same issue. The engineers mainly see this issue, when they need more 
specific information of products e.g. regarding the U-value of the facades, as 
the product information in general are dacumented for the facade in total in 
stead of the local U-values.
There is a common opinion for all participants in the interviews, that the 
cooperation between the architects and the engineers is good, if both parts 
have understanding and shows interest in each other and the common 
project. From the engineers point of view this cooperation is challenged 
by, at one hand some architects, who expect that everything can be solved, 
and on the other hand some engineers, who are unable to communicate on 
behalf of the receiver, so the architects understand. The engineers have a 
tendency to seem negative, which is related to the responsibility they have 
to face when the client call them later on. 
Something doesn’t work. From the architects point of view the cooperation 
fails because some engineers have a negative attitude and do not even want 
to try to calculate and consider on the new ideas.  Besides this, there can be 
a tendency for each profession to ensure a big safety margin in the project 
solving, in order to be sure on their side, which limits the challenging designs. 
Despites the issues related to the communication none of the interviewed 
persons had any ideas of specific measures for improvement. 
Even though the cooperation between architects and engineers is improving, 
and better integrated architecture is accomplished, the cooperation will 
always be complicated due to the specific responsibilities within each 
working field, which are basically the apperance (architects) vs. the technic 
and function (engineers).
==PAGE:52==
44
3. LITERATURE STUDY
3.6 SUB-CONCLUSION
Through the literature studies and interview study the glasshouse has been 
discussed concerning problems and qualities. 
The literature study gives an overall picture of the history of glass, and how 
it has been developing in use and quality through the past decades. The 
history of the glasshouse also gives an understanding of the background for 
design and use of glasshouses today. Through variuos articles, challenges 
and qualities, when designing and using glass facades have been presented. 
In general the problems mentioned are the risks of thermal and visual 
discomfort as well as the purpose of privacy and comfort next to the big 
glazed facades. The litterature study indicates that glass buildings are mainly 
designed for having a commercial and econnomical value rather than for 
having a good working environment.
The interviews with both architects and engineers have given an indication 
of the problems and qualities in the glasshouses as it is today. The interviews 
showed an overall understanding from both the architects and engineers that 
glass has an architectural value due to the transparent material properties, 
which are advantageous regarding daylight and view out.
Regarding the thermal climate there is however high risks of overheating, 
down draft from the windows, draft from increased use of mechanical 
ventilation, risk of limited view out due to the need of solar shading, etc. Even 
though several problems have to be taken into consideration when building 
glasshouses, the overall architectural quality of glashouses are valued so 
high that the glasshouse has come to stay in the building design world wide. 
As the typology has come to stay both architects and engineers constantly 
aims to optimize the building and thereby limit the related problems. 
The past years the cooperation between architects and engineers has been 
improved, according to the person interviewed. The improved coorporation 
has resulted in glasshouses of higher quality regarding regarding the indoor 
climate and with reduced energy consumption. The development of the 
cooporation hasn’t been easy, as there always will be different responsibilities 
among the parts, which results in a mis-match of preferred solutions. 
Another important factor for the coorporation within the building industry 
is the relationship to the suppliers, as they have the products to be used and 
therefore the power of how to develop the products, which based on the 
interviews also have some challenges. 
From the litterature study a list of problems to be further integrated in the 
report has been developed and listed below: 
	
Facade design
	
Daylight 
	
Solar shading
	
Thermal discomfort

4. PROCESS
4.1 INTRODUCTION
The thesis follows the process of T30 but also has its own agenda. The 
reason for following the process of T30 is to accommodate the qualities and 
problems related to glasshouses/glass buildings in praxis. The close relation 
between the architects and engineers at the early stages of the process has 
a big influence on the final design and the performance of the building when 
in use. By following the process the outcome of this thesis can give more 
specific and detailed inputs during the process than might be possible to get 
from the engineers due to limitation of time. The thesis can embrace the 
related issues and identified challenges within the project, and at the same 
time be an independent study to support the thesis with further and more 
detailed investigations. 
In the report the process has been illustrated by the following figure 4.1, 
illustrating the process of T30 and the process of the thesis. The process of 
T30 is illustrated by the dates of project meetings with the architects and the 
engineering company. This chapter of the thesis focus on the design process 
in praxis, which is highlighted on the figure, but the figure also shows the 
relation to the thesis and the interaction among these.

This chapter covers the process of an actual project through project meetings 
with the architectural firm Lundgaard & Tranberg and the engineering firm

4. PROCESS
4.2 THE CASE T30 IN PROCESS
4.2.1 THE CASE T30
WHO
Architects: Lundgaard & Tranberg
Engineers: Steensen Varming 
WHAT 
The project T30 is a new glasshouse/glass building in Stockholm for 
accomodation of a city centre containing mixed functions, mainly offices but 
also retail stores and restaurants. The architects and engineers work closely 
together in the beginning of the project to optimize the building envelope.
The building contains 12 storeys in total, with retail stores and restaurants on 
the 3 first levels and offices on the rest of the levels. The building is rectangular 
with two large atriums making the main building shaped as a comb around 
the atriums. The atriums are as high as the building and approximately half 
the building width. The atriums are facing the main street. 
From the beginning the slogan from the developer has been ‘Urban Escape’, 
which relates to the wish for a recreative space next to and in relation to 
the existing urban and dynamic cityscape. The architectural concept of the 
building is a floating landscape, which makes it acceptable to differentiate 
from the urban surroundings. It has to be a living building when seen from 
outside, and from inside, it has to relate to the exterior. The employees have 
to get a feeling of working in the treetops, where the building is built on tree 
trunks and the closed partitions in the facades are the treetops. To intensify 
this concept, the size of 3 top levels reduces gradually for each level. The 
concept of the building is illustrated by a sketch drawing, shown on figure 
4.2. 
Figure 4.2: Sketch drawing of the concept of T30.
Towards the atriums a big transparency imitate the light openings in the 
wood. The transparency in the facade will connect the urban dynamic city 
and the new building. The atriums will function as ‘winter gardens’ and be 
the urban escape of an interior outdoor space, by being a recreative area 
with visual contact to both the interior office landscape and the exterior 
urban cityscape. 
The developer has a goal of achieving LEED-platinum certificate for the 
==PAGE:58==
50
4. PROCESS
project, this led to some concern in the beginning of the building because of 
fear that it might compromise the design to have to fulfill this goal. During 
the project this goal was deprioritised and is now only a small part of the 
project. 
The concept of the interior is to create an open and flexible space and 
therefore it is designed to have open plan offices. All the desks are placed 
along the facade to optimise the daylight conditions and further away from 
the facade, some meeting rooms are placed. The total area for all 12 storeys 
is 30155m2. The interior plan for one of the floors can be seen on figure 4.4. 
As described the relation to the surroundings is important, as the building 
is placed in a very urban area, and therefore it is a wish that the building 
provides quality to the surroundings through the design, the atriums and the 
retail, which makes the building accessible for the public. On the following 
figure 4.3 the overall design of the building can be seen in 3D view. 
Figure 4.3: 3d-view of the building. 
HOW
The architects and engineers participated in the competition together 
last fall and won the project. The architects make the design of the entire 
building in prephase and main phase, while the Danish engineers make the 
basic energy simulations to optimise the planning and design of the building. 
When the overall design is made, Swedish engineers will finish the project 
in cooperation with the architects. The switch between the two groups of 
engineers will happen in the late spring, which makes the project deadline 
well suited for the thesis deadline. 
4.2.2 THE CASE IN PROCESS
In the following section, all project meetings are listed and described through 
main points from the meetings. The description of the content of the 
meetings is divided into 3 main sections: Entry, Reflection and Reaction. In 
the section of “Entry” the prerequisites for the meeting are described along 
with the preparations made. The section of “Reflection” covers a description 
==PAGE:59==

4. PROCESS
Figure 4.4: Plan drawing of 10th floor 
from the architects.

4. PROCESS
of issues discussed at the meeting, decisions made and response to the 
prepared material. The last section is “Reaction”, which covers the actions/
reactions the meeting has caused. 
The meetings have identified several issues to be investigated through our 
thesis. The most relevant and urgent investigations have been performed 
during the process and presented at the meetings, and these are desribed 
in the minutes of the meetings, but the meetings have also identified more 
comprehensive and time-consuming issues to be investigated, some pointed 
out by the architects and engineers and others identified by us during the 
process. Due to the extent of some of these investigations, they have not 
been investigated during the process of T30 but afterwards. The interesting 
and comprehensive issues for further investigation, which will be performed 
in chapter “5. Technical Investigations”, are listed in the green boxes in order 
of occurrence after each project meeting. 
For all project meetings the attendees are mentioned only by profession, as 
the project is anonymised due to request from the developer of the project. 
FIRST PROJECT MEETING 20/1-15
Attendees: Four architects, one landscape architect, two engineers and the 
two of us.
Entry: 
The meeting was the first meeting of the project in the new year and the first 
possibility to introduce the thesis for the architects. At this stage it was not 
yet decided, whether the project should be combined with our thesis. 
Reflection: 
At the meeting the architects explained the state-of-the-art of the project for 
all participants. The architects also stated that they wanted a “glasshouse”, 
and by that statement everybody knew the basic idea of the building and 
what to aim for. 
Figure 4.5: Rendering from the architects, shows the office with direct acces to the terrace in the atrium.
This led to a brief discussion about the problems regarding the light related 
==PAGE:61==
53
4. PROCESS
to the big glazed facades. When discussing the glasshouse the atriums were 
the main topic regarding the indoor climate, because it is not only important 
for the architectural value but also for the engineering investigations and the 
plants to be selected by the landscape architect. Not all plants can endure 
frost but some need rather cold nights. The atriums are in the architectural 
term a winter garden as seen in figure 4.6. The architectural concept of the 
winter gardens is a high transparency to the surrounding urban environment, 
and the architects therefore suggest having a 1-layered glass facade between 
the atrium and the exterior. The engineers only see the solution with 1-layer 
glass as a possibility, when having an unheated winter garden, and at the 
same time they underline the high risk of condensation on the internal side 
of the glass. Due to the risk of condensation, which is not wanted by either 
engineers or architects, the idea of only 1-layer glass is put aside until later 
investigations have been performed.
At the end of the meeting the engineers bring up the developer’s aim for 
achieving a LEED-platinum certificate for the building to attract more renters. 
From the developers point of view, it’s desirable to get as many squaremeters 
to rent out as possible, and therefore the developer is questioning the sizes 
of the atriums. Consequently the architects are interested in getting some 
daylight investigations, so they can use this as a reason for not reducing the 
sizes of the atriums further. 
Reaction: 
For the next meeting the architects have to send updated drawings and 
models to the engineers, so they can do some daylight investigations of the 
daylight at the critical office levels. Both engineers and architects have to 
investigate the requirements for achieving a LEED-platinum certificate.
PROJECT MEETING 16/2-15
Attendees: Four architects, two engineers and the two of us.
Entry: 
Prior to this meeting the case T30 was selected as subject for the thesis, and 
now the real work began. The first step was to build a very simplified IESVE 
model of the atriums with the offices as adjacent rooms and without any 
kind of ventilation etc. in order to investigate the temperatures in the atrium 
on the coldest and warmest days, as well as the yearly temperatures related 
to the outdoor temperatures. The results were brought to the meeting to 
show the architects and to start the technical discussions.

and the adjacent rooms. 
For this meeting the engineers had prepared some simple daylight

Reflection: 
At the meeting the architects pointed out that the first priority was to optimise 
the soft values of the building to achieve a good design, and then later the 
concerns of achieving LEED-platinum certificate could be considered. 
Furthermore the architectural idea of the atriums, or as described by the 
architects, the winter gardens, were discussed concerning their use, the 
climate, and the visual and physical connection to the urban context.  
This was the first meeting, where the supervisor of our thesis attended in the 
case, and he showed and explained our output from the IESVE simulations, 
which led to new discussions of the winter gardens, and a discussion about 
the type of glass needed to accommodate both the architectural ideas and 
the engineers’ requests. 
In IESVE the building is divided into different climate zones to be able to 
distinguish the thermal conditions. In the simple model, the atrium has 
been divided into zones for each level, which is indicated by the transparent 
squares on figure 4.7. The offices are modelled as adjacent zones indicated 
by the blue/purple area on figure 4.7.  Level 3  and 4 are connecting the two 
atriums, and therefore they are divided into three zones; south, middle and 
north. In figure 4.8 the northern atrium is evaluated, as it is the most critical 
when looking at the max. and min. temperatures in the results.  
From the results in IESVE, seen in figure 4.8, the temperatures could be very 
low during winter times, which the architects found too low, and therefore 
they discussed having heating in the atriums as well. The entire concept of 
the atriums were up for discussion, whether it should be a place, where it’s 
warm all year and people didn’t need their jackets, or a colder and more 
natural ventilated environment, where jackets still could be needed in some 
periods of the year. The architects didn’t seem to be clarified about this yet, 
so no final conclusions were made at this meeting.

The architectural idea is to have high transparency in the atriums to avoid 
sleepy and dimmed light, which energy efficient glazing can cause. The 
main concerns from the engineering point of view are the U-value and 
g-value of the glass. During this discussion it was considered whether to use 
1-/2-/3-layers of glass, and if low-iron glass should be used to increase the 
transparency and decrease the tint of green colour in the glass, and thereby 
achieve an outdoor feeling in the winter gardens. 
The engineers explained their concern for the high amount of glass in the 
facade, and the architects stated their love for the glasshouse. The architects 
try to accomodate the engineering point of view by considering the facade 
design, and whether to have vertical massive sections between the glazed 
sections or having parapets in the facade. The architects see both pros and 
cons for using parapets.The employees sitting next to the facade can feel 
exposed to the urban context, if there is glass from floor to ceiling, but at the 
same time they are concerned if the view from workingstations in row 2 or 
3 will be too limited. Furthermore it should be considered, how the window 
design appears from the outside to preserve the architectural concept of 
treetops. For the facades related to the offices it is discussed, how many 
rows of workstations can be achieved depending on the type of glazing and 
the placement of the glass in the facade.  
Reaction: 
Prior to the next meeting the architects had to ask the developer whether 
we were allowed to use the case T30 as a part of the thesis. Secondly the 
architects had to send the drawing material to the engineers, so they could 
make further investigations of the daylight conditions of level 5 and 8. 
The architects have to concider the concept of the winter gardens, wether it 
will be unheated or heated. Parallel to that consideration the engineers have 
to investigate the  indoor climate in the atriums, with the temperatures and

PROJECT MEETING 05/3-15
Attendees: Three architects, one engineer (our supervisor) and the two of us.
Entry:
At this meeting we had a lot of material to present to the group. At the previous 
meeting it was decided to have some sort of heating in the atriums to secure 
it can be used the entire year. To avoid a too high energy consumption, a 
temperature of 15 0C as a minimum during the day was considered, and 
planned to be investigated. Outputs showing warmest and coldest day, as 
well as the yearly energy consumption were shown. The simulations were 
based upon the defined window types from Spectrum Pilkington for both 1-, 
2- and 3-layer glass [Appendix B - Pilkington Spectrum]. Natural ventilation 
was assigned in the model in the facade of level 1 and 3 in the atriums and 
in the roof at level 9 and 10. Figure 3.9 shows the percentage temperature 
division for the glass combination 2.2+2.2.

[%]
[0C]
==PAGE:65==
57
4. PROCESS
Reflection:
Our outputs from the IESVE simulations were well received by both engineers 
and architects. Suddenly we were more a part of the project, even though 
we hadn’t received any approval from the developer. The investigations led 
to a discussion about other combinations of glass types and consideration 
about the minimum temperature in the atrium. 
Three solutions for minimum temperature were discussed: 9, 15 and 
20 0C. At first mainly 9 0C was considered, as this would be considered as 
unheated space according to the Swedish building regulations, below 10 0C, 
which does not count when calculating the total energy consumption of the 
building. Due to the low temperatures showed in the results from the IESVE 
simulations the architects became more interested in a higher temperature 
in the atriums. This debate was leading back to discussing the basic concept 
of the atriums, whether they should function as an outdoor and recreational 
space or a more suitable indoor space, which could also be used for meeting 
rooms. 
The window types were discussed in relation to energy and daylight. The 
window type for the offices had been pretty fixed on a 3-layered window, 
while the discussion had been a bit back and forth in relation to the window 
type in the atriums, both towards the street and internally towards the 
offices. The architects desired as high transparency as possible towards the 
street to reduce the barriere between inside and outside, but a 1-layered 
window would give condensation problems and was also unwise in relation 
to energy, if heating up the atriums to 20 0C was decided. A 3-layered window 
was more or less not an option, since this would make the transparency too 
low, and also reduce the quality of the daylight. The options came down to 
2-layered windows, where one type had a low content of iron (2.2) and the 
other type was “regular” (2.3) both having the same U-value of 1.1 W/m2K. 
The choice fell on 2.2 since this would provide the best quality of daylight. 
The internal glazing towards the offices was chosen to be 2.2 as well to let in 
as much daylight as possible, and as the atriums were a kind of ‘buffer zone’, 
this was considered a good solution in relation to energy as well.  
Reaction:
For the next meeting we decided to investigate the influence of the three 
different temperatures in the atriums, at 9, 15 or 20 0C, energywise, based 
on the 2.2+2.2 glass type combination. 
Subjects for further investigations: 
	
Temperature in atrium - Further investigation of the heating 
	
set-points in the atrium, how it influences the offices and the total 
	
energy consumption of the building.
	
	
Glass distribution - Investigate how to distribute the glass on the 
	
facades depending on the horizontal angle from the surrounding 
	
buildings. 
	
Glass types - Further investigation of the glasstypes selected by 
	
the architects and how i affects the temperatures.

Reflection: 
In relation to defining the window area on the detailed model in IES, the 
window area was brought up for discussion, since it hadn’t been discussed 
in detail. The architects expressed their desire to keep as much window area 
as possible, but it was necessary to discuss their thoughts and figure out the 
distribution. The architects had a wish for an average glazed area of 50-60% 
of the facades, with most window area on the first levels, and decreasing 
upwards, because there are no surrounding buildings to shadow. Taking 
into regard the retail and the limited amount of daylighton the first levels, a 
glazing area of 100% would be optimal, gradually decreasing level by level. 
Figure 4.11 shows the graduation of glazed area proposed by the architects 
as a start. 
The building design is quite special with a lot of offset levels, which will 
complicate the construction and increase the transmission area, so the 
discussion came up, why the design was made so complex. The architects 
explained that it was a matter of architectural value. Having a unique, 
interesting design makes the building attractive, which can’t be measured 
directly against the extra cost of construction and loss of heat,  because it’s 
unmeasurable but never the less important. So the architecture brings value 
to the building. This can also be transferred to the companies resident in the 
building. Firstly the building doesn’t need the same level of marketing, and 
secondly it’s beneficial for the image of the companies.  
Our supervisor presented how he had built up the model in IESVE, and the 
energy simulations of the heating set points were discussed. The difference 
in the energy consumption between heating to 9 0C and 15 0C was quite

FINAL PROJECT MEETING 21/5-15
Attendee: Two architects, one engineer (our supervisor) and the two of us.
Entry:
The outcome of the meeting with the developer in Stockholm was good. There 
had been some discussions about U-values and the general differences in the 
energy requirements in Denmark and Sweden etc., which was explained to 
us by our supervisor after the meeting. The period from the previous project 
meeting and today’s meeting had been long, and the project had progressed 
a lot. The architects had developed the entire facade in details, and various 
detailed investigations and calculations of the common U-value of the entire 
thermal envelope were made prior to this meeting. The latest elevation of 
the east facade of T30 is showed in figure 4.15.
We brought our daylight investigations to the meeting in case the architects 
and our supervisor wanted a closer look.
Figure 4.15: The latest elevation of the east facade of T30 from the architects. 
==PAGE:70==
62
4. PROCESS
Reflection:
The architects showed an updated elevation of the facade to illustrate the 
concept for the thermal envelope as a glasshouse. The facade cladding are 
supposed to be glass with closed insulated parties between the glass parties 
to create a spotted impression from outside at the same time as the glass 
facade gives a uniform surface. The three top levels, level 10-12, were now 
designed with a parapet, reducing the glazing area.
During the meeting the discussions included many simulations made by the 
Swedish engineers, since the project will be handed over from the Danish 
engineers. The main discussion of the meeting was concerning the facade 
design and the related U-values. In general the Swedish engineers have a big 
risk margin compared to the Danish engineers, and this can be explained by 
the difference in achieving final approval for the building in Sweden. In Sweden 
the building has to be tested when in use, to document the performance is 
as defined, whereas in Denmark the building only has to be documented 
before it’s built and no following measurements will be required. Therefore 
the U-values suggested by the Swedish engineers are in general higher. The 
Swedish engineers had come up with an U-value for the insulated parties in 
the facade of 0.24 W/m2K, which our supervisor found high, and assumed a 
value of 0.15 W/m2K was possible to reach. After having a closer look at the 
details for the chosen system for the insulated parties a better understanding 
for the high U-values was reached. Our supervisor, however, still expressed 
that there were room for improvement. 
For the next simulations they will consider an U-value at 0.2 W/m2K, when 
having a module span at 1.2 m. 
To improve the U-value for the closed parties, the size of the windows 
could be increased to 2.4 m instead of 1.2 m, hereby the line losses would 
decrease, but this would have a big influence of the architectural impression, 
so the architects will have to test it in the facade before deciding. To keep 
the architectural impression, fake mullions could be used on the middle of 
the window to keep the visual grid at 1.2 m. To limit the heat transmission 
through the frames, the architects and our supervisor suggested to use 
wooden profiles or fiberglass instead of the more regular aluminium profiles, 
but this is still being considered until next phase of the project. The glass 
itself has a U-value at 0.6 W/m2K and including frames it is approximately 0.9 
W/m2K.
The selected facade system had been used before by the architects, but 
they had never previously had to fulfill such high energy requirements, to 
fulfill at the same time. The developer aims for a yearly energy consumption 
of 50 kWh/m2, and currently it is approximately 60 kWh/m2 based on 
IESVE simulations, which means that optimization is needed. The DHW 
and ventilation can not be decreased, but for artificial lighting the energy 
consumption will be decreased in praxis, since no dimming profile according 
to illuminance level are included in the simulations. In total the energy 
consumption for all equipment is 40-50 W/m2. The overall U-value for the 
closed facade parts will be increased from 0.15 W/m2K to approximately 0.2 
W/m2K. At this time no solar shading is added in the simulations, except for 
the coating in the glazing. At the meeting the architects and our supervisor 
agreed that at least some sort of internal curtain was needed. 
==PAGE:71==
63
4. PROCESS
Another important factor is the people load in the offices. At the moment 
it has been calculated to 12 m2 per person, since this was the aim from the 
developer. It was discussed at the meeting to increase the squaremeters per 
person, since this was assumed to be more normal. For Denmark you usually 
have 20 or 25 m2 per person.
At the end of the meeting the entire concept of building a glasshouse was 
questioned. Will it be possible to build a glasshouse today with so many strict 
requirements to indoor climate and energy? The task of designing a glass 
house is way much more difficult for the architects today than 100 years 
ago, but the architectural values are big, so the architects aim to keep the 
typology. To make the glasshouse a realistic building concept in the future, 
will require more focus on human values and to be able to measure the values 
to scale it. The aim of achieving a LEED-platinum certificate for the building 
can have many limitations for the design and the human values but gives 
the technical parameters a high value. When using LEED, focus is more the 
economical purpose of renting out expensive square meters than ensuring 
a sustainable building with a good working environment. For instance there 
are only very few points to achieve in LEED for good daylight conditions and 
view out for the employees, which are very important factors for a good 
working environment. This led the discussion to the daylight condition in 
the building. We explained our daylight investigations with focus on the two 
different facade and window designs, and for the effect of having a parapet 
in the atrium as well as changing the internal reflectances of the parapet. 
Our investigations were very well received, and the architects saw a potential 
of introducing the investigations to the Swedish engineers.

4. PROCESS
4.3 SUB-CONCLUSION
The process and the project of T30 has developed a lot during the past four 
months, when following it in close relation to the thesis. It developed from a 
very conceptual building designed by the architects, through several phases 
considering the concept of the indoor climate in the atriums, geometry, 
materials and not at least facade and window design. In close cooperation 
with the engineers it finally ended up with fixed geometry, a strategy for 
the indoor climate in the atriums and offices and very detailed deesign 
concepts for the facade. At the start of the process it was the architects 
having a project, but soon the engineers and we got involved in the process 
work, and it became a common project. Based on our thesis we were able to 
contribute and make some additional and more detailed investigations, than 
possible for the engineers, which at times pulled the discussion further both 
regarding conceptual and technical issues. 
OUR CONTRIBUTIONS 
FACADE DESIGN OF THE OFFICES 
We mainly pushed to the idea of when it is a glasshouse and when it is no 
longer a glasshouse, which gradually forced the architects to decide the 
concepts of the glass facades. Mainly focusing upon the windows and the 
insulated parts.
In this discussion we did some additional studies of the facade design and 
how the placement of the used glazing influenced the daylight conditions, 
the overall indoor climate and the energy consumption of the building.
DAYLIGHT IN ATRIUMS
Throughout the process we made daylight studies of the project, considering 
the different types of glazing for both the atriums and the offices. These 
studies were well received from both architects and engineers and used for 
the selection of glazing types. 
The architects used our daylight investigations as to document the existing 
sizes of the atriums, as the developer aimed for smaller atriums to achiece 
more squaremeters for offices. Our contribution had also an influence on 
the project and the discussions between the architects and the developer. 
Furthermore some of our outputs from the simulations were brought to 
meetings in Sweden with the developer and were used in the final report 
submitted to the Swedish engineers and the developer. 
CILMATE IN ATRIUMS
Several simulations were done in IESVE by us through the process as well. 
Through these simulations we investigated the connection between the 
type of glazing and the indoor climate as well as energy consumption. These 
studies resulted in some qualified solutions for the heating set point, the 
degree of natural ventilation and the type of glazing in the atrium. 
By introducing these studies to the architects, they were challenged to 
reconsider the concept for the thermal conditions within the atrium; if it 
should be a winter garden, referring to a climate similar to outdoor, or if it 
rather should be a heated atrium with indoor conditions.

5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
5.1 INTRODUCTION
In this chapter the analysed outputs of the previous chapters, process and 
literature studies will be confirmed and possibly clarified into technical 
investigations. This is illustrated by figure 5.1. 
20/1
16/2

21/5
Figure 5.1: The green process is in focus, symbolising the technical investigations. 
The figure illustrates the interaction between the yellow chapter “3. Literature 
Studies” and the blue chapter “4. Process in praxis” and how output from 
both chapters forms the basis and clarifies the subjects to be contained in 
the technical investigations, symbolised by the green colour. 
The clarified issues for further investigations, have overall been divided into 
two sections. The first section is concerned with investigations of daylight 
while the other section concerns investigations of daylight, while the other 
section conserns investigations of the indoor environment and the energy 
consumption. 
Investigations of the daylight conditions was a continuous subject through 
the process of T30. During the process there was a need to get an overall 
exposition of the daylight conditions, but optimisation of this wasn’t top 
priority at this stage of the project. The investigations performed during the 
process and additional relevant investigations to get into depth with the 
subject and clarify the possible optimisation areas will be described. 
One of the general issues expressed by the interviewees about building 
in glass was the related contrasts of the light. In extension to the daylight 
investigations the problem areas for glare issues will also be investigated. 
The method of analysing the daylight based on the Daylight Factor have 
certain limitations, especially when evaluating a glass building where the 
daylight is an essential parameter. During the interviews the method of UDI 
was mentioned for being a more useful method, and therefore this method 
has been used to investigate the daylight conditions as well for certain 
relevant designs. 
The section of indoor environment and energy consumptions is concerns 
investigations in relation to the configuration of the building envelope. Glass 
types and facade designs are investigated to see, how the indoor climate and 
energy consumption are affected. During the process of T30 there was a lot 
of reflections concerning glass types for the atrium and the facade, which 
==PAGE:77==
69
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
were not investigated into details at the time. For the facade a couple of 
designs were discussed, but the effects of these were not investigated at 
the time either. The discussions about glass types and facade design, will be 
further investigated to clarify these issues by using the dynamic simulation 
tool IESVE. 
Additional investigations concerned optimisation of solar shading. This is 
an essential subject when working with glass buildings, and also a subject 
discussed at the interviews. It is a big subject to cover, but optimisation in 
relation to investigations in IESVE will be performed to optimise the model. 
Furthermore the glazing areas of a glassbuilding will be investigated. The 
qualities and issues of glassbuildings have been a subject for debate from 
the start of the thesis and investigations are made to see how and how much 
the overall indoor environment and energy consumption are affected, when 
decreasing the glazing areas step by step. 
Another subject of issue from the interviews was the concern for radiant 
temperature asymmetry, when having big glazed areas, and this matter will 
also be investigated. 
Having glass buildings often include atriums or other big entrance areas of 
glass. The T30 project has two big atriums, and a great deal of concern has 
been directed towards the possible indoor environment, due to the wish to 
decrease the energy consumption. In stead of cooling, natural ventilation 
has been the used strategy, and simple investigations in relation to the most 
optimal openings and air velocities in the atriums have been performed. 
To complete this chapter investigations combining the results of the most 
optimal solutions will be performed and evaluated. 
In the next section we will introduce to the model, which has been used for 
the performed investigations.
5.1.1 INTRODUCTION TO MODEL 
The technical investigations, which will be described through this chapter, 
are based on models with identical geometry. 
The original geometry and design of T30 is complex and adjusted to this 
project, especially in the atriums, which have offset levels all the way up.  In 
order to make the model more general, the geometry is simplified so it could 
bear resemblance with similar buildings. Thereby the results can be used 
generally, and parameter variations can more easily be performed.
The geometry in the atriums is simplified, so the offset levels are equaled 
out creating plane facades in the atriums and a rectangular shape. At some 
places the outer facades had some small offset levels, which are also equaled 
out, so the outer facades are completely plane, see figure 5.2. 
==PAGE:78==
70
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
Figure 5.2: 3d view of the model in IESVE. 
The building contains more functions. Level 1-3 have shopping facilities and 
restaurants, while level 4-12 are for offices. On level 1-2 the atriums are more 
slim, because they are entryways containing escalators. On level 3 the area 
between the atriums is removed so they are connected. Level 4 have a small 
passage between the two atriums facing the eastern facade, but otherwise 
level 4-10 have the same plan layout, while the two last levels are reduced 
in size, as seen on figure 5.2. The investigations in this project are focused 
on the use of offices, and therefore the 3 lowest floors will be disregarded. 3 
levels are chosen for further investigations. These are level 4, which are the 
lowest placed office level, level 6, more or less in the middle of the building 
and in the same height as the surrounding buildings on the eastern facade, 
and level 9, which is the highest placed level still having the “regular” plan 
layout, and totally free of the surrounding buildings.
On the following figure the plan layout is shown for level 1-2, and the 
“regular” plan for level 4-10, they can be seen on figure 5.3.

5.2 DAYLIGHT INVESTIGATIONS
To evaluate the indoor climate and consider the placing of workstations in 
T30, the daylight factor is an important factor to take into consideration, as 
the daylight can contribute to better working environment and health for the 
employees. In Denmark the demand for light at a workplace is defined as a 
minimum daylight factor of 2%, which will be the guideline for the daylight 
studies of T30 [SBi 230]. The studies are based on simulations performed in 
Velux Daylight Visualizer (VDV), with imported models from SketchUp. 
In the following the selected settings for reflectances and transmittances etc. 
will be described. 
REFLECTANCES
All reflections of the surfaces have to be defined in VDV. Some of these 
reflections are general for all the simulations, and some will be varied during 
the process. The guideline [SBi 230] has some general guidelines for some 
reflectances but these are quite strict and not corresponding to the case of 
T30, and therefore some have been changed as listed in the table 5.1 below.
[SBi 230, Table 11]
T30
Floor
0.05 (old)
0.25 (new)
0.2
Ceiling
0.7
0.6
Parapets
0.7-0.9 (new)
0.4 (old)
0.4, 0.6 and 0.8
Columns
0.1-0.35 (dark grey)
0.2
Terrain
0.1
Surrounding buildings
0.25-0.35
0.3 and 0.6
Table 5.1: Overview of the used reflectances.
LIGHT TRANSMITTANCES FOR WINDOWS
As a prior study for the daylight investigations, different types of glazing have 
been investigated to see the differences in relation to the demands from the 
architects and the engineers. The investigation is a way to see how the two 
different disciplinary approaches can meet. The architects stated that high 
light transmittance and a high transparency in the atrium would be preferred 
as an architectural quality in the project, whereas the glazings for the offices 
only had to fulfill a high light transmittance to accomplish a good working 
environment and reach the amount of workstations, which the developer 
aims for. The engineers are in general interested in a low U-value and a rather 
low g-value. To accomplish these different requirements, the online glass-
program Pilkington Spectrum [NSG group, Pilkington] is used to combine and 
determine some optimal solutions for T30. The wanted types of glass panes, 
coatings and gas is defined in the program, and then it calculates the total 
values for the final solution for the glass. A good parameter for this program 
is, that only possible combinations are available. From this study four types 
of glass were selected, which can be seen in table 5.2, as well as the selected 
glass panes and coatings.
