In the following daylight investigations type 1.1, 2.2 and 3.1 are investigated 
for the atrium with different combinations, and type 2.2, 2.3 and 3.1 are 
investigated for the external facades of the building. The daylight analysis 
is based on simplified models, with no thickness of the walls and no frames 
around the windows and thereby no resulting shading of the window. This 
will of course have an impact on the daylight in the building but since all the 
investigations are performed with the same level of detail, it won’t influence 
on the results from the different investigations.
In VDV the only factor to vary for the glazing in these simulations is the light 
transmittance.
LOCATION
VDV is dependent on the weather data from the specific location, the chosen 
location for all simulations are therefore set as Stockholm, Bromma. This 
location is the closest possible location to Stockholm city.
CAMERA SETTINGS
For all simulations in plan view the camera settings will be 850 mm above 
floor level, which corresponds to the surface of a desktop. The daylight 
simulation will be investigated for level 4, 6 and 9, as they all represent the 
office space in T30, level 4 has the most limited daylight conditions due to 
the surrounding buildings, level 6 is just at level with the top levels of the 
surrounding buildings, and level 9 has the same plan-layout but is free of 
the surrounding buildings. All three levels are interesting to investigate for 
critical daylight conditions. For the three investigated levels, the height of the 
camera setting from ground level are listed to get an idea of the dimensions, 
they can be seen in the following table 5.3.

SIMULATIONS
In order to distinguish between the different glass types in relation to the 
associated daylight conditions, the first simulations are investigating this 
==PAGE:82==
74
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
aspect. Based on the simulations, glazing types for a ‘Basic Model’ will be 
found, which will lay the foundation for the following parameter simulations 
of daylight conditions. 
Each simulation has been performed for 3 levels in the building, level 4, 
level 6 and level 9 illustrated by figures with the distribution of the daylight 
factor. All the results can be seen in “Appendix D.1 - Daylight study of T30”. 
In the results of the simulations, with closely related parameter variations 
the illustrations of the daylight distribution will only be shown for level 4 and 
to see the relation to the starting point, level 4 from the basic model will be 
shown and indicated with a green frame. 
The working stations are placed next to the facades, with a distance of 0.6 
m to the external facade, as the guideline for the occupied zone states [DS 
469], so the further in the daylight reaches the better. On the plan drawings 3 
working stations are placed next to each other in most areas, which equals a 
depth of approximately 5 meters. On the illustrations, columns are indicated, 
which are placed with a distance of 6 meters. This gives an easy overview of 
the dimensions. 
It is difficult to see the small changes in the penetration length of the 
daylight from simulation to simulation, and  therefore the dimension of the 
penetration length are measured to the limit of a daylight factor of 2% and 
indicated on the figures as well. There are four measurement points for the 
simulations concerning the facades, one for each facade, placed in the middle 
of the facade lenght. Three measurement points are selected for the atriums, 
one for each side, and the smallest atrium are chosen to give the worst case 
scenario. On figure 5.5 and 5.6 the placement of the measurement points 
are indicated. 
The illustrations of the results from the simulations will be followed by a 
table where the average daylight factor measured for the whole floor for all 
3 levels is listed for each simulation and for the basic model. 
For the single parameter investigations the illustrations from all three levels 
will be shown along with a marking of the average Daylight Factor from the 
basic model, marked with a green frame. 
5.2.1 DAYLIGHT STUDY - GLASS TYPES
GLASS TYPES FOR FACADE
In the following investigations the model is assumed to have a 100% glazing 
area in the facades and between the atrium and the offices. This is not 
realistic as the glazing area always will be reduced due to framing of the 
windows, but since the requisite are the same for all the simulations the 
assumption is acceptable. 
In relation to energy perspectives it seems most reasonable to choose 
a 3-layered window in the facades. In this invistigation it is however also 
examined how the daylight conditions will be, if windows with only 2 layers 
are used. 
The investigated window types are 2.2, 2.3 and 3.1. The light transmittances 
for these are described in the previous section “Light transmittances for 
windows”. In the simulations it’s only the value for the light transmittance for 
the glazing types of the facade, which are changed, the light transmittance 
for the glazing in the atriums are unchanged, both from the atriums towards

GLASS TYPES FOR ATRIUMS
The atriums are important to channel more light further into the building 
and improve the working conditions. The light, however, has to reach the 
office through two glass facades, which reduce the light. The following 
investigation examines the daylight conditions in relation to the different 
types of glass and the placement of the glass in relation to the external facade 
==PAGE:84==
76
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
of the atriums as well as for the internal facade. The following study consists 
of three combinations of glass types: 2.2+2.2, 3.1+1.1 and 1.1+3.1 according 
to the definitions in the previous section. The glass type mentioned first is 
placed in the external facade and the glass type mentioned secondly is placed 
in the internal facade. Hereby the daylight is investigated and how/if it will 
change depending on the placement of the glass types. 2.2+2.2 have the 
same reflectance and light transmittance for both glazing and are considered 
as a very likely glass combination from discussions at the project meetings. 
1.1+3.1 have energy glass between the atrium and offices and 1-layered low-
iron glass between atrium and outdoor. By having a heated room with only 
1-layer glass facing outdoor there will be a high risk of condensation of the 
internal side of the glass. Furthermore the use of 3-layered glass towards the 
atrium indicates a clear distinction between the two zones, which should be 
considered in relation to the wanted temperatures and energy consumption. 
For 3.1+1.1 it is the opposite siuation, where the office-zone and atrium-
zone will be more dependent on each other due to the thin barrier of only 
1-layer glass. On figure 5.8 the results from the simulation are shown.

==PAGE:85==

5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
Type 3.1+1.1 has lower daylight factors than type 1.1+3.1, which is a bit 
strange since the daylight has to penetrate through the same layers of glass in 
total. An explanation might be that the daylight is reduced when penetrating 
through the 3-layered glass, furthermore the 1-layered glass doesn’t reflect 
the light as much as the 3-layered and therefore the daylight is alltogether 
reduced compared to 1.1+3.1.
On the highest levels the biggest differences in the average daylight factor 
is seen but here the daylight levels are so high, that it doesn’t have the 
major impact. Taken into consideration the high risk of condensation with 
the 1.1+3.1 combination, the selected glass combination will most likely be 
2.2+2.2, which anyway have good average daylight factors and at the same 
time also have the best penetration lengths .
BASIC MODEL A
The Basic Model A, which will form the basis for the following parameter 
studies, is chosen to be with the glass type 3.1 in the external facades, as 
this type gives pretty good daylight conditions and is most optimal from an 
energy perspective. For the atriums the glass type is chosen to be 2.2 for 
both the external and internal glazing towards the offices. This type reduces 
the risk of condensation and still gives good daylight conditions.

5.2.2 DAYLIGHT STUDY - REFLECTANCES
Reflectances of surroundings can have an impact on the daylight conditions. 
Dark materials/surfaces doesn’t reflect the light as well as light materials 
and surfaces. Therefore light materials with a high reflectance improve the 
daylight. The following simulations investigate how the daylight is affected, 
if the reflectances of the surroundings are changed. Since glazing does not 
reflect the light as well as light coloured surfaces, the first simulation tests 
how the daylight changes, if a parapet was made in the atrium and secondly 
the reflectances of the surrounding buildings are changed to see the impact 
of this.  
REFLECTANCE OF PARAPET IN ATRIUMS
A parapet with a height of 700 mm is investigated. A parapet with a height 
of 700 mm has been discussed at the project meetings as an architectural 
strategy, see figure 5.10 for a visual illustration of the parapet in the atriums. 
The edges of the decks in the atriums have been simulated with a reflectance 
of 0.2 so far, but are now simulated with the same reflectance as the parapet. 
The reflectance is increased from 0.2 to 0.4, which equals the reflectance 
of an internal wall. Furthermore the effect of increasing the reflectance to 
0.6 and 0.8 approximately corresponding to white paint are also tested. On 
figure 5.11 the results from the simulations can be seen.

The average Daylight Factor is increased at all three levels despites the 
decrease of amount of glass. Since the daylight factor is measured in the 
height of 850 mm above floor level, it can be discussed if the glass below this 
height is ‘necessary’. The glazing areas below 850 mm, so the window goes 
from floor to ceiling are however often preferred in glass buildings due to 
better view and thereby visual contact to the surroundings, especially when 
it’s a dense urban context, as the architects also stated during the interviews 
[Schmidt, 2015].
The simulations state that having light surfaces compared to glazing increases 
the daylight conditions, because more light is being reflected. The daylight 
conditions increase a bit, when the reflectance increases but the biggest 
effect is seen, when going from 100% glazing area to having light parapets. 
Especially on the lowest levels where there is less light available and where 
the reflections therefore have an impact.
Further investigations, where the reflectance of the flooring were increased 
as well, have been carried out and can be seen in “Appendix D1 - Daylight 
Investigations of T30”. This investigation did not really have an influence, but 
it has to be taken into consideration, that the lowest investigated level is 
level 4, so the effect will probably only be seen for the lowest levels, because 
the reflections from the flooring only really have an impact for these. 
REFLECTANCE OF NEIGHBOURING BUILDINGS
If the reflectance of the neighbouring buildings were increased, it could be 
interesting to see, if this would affect the Daylight Factor in the offices, as 
well as the results shoved with the parapet in the atriums. The reflectance of 
the neighbouring buildings is increased from 0.3 to 0.6.

5.2.3 DAYLIGHT STUDY - GEOMETRY OF ATRIUMS
The first study to be made, is how changes of the geometry of the atriums 
influence the daylight conditions in the offices around the atriums. 
Workstations are placed around the atriums because the value of the 
building increases for the developer the more workstations and therefore it 
is important to optimize the conditions. 
This issue is a general discussion in projects with an integrated atrium and 
therefore three simple geometries have been developed and investigated, to 
optimise the daylight conditions.
For this analysis the glass types used are type 3.1 for all external facades and 
for atrium type 2.2 for both the internal and external facade and rooflight. 
To improve the penetration length of the daylight, the geometry of a converse

As the building area have been reduced by removing the atriums, a

so this parameter can’t be used to compare the two simulations. From the 
figure it can, however, be seen that the daylight conditions more or less are 
the same and so are the penetration lenghts as well. A difference between 
the two scenarios, which can’t be seen from the results, could be that having 
the atriums might soften the daylight entering the offices, because the light 
have already passed one glazed facade so the light is more diffuse. 
The presence of the atriums can be verified, because they bring architectural 
quality to the building. In later investigations the existence of the atriums  will 
be evaluated in relation to energy consumption, see chapter “5.3.2 Facade 
Design”. 
5.2.4 DAYLIGHT STUDY - DESIGN OF FACADES
A facade with 100% glass area can cause problems due to overheating, and 
therefore an increase in the energy consumption for cooling. Furthermore 
==PAGE:91==
83
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
problems are also seen in relation to the solar radiation, which causes 
thermal discomfort. These are some of the major problems connected to 
glass facades. The simulations of the daylight distribution show that the 
amount of daylight increases with the floors, because surrounding buildings 
don’t shield the light as much as on the lower floors. Therefore the upper 
levels don’t need the same amount of glass, and it could be a wise solution 
to decrease the glass area level by level.
The challenge is to decide the percentage of the reduction of the glass area 
level by level in order to obtain acceptable daylight distribution at all levels.
 
The architects have a desire to keep the lowest floors with 100% glass area 
and then decrease the glass area to approximately 30-50% on the top, level 
12. The exact design isn’t decided yet at this stage of the design process, 
but the architects have imagined the glass to reach from floor to ceiling and 
therefore vertical glass design is investigated at first.
The interior plans for the levels show that it is planned to have 3 workstations 
next to each other. According to the plans, see figure 5.17, this equals a 
length of 5 m. This length will be the point of reference for the penetration 
length of the daylight.
A guideline for the length of the daylight penetration, still having sufficient 
daylight conditions on the working desk, is according to [SBi 230, 2014] that 
the penetration lenght approximately can be calculated by the following: 
	
((2.5 · Upper edge of the window glass - height of table) + height of 
	
glass beneath table) · light transmittance
Using rough measurements with windows from floor to ceiling, the 
penetration length will be as follows:
	
((2.5 · (2.7 - 0.85) + 0.8) · 0.70 = 3.79 m 
This result is based on the window with the lowest transmittance and it is a 
bit from the 5 m, which was the goal, so this goal might be a bit optimistic. In 
the following investigations it will be investigated if it can be achieved.
A parameter influencing on the daylight penetration is the surrounding 
buildings. This is described by the horizon angle, which is defined by the 
angle between a horizontal plane from the middle of a window and the top 
of the opposite building.
Knowing the horizon angle and the room depth of the building for the 
daylight penetration, an estimation of the window area can be found. This 
can be done by using graphs developed in the “Design Guide – Daylighting 
Design for Urban Master Planners” [Wille, 2008]. 
The graphs, from the report show a correlation between room depth and 
the horizon angle where a daylight factor of 2% is reached. The lines from 
the x and y-axis respectively representing the horizon angle and the room 
depth have to be under the graph when they meet in order for the daylight 
factor of 2% to be reached, see figure 5.19 for an example of the graph. 
Each graph is represented by a percentage of Window to Wall Ratio  (WWR), 
which describes the window area and therefore an estimation can be found 
for the levels. 
Figure 5.17: Dimension for  having 3 
working stations next to each other.
==PAGE:92==
84
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
This method will be performed in the following for the eastern facade to find 
an approximation of the glazing area. The investigated levels is 4, 6 and 9. 
On figure 5.18, the horizontal angles are shown for the levels on the eastern 
side. 
      Figure 5.18: The horizon angles for the eastern facade. 
As seen on the figure, it is the lowest levels which are affected by the 
surrounding buildings and therefore they should have the highest glazing 
area The graph with the highest WWR of 90% is investigated to find out how 
much the glazing area can be decreased, while having an acceptable daylight 
distribution. This can be seen on figure 5.19, where the horizon angles for 
the 3 lowest levels are indicated. 
               
            Figure 5.19: Graph showing relation between room depth, horizon angle and WWR. 
            The horizon angles for the 3 lowest floors are indicated by the yellow, purple and green line. 
As seen on figure 5.19 it is not possible to reach a daylight penetration of 5 
meters on the lowest level, level 1, where the horizon angle is 450 (the yellow 
line). It is seen that it is not possible until level 3 (the green line), where the 
horizon angle is 290, to reach a daylight distribution of 2% with a glazing area 
of 90%.
The same is investigated when the glazing area is decreased to 75%. This 
==PAGE:93==
85
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
situation is seen on figure 5.20.
               
              Figure 5.20: Graph showing the relation between horizon angle and room depth
             for 75% WWR. The horizon angle for the third lowest floor are indicated by the green line.  
Figure 5.20 illustrates that it is actually just possible to decrease the glazing 
area to 75% and keep the daylight penetration of 5 meters and the daylight 
factor of 2% for level 5, where the horizon angle is 29 degrees.
On the next level, level 4, the horizon angle is decreased to 21 degrees, and 
it is investigated, if the glazing area can be further decreased to 60%, this is 
shown on figure 5.21.
                 
            Figure 5.21: Graph showing the relation between horizon angles and room depth for 60% WWR.
Figure 5.21 illustrates that it is not possible to decrease the glazing area to 
60% on level 4 (the pink line). Level 5 (the dark blue line), has a horizon angle 
of 12 degrees and therefore it is possible to decrease the glazing area to 60%.
The next glazing area is 45%, shown on figure 5.22, where it can be seen that 
it is not possible to obtain a daylight factor of 2% in a depth of 5 m, no matter 
of the horizon angle. 
==PAGE:94==
86
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
                 
          Figure 5.22: Graph showing the relation between horizon angles and room depth for 45% WWR. 
SUM UP
On the two lowest levels, level 1 and 2, it wasn’t possible to decrease the 
glazing area not even with 10%, and therefore these two levels will have 
100% glazing area. On level 3 the glazing area shouldn’t be below 75%, 
possibly it should be in the range of 75-80%. For level 4, the glazing area was 
possible to decrease from 75%, but it was not possible to decrease it to 60%. 
The glazing area for this level should be in the range of 65-70%. For the rest 
of the levels, level 5-12 it is possible to decrease the glazing area to 60%, 
while 45% is too little to obtain the Daylight Factor of 2% in a depth of 5 m. 
Therefore the glazing area for level 5-12 should be in the range of 50-60%.

DAYLIGHT ANALYSIS FOR HORIZONTAL WINDOW DESIGN
Window mass placed high in the facade lets the daylight penetrate further 
into the room. Therefore it can be discussed that having windows from floor 
to ceiling isn’t the best way to make good use of the window areas potential, 
since window area below desk height doesn’t really improve the daylight 
for working conditions. It will be investigated how much the daylight will 
improve if the window area below desk height is divided on the top part of 
the facade instead.
The architects have also discussed this option, because they are aware that 
the daylight distribution will improve. Due to some architectural reflections 
they prefer that the height of the parapet doesn’t exceed 0.7 m. The facade 
design of the following simulations have been performed according to the 
investigated optimal glazing areas and thereby the massive area. The massive 
area have been spread out in a parapet of 0.7 m height and the remaining

WESTERN FACADE
The daylight distribution has biggest difficulties from the western facade as 
seen on all the previous simulations. Until now the eastern facade has been 
the starting point to decide the percentage of glazing area and then the same 
percentage area have been used for all orientations. This doesn’t create the 
most optimal daylight conditions because the horizon angles can be very 
different for each orientation, and therefore every orientation should be 
adjusted to this accordingly. On figure 5.27 the horizon angles are illustrated, 
and it can be seen that the surrounding buildings are shadowing the daylight 
more on this side.Therefore the same procedure is used for the western 
facade to find the most optimal glazing areas as found for the eastern facade.

The simulations show more or less no change in the daylight distribution. For 
level 4 the average Daylight Factor doesn’t change even though the glazing 
area for the western facade has been increased to 100% compared to 70% 
before. It’s quite a difference in glazing area which should make a difference, 
but when it doesn’t, it must be partly because the opposing building is placed 
so close, which reduces the amount of daylight and partly because most of 
the removed massive area was placed in a parapet and therefore doesn’t 
have influence on the penetration depth of the daylight.  
The daylight distribution for level 6 doesn’t show any change in the average 
Daylight Factor either, even though the glazing area on the western facade 
also has been increased from 60% to 70%, and the removed area has been 
taken from the massive rectangles from the parapet to the ceiling between 
the windows. It can be concluded that 10% isn’t enough to make a difference 
in the average Daylight Factor. The same glazing area has been used for level 
9, so this remain unchanged. 
==PAGE:99==
91
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
5.2.5 SUM UP - DAYLIGHT INVESTIGATIONS
According to the simplified daylight studies in Velux Daylight Visualizer for 
the atriums the most optimal solution for a high average Daylight Factor will 
be to use the glass combination 2.2+2.2, with parapets of high reflectance at 
0.8. If considering the shape and dimensions of the atriums this can improve 
the daylight conditions as well. If the building is high, the atrium has to have 
certain dimensions in order for the lower levels to get enough light, so it’s a 
balance between not ‘wasting’ to many squaremeters in the atriums and still 
have sufficient daylight. The tranvers pyramid shape is worth considering in 
improving the daylight, especially on the lower levels, but again it has to have 
certain dimensions to have an effect.  
If there’s no desire to have a heated atrium but instead using it as a winter 
garden a good solution regarding the daylight will be the 1.1+3.1, which 
was found to give the best daylight conditions, though problems regarding 
condensation have to be considered. This solution will be investigated later 
in relation to energy consumption in chapter “5.3.1 Glass Types”. 
Taking into consideration that the building is quite deep the atriums are 
necessary to increase daylight conditions and create a light and useful 
environment in the middle area of the building. 
The design of the facades are also an important factor to optimise the useful 
area next to the facades. The investigations show that 100% glass area is not 
necessarily equal to the best daylight conditions. In the atriums the daylight 
improves if the glass area is reduced and replaced by a reflecting surface. 
Investigations of the facade show that it’s more or less possible to reduce 
the glazing area and reach the same daylight conditions as for 100 % glazing 
area, if the glazing is placed wisely. Glazing area placed in the top of the 
facade lets the light penetrate further in, making glass in the lower part of 
the facade towards the floor redundant. 
The question is how much it’s possible to reduce the glazing area, and here 
the method of deciding the glazing area using horizon angles and depth of 
penetration showed to be a good indicator of the possible areas. It was found 
that it was possible to reduce the glazing area significantly at certain levels 
without compromising the amount of daylight, when taking the surroundings 
into consideration. 
==PAGE:100==
92
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
5.2.6 INVESTIGATION OF SOLAR IRRADIATION
In the previous section the glazing areas were chosen based on the 
penetration length and the horizontal angles from surrounding buildings. In 
IESVE a simple SunCast simulation of the solar irradiation on the facades was 
performed to see how it influenced the division of the glazing areas of each 
level. On figure 5.29 below, the result from a yearly based simulation can be 
seen. 
Figure 5.29: Solar irradiation on the facades.
For solar irradiation on the facade it is generally known that the highest 
irradiation for the southern oriented facade is during spring and fall due to 
the low angle of the sun at the sky. Opposite for the eastern  and western 
facade, the solar irradiation is highest during summer [SBi 219, 2008]. The 
analysis indicates high solar irradiation on the facades at the levels, which 
are situated above the surrounding neighbouring buildings. The increase of 
solar irradiation from ground level and upwards correlates very well with 
the previous investigations of the daylight analysis of the facade design in 
chapter “5.2. Daylight investigations”.
Both studies indicate the big influence of the height of the surrounding 
buildings, as well as the distance between the facades, when looking at the 
solar radiation on the facades and the daylight in the buildings. The analysis 
is made on a yearly basis, which shows that level 9-12 will never be shaded 
by the surrounding buildings, and the lowest levels on the western, eastern 
and parts of the southern facade will almost always be shaded from the sun 
by the surrounding buildings.
The analysis can be used as basis for graduating the glazing in the facades by 
defining a maximum and minimum percentage for the measured values of 
solar irradiation. The highest percentage of glazing is 100% relating to 220 
kWh/m2, and the lowest percentage of glazing is 35% relating to 851 kWh/
m2 as seen in figure 5.30. 
To give a better idea of the values, the values are recalculated to W/m2, which 
gives a range of 25.2 W/m2 to 97.2 W/m2. The graduation will result in a 
graduation similar to the version the engineers did for the T30 case, as seen in 
chapter “4.2.2 The case in process”, where the division of glazing can be seen

in figure 5.30. This analysis is more flexible compared to the previous study 
in chapter “5.2. Daylight investigations”, since it is not a specific method used

is the good visual output, as seen in figure 5.29, which is a good tool when 
communicating with both engineers and architects. As previous interviews

From figure 5.37 it is seen that the solar irradiation on the facades on the 
21st of December is very low, having the highest value at the facades of 0.05 
kWh/m2 which equals to 5.6 W/m2.
For March the irradiation on the facade is highest for the top levels facing 
south of 4.24 kWh/m2 which equals to 471 W/m2. The highest irradiation at 
level 6-12 of the eastern facade and at level 11-12 of the western facade is 
approximately 1.88 kWh/m2 which equals to 209 W/m2. 
For June the irradiation on the top three levels of the western facade, the 
unshaded levels of the southern facade and the 6-12th level for the eastern 
facade are more or less the same. The irradiation is 2.82 kWh/m2 which 
equals to 313 W/m2.
As seen on figure 5.37 the irradiation is highly dependent on the surrounding 
buildings, due to their blocking the direct light for big parts of the facade. It 
is clear to see the difference in the distance between the buildings, when 
comparing the eastern and western facade as well as looking at the partly 
shaded southern facade. Due to the high correlation among the neighbouring 
buildings it might have crucial effects on the T30 building if the surroundings 
change during time, if an extension upward occurs etc. A further analysis 
will not be carried out here, but it is important to design in relation to the 
surroundings and the planning of the are.

5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
5.2.7 GLARE STUDY AT THE WORKPLACES
Glare is an important factor when considering visual comfort at workstations, 
as mentioned in the interviews in chapter “3.5 Interviews”, since it can 
cause visual disorder and headache and thereby limit the performance of 
the employees. High amount of glare results in an increased use of solar 
shading, which limits the daylight and view out. It is important to investigate 
the risk of glare to optimize the prior energy simulations of the building, and 
the quality of the indoor environment [SBi 219, 2008]. When analysing glass 
facades the importance of investigating the glare conditions are therefore 
very vital. For further descriptions of the different types of glare reference 
is made to chapter “3.4 General Knowledge”.  In this section the risk of glare 
in T30 will be investigated depending on the two following main parameters 
[van Ooyen et al., 1987] [SBi 219, 2008]:
	
Contrasts of luminance in the field of view at a ratio of 10:4
	
Illuminance level in the face of the employees at maximum 2000 lux
The glare analysis is done in Velux Daylight Visualizer (VDV). The workstations 
are analysed in the 3D model having added both the person, desk and PC. The 
height of the head is 1.2 m above floor level, and the height of the desktop 
is 0.85 m above floor level, which leads to the following view heights of the 
three investigated levels:
	
Level 4: 16900 mm
	
Level 6: 24300 mm
	
Level 9: 35400 mm
The locations investigated are the assumed most critical spots for glare, 
scenario 1 and 2. The camera settings in VDV for the analysis of contrasts levels 
are ‘perspective’, to get an illustrative analysis of the contrast investigations, 
with render specifications, measuring the luminance at a sunny day. The 
analysis was made on the 21st of March, 21st of June and 21st of December 
at 9 am and 12 pm to give an overall idea of the risk of glare. There are two 
scenarios for the analysis of contrasts and risk of glare:
	
Scenario 1 - At 1st row from the eastern facade
	
Scenario 2 - At 1st row from the facade in the southern atrium
SCENARIO 1
Workstation first row from the facade in the center of the eastern facade 
at level 4 and 9. Level 4 and 9 are investigated as they are considered as 
representative for the levels in between, and as level 9 most likely will be 
worst case of glare and level 4 is investigated to find out to what degree 
there will be glare here as well. The location and orientation of the workspace 
considered are as seen in figure 5.32.
This scenario is interesting due to the high amount of workstations next to 
the facade and the high amount of glazed area in the facade. The study is 
done for two of the three final facade designs of the daylight investigation in 
chapter “5.2 Daylight investigations”:

5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
SUM UP
Velux Daylight Visualizer has been an efficient and uncomplicated tool to 
simulate the risk of glare for different scenarios in the building. The analysis 
indicates the high risk of glare at 9 am and 12 pm for the most of the 
year both in spring, summer and fall, which equals to spring, for different 
scenarios. The simulations have only been considered for the eastern facade 
since it’s assumed to be the critical orientation due to the big distance to the 
surrounding buildings. For the western facade it is considered to be similar, 
but the risk of glare will be present during the hours in the afternoon, but 
for the lower levels the glare risks will be low due to dense urban context. 
The southern facade will just as the eastern and western facade have a risk 
of glare. Since there will be risk of glare for all three orientations at different 
times a day, the parameter study for east clarifies the glare problems and 
the overall need for shading to ensure a good working environment. For the 
east facade the simulations showed critical glare problems for both model 
A and C at level 9. The glare was both caused by high luminance levels at 
the surfaces in the field of view, too big contrast ratio between the screen 
and the surrounding bright surfaces, as well as too high illuminance level 
directly in the face of the employee. In the morning only some high values 
for the work plane or floor will occur and result in risk of glare, which could 
be expected due to the shade from the deck above.  
The simulations have only been considered for the southern atrium, since it 
is assumed to be less critical than the northern atrium and if glare occur in 
the southern atrium, it will definitely occur in the northern atrium as well, 
which makes it a more general parameter study and therefore representative 
for both.
For the southern atrium, the most critical time of the day was in the morning 
due to the position of the sun on the sky, which will be covered in the atrium 
by the building itself in the middle of the day. In March the risk of contrast 
glare was mainly due to the ratio between the screen and the table, whereas 
in June the main reason was the contrast ratio between the screen and the 
parapet and deck edge in the atrium. The risk of glare in the face of the 
employees is considered limited, when sitting next to the atrium compared 
to sitting next to the facade. 
For the atrium the high reflective parapet can influence the risk of glare, 
but it is limited compared to direct sunlight on the desktop and the screen. 
Therefore the parapet is evaluated to have a bigger quality, when reflecting 
the light further into the office space and increase the diffuse light than 
the risk of glare in shorter periods of the day. Overall it is seen that glare 
reduction is needed in the building during most of the year. 
==PAGE:109==
101
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
5.2.8 DAYLIGHT ANALYSIS IN DAYSIM
As earlier described in litterature study chapter “3.3 Literature study” and 
“3.5 Interviews” the Daylight Factor (DF) gives a quite limited indication of 
the daylight conditions of the building and therefore a further investigation 
is performed in the simulation program DaySim. By using DaySim the DF, 
Useful Daylight Illuminance (UDI) and Daylight Autonomy (DA) is found 
as a supplement to the daylight investigations in Velux Daylight Visualizer 
(VDV) to investigate the useful light in the building and the related energy 
consumption for the artificial light. 
The models used for the analysis have some simplifications compared to 
the daylight investigations in Velux Daylight Visualizer (VDV). The models 
are simplified to only consist of the selected level, the atrium, surrounding 
neighbouring buildings and the terrain. Due to limitations in the program, 
it was not possible to do the simulations with the structural columns and 
therefore all the columns are removed. All components are assigned to the 
correct material on both sides, and the layers and materials are named. It’s 
important to simplify the model as described to decrease the simulation time 
in DaySim, and the risk of errors in the model, which can stop the simulations. 
The model is exported from SketchUp and imported to DaySim through the 
plugin su2ds. In DaySim a few factors have to be specified, such as illuminance 
level. The method is further explained in “Appendix F - DaySim”. The results 
are imported back to SketchUp to be visualised in the plan, which can be 
seen in the appendix. The three models investigated are as follows:
	
Basic Model A: 100% glass
	
Facade design B: Vertical windows
	
Facade design C: Horizontal windows
For facade design A, B and C the simulations have been done for level 4 and 
level 9 and all results are gathered in “Appendix F - DaySim”. Only the most 
important results are added to the report in this chapter and gathered in table 
5.19. The three main points considered when simulating in DaySim is the DF, 
the UDI to evaluate the quality of the daylight, and finally the DA, which 
measures the need for artificial light, when the daylight is not sufficient.

DAYLIGHT FACTOR

which makes good sense since the facade is 100% glass. Considering the two 
more detailed designs B and C, of the facade, the best solution is for C, which 
is expected due to the wide window openings 0.7 m above floor and only 
very slim vertical insolated parts in the height.
USEFUL DAYLIGHT ILLUMINANCE
The Useful Daylight Illuminance (UDI) is found to be a very important 
parameter to evaluate the useful daylight in a building combined with the 
evaluation of the Daylight Factor (DF), as the DF is very limited and does not 
consider overexposure or under exposure of light, as described in section 
“3.4.3 Daylight”. This importance was as well highlighted in some of the 
interviews with both architects and engineers and in the literature study 
in the empirical section “3.5 Interviews”. The table 5.19 shows the average 
percentage of the UDI, where the middle section of 100-2000 lux indicates 
the useful daylight spectra. It is seen that Basic Model A has a bit higher 
percentage of hours exceeding the 2000 lux, which will result in glare and 
visual discomfort, and design C has the lowest percentage and is therefore 
the preferred model in this concern. The percentage of too high illuminances 
at level 9 corresponds very well with the investigations done in Velux Daylight 
Visualizer in chapter “5.2.4 Daylight study - Design of Facades”. 
For the UDI below 100 lux, design C has the highest values, and Basic Model 
A the lowest, which makes good sense due to the glass percentage in the 
facade. A high percentage of UDI below 100 will result in a higher energy 
consumption for artificial light in the offices. The UDI is therefore a good 
indicator of the actual useful light in the building, as seen on the illustration 
in figure 5.38 for design C and for the other designs in “Appendix F - DaySim”. 
From figure 5.38 it is easy to locate the specific areas of dark spots, which 
is the red areas for UDI < 100 lux, the areas with best conditions for useful 
daylight illuminance are the yellow areas in the diagram for UDI 100-2000 
lux and for UDI > 2000 lux, the areas of overexposure are seen closest to the 
facade.

DAYLIGHT AUTONOMY
The Daylight Autonomy (DA) has a preferred level between 50-70%. A level 
below 50% is too low and above 70% is too high [Reinhart, 2012]. Further 
description is done in previous chapter “3.4.3 Daylight”. The Maximum 
Daylight Autonomy (DAmax) indicates the risk of too high illuminance level, 
which in this case is 200 lux on the work plane, which gives a DAmax at 2000 
lux, similar to the definition of UDI. Therefore the DAmax is not further used 
here. The two factors are simulated to evaluate the energy consumption for 
artificial light in relation to the daylight in the building. From table 4.19 it 
is seen that C has the best conditions for daylight by the highest DA factor 
within the preferred interval, A just exceeds the max limit for level 9 and is 
therefore not the best solution. The high DA factor also results in a high DAcont 
of daylight possible, when adding a dimming profile, which underline the 
preferred facade design C.
ENERGY USE FOR ELECTRICAL LIGHTING
Table 5.20 shows the different related values for electrical lighting. The table 
shows that the need for electrical lighting is lowest for C compared to A and 
B. Despite the short time of activated electrical light for C, the results in table 
5.20 show the highest predicted annual electrical lighting energy use and the 
total annual lighting energy use at level 9. This is also  a bit strange compared 
to the quality of daylight as described earlier. Overall the energy consumption 
for electrical light is best for A, due to the high amount of daylight at and 
above 200 lux. The second best solution is evaluated to be design C, as level 4 
is representative for more levels in the building compared to level 9, because 
level 9 is above the surrounding building height, and therefore design C is 
evaluated to be better than design B.

The results from simulations of the Daylight Factor for level 4 and 9 in both

quality of layout and also quite big differences in the average Daylight Factor.

are predefined in VDV and added manually in DaySim. Another reason for 
the difference in the outputs could to some extend be the shade by the 
columns in the building, which are only included in the VDV-model and not 
in DaySim.  Despite the different outputs, both programmes are considered 
equally reliable. The advantage of DaySim compared to VDV is the possibility 
for simulating the UDI and DA for the building, which gives more detailed 
indications of the qualities of the daylight and the related energy consumption

FURTHER DETAILED ANALYSIS
The best solution according to daylight and energy consumption for artificial 
light is evaluated to be design C. Design C is chosen prior to the Basic Model 
A due to the high percentage of hours above 2000 lux in A, since it is known 
that this as well as visual discomfort will accommodate thermal discomfort 
as unwanted heat gain. For the selected design C a further analysis is done 
to optimize the energy consumption for artificial light. For this purpose the 
building is divided into three zones, the edge-zone, the atrium edge-zone 
and the center-zone. The minimum lux level is now differentiated between 
the zones, from 200 lux in all zones to having 200 lux in the edge-zone and 
atrium edge-zone and 100 lux in the center-zone. The depth of the zones 
from the facades is 5 m corresponding to the area for workplaces in T30. 
On the back of the atrium the depth of the zone is only 2 m since only few 
workplaces are planned here. The three zones are as seen in figure 5.40.
In DaySim the predicted annual electrical lighting energy use and the total 
annual lighting energy use are found for each zone at level 4 and 9, as seen in 
table 5.21, further details can be seen in “Appendix F - DaySim”.

The results, seen in table 5.21, show a higher annual lighting energy use for 
level 4 compared to level 9, which makes good sense in relation to the level 
in the building and the impact of the surrounding neighbouring buildings. 
For the predicted annual electrical lighting energy consumption it is seen 
that the edge-zone has a lower energy consumption per square meter 
compared to the atrium-zone, and again the center-zone has the highest 
energy consumption, which is as expected due to the previous studies of 
the daylight conditions. To do a proper comparison, the savings for the total 
annual lighting energy use is calculated as it appears in table 5.21. For level 
4 the savings will be 2.3 kWh/m2 per year, which corresponds to a yearly 
saving at 19.5%. For level 9 the savings will be 3.6 kWh/m2 per year, which 
corresponds to a yearly saving at 42.4%. To optimize the electrical lighting 
in the building even more, the edge-zones could be divided further into two 
parts depending on the previous daylight study, which indicates an optimal 
zone for daylight up till 2 meters from the facade. 
A simple second version for level 4 has been made with an edge-zone and 
atrium-zone at 3.5 m, which gives a total annual lighting energy use at 
41951.2 kWh, and a yearly saving of 9212.5 kWh, corresponding to 22%. 
Further details are available in “Appendix F - DaySim”. For this simulation it 
has to be mentioned that the 200 lux are only considered in the 3.5 m from 
the facades and the rest is 100 lux. Therefore it does not fulfill the needs set 
by the developer concerning workplaces. It is interesting to compare  this 
simulation with the other simulations, because it indicates that big energy 
savings are possible by dividing the zones. 
To take the analysis of energy consumption for artificial light a step further, 
it is interesting to see the energy consumption for level 9 when adding solar 
shading. In DaySim the selected solar shading used is the “dynamic shading 
device model (simple)”. This simple shading device model calculates the 
effects of generic venetian blinds on a yearly basis, considering all light 
available. DaySim assumes that the shading system is activated due to direct 
glare. For this “dynamic shading device model (simple)” the specific type 
of shading device is still unknown and overall for this model it is assumed 
that the entire building, in this case level 9 including atriums, has added the 
==PAGE:114==
106
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
generic venetian blind system and that they will be activated simultaneously. 
Furthermore the blinds are assumed to have an angle of 45 0 downward, 
when fully activated and at this point it blocks all direct sunlight and transmits 
only 25% of the diffuse daylight [Reinhart, 2015]. At this stage of the process 
this simple shading device is considered sufficient to give an indication of 
how this will influence the energy consumption for the artificial lighting. 
Again the three different zones are analysed for level 9, but as seen in table 
5.22, the edge-zone is divided into 6 smaller sections. The reason for the 
division of the edge-zone is due to repeated error messages from DaySim 
that could not simulate it as one file. Several tests with the simulation was 
done to accommodate the error and it finally indicated a programme issue 
where it found the geometry of the sensor mesh too complex. DaySim is 
originally a tool for education as described earlier in chapter “2.2.3 DaySim”. 
Nobody earns money on the program and some limitations will be found in 
the program as it is not continously maintained and developed.

As seen in the results from the simulations with an added solar shading, the 
energy consumption increases with 12028.8 kWh per year. The type of solar 
shading for the individual windows of T30 is not optimised according to the 
orientation, the level or the shades caused by the surrounding buildings. 
Therefore this is only an estimation to see how much the energy consumption 
for artificial light is affected by the use of solar shading. 
SUM UP
During this investigation of the quality of daylight for the three types of facade 
designs it is evaluated that facade design C has the best conditions. For design 
C the main percentage at 56% of the light is within the preferred level of a 
UDI 100-2000 lux, but still big parts of the building have both areas below 
and above the preferred level. This division is not necessarily a big problem 
since the dark center of the building will be used for other purposes, such 
==PAGE:115==
107
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
as technical cores and passage areas etc. For the values above the preferred 
level solar shading could decrease the visual discomfort.
When evaluating the energy consumption for artificial light, remarkable 
decreases are seen, when subdividing the level into zones related to the 
daylight conditions and functions. For the working zones it is very important 
with good daylight conditions and 200 lux is kept as a minimum, whereas 
the center-zone has suficcient light conditions when having 100 lux as a 
minimum. As more detailed and specific design of the artificial light done as 
more energy saved and better light conditions in the room, which is important 
for the quality of the working environment for the employees. The savings 
for dividing the zone into three zones is found to be 2.3 kWh/ m2 per year for 
level 4 and 3.6 kWh/m2 per year for level 9, which is quite a deal. 
For level 9 it is considered that solar shading is crucial to have, when looking 
at the previous analysis of the UDI > 2000, and therefore simulations have 
been done for the different zones. The further analysis with solar shading 
shows an energy consumption for the artificial light at 44032.9 kWh, which 
gives an increase of 12028.8 kWh. This increase of total annual lighting 
energy use is a lot when adding shading, which is not completely reliable, 
since the shading system can be optimised a lot. The solar shading used 
is only a generic venetian blinds, which in more optimal conditions can be 
designed due to orientation, level and external shades from neighbouring 
buildings. Further more the blinds can be optimized due to the use and the 
user behavior is a big factor to take into account. 
In the building requirement the Daylight Factor (DF) is used, when indicating 
the recommended values for daylight. The past years the DF has been up 
for discussion as not found sufficient enough when evaluating the daylight 
conditions in buildings and as the reason for limiting the possibility for 
building energy efficient buildings. As an addition to the DF the Useful 
Daylight Illuminance (UDI) has been used increasingly during the past years, 
and it has been discussed, whether it should completely changed to only 
evaluate the UDI instead of the DF [Rasmussen et al., 2015]. 
From the investigations performed here, it is evaluated that a mixed use of the 
two methods and programmes are preferrable. The first simple evaluations 
of the daylight conditions in a building the Daylight Factor is considered 
sufficient to give an overview. As the project progress more specific values 
for the daylight are preferred to be able to adjust the useful daylight in the 
specific zones. Hereby the daylight conditions can be optimized to the specific 
functions by evaluating the UDI and the energy consumption for artificial 
light can be further optimized by evaluating the DA.  
==PAGE:116==
108
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
5.3 IESVE INVESTIGATIONS
In the following section, issues related to energy consumption and indoor 
climate will be investigated. 
From the beginning of the project, one of the subjects, which was up for 
discussion several times at the project metings, was the different glazing 
types. It was especially a concern in relation to choosing glazing types for the 
atrium in the external and internal facade. Studies concerning glass types for 
the building, will be the first subject to be investigated further to see how the 
indoor environment is affected. 
Solar shading is essential for glass buildings. Optimisations of the solar 
shading in IESVE will be performed for an all glazed facade. 
Furthermore the facade design has been processed with the architects, 
where concerns in relation to a vertical versus horizontal window design 
have been discussed. The next study will investigate the influence of the 
facade designs, to see the possible influence on the indoor environment and 
the energy consumption. 
The glazing areas of the facade, have so far been decided in combination 
with daylight investigations and the design concept from the architects. It 
will be investigated how the indoor environment and energy consumption 
will be affected, if the distribution of the glass area is changed.
Furthermore investigations of the effect when the glazing area is decreased 
will be made. Reducing the glazing area is often considered as a direct method 
for reducing the energy consumption and obtain more equal temperatures 
throughout the year. It will be investigated exactly how big an effect is seen 
when reducing the area step by step. 
Radiant temperature asymmetry has been an issue pointed out by all the 
professionals during the interviews as one of the possible issues related to 
big glass areas, and this aspect will also be studied. 
The building have two big atriums, for which the concept is to minimise 
the energy consumption by using natural ventilation in the summer. Simple 
studies of this subject will be carried out to find the most optimal sizes of 
openings and placement and make sure the air velocity is not too high. 
Final studies will be made, where essential outputs from the investigations 
will be combined to see the effects from the studies. 
The investigations will be evaluated and studied based on a zone level and 
a building level. Parameters for evaluation of the zone level are heating and 
cooling loads, and the heat gains and losses leading to these. Furthermore 
the distribution of temperatures will be studied. 
Evaluation on a building level includes the energy consumption. 
The building envelope has to protect the inside from the outside weather, 
creating an indoor environment. Over the years more strict requirements 
for the indoor climate have been developed in order to satisfy as many 
occupants as possible, and it can be a challenge to meet the requirements. 
Indoor thermal comfort is a subjective parameter, so it can be perceived very 
==PAGE:117==
109
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
differently from person to person, and this is difficult to take into account 
when channelling the indoor environment to meet specific demands. 
The results of the different investigations will be evaluated in relation to the 
requirements for indoor climate and energy consumption, these are listed 
below:
Thermal comfort:
	
In wintertime the operative temperature should be in the range of 	
	
20-24 0C, while in summer 23-26 0C [DS 469] . 
	
	
The hours above 26 0C shouldn’t exceed 100 hours in the occupied 	
	
hours during cooling season and not more than 25 hours above 27 0C. 
	
	
The mean air velocity when heating shouldn’t exceed 0.15 m/s and 	
	
0.22 m/s when having mechanical cooling. 
	
	
The radiant temperature asymmetry from windows or other cold 	 	
	
vertical surfaces must not exceed 10 0C [DS 474].
Energy consumption [BR2010]: 
	
2010: (71.3 + 1650/A) KWh/m2 pr year
	
	
2015: (41 + 1000/A) KWh/m2 pr year 
	
2020: 25 KWh/m2 pr year
Where A is the heated area, which for this building is 30155 m2. 
In the following the model and the important assumptions for the systems 
and heat gains and losses will be desribed shortly. This model will form the 
basis for the following investigations. 
BUILDING THE MODEL IN IESVE
A detailed procedure for building the model in IESVE step by step and 
explanations for all the input prameters can be found in “Appendix G.1 - 
Creation of IESVE model”. A short sum up of the model and important inputs 
and assumptions will be described below. 
The model is built in IESVE with the geometry described and showed in 
section “5.1.1 Introduction to Model”. The 3 levels, 4, 6 and 9 are chosen to 
be the representative floors for further investigations. In IESVE the measured 
parameters are taken as an average of the modelled zone therefore the 
chosen levels for further investigations are modeled more detailed. 
Originally the building has open plan offices more or less all over each 
floor, but because of the calculation method of average values it would be 
impossible to see the differences, which would appear locally caused by 
orientation and so on. Therefore the chosen floors are divided into several 
rooms, which represents the thermal zones on the floor given by geometry 
and interior arrangement. The building is divided in the middle of the short 
sides and then divided into several zones. On figure 5.41 the division of the 
==PAGE:118==
110
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
levels into zones can be seen. 
    
Figure 5.41: The division of zones on the detailed levels.
The atriums have their own thermal environment, which is different from 
the rest of the building, and they are therefore built up by separate zones. 
A zone is made for each level in order to be able to get data and investigate 
the conditions, wherever wanted in the atriums. In order for the program 
to simulate the atrium as one big zone, the top and bottom of each zone is 
assigned a hole, covering the surface 100%. The same has been done for the 
internal surfaces of the zones at the levels, so it is simulated as one big room, 
while being able to get results within each zone. 
CONSTRUCTION
In table 5.23 the used U-values for the different building parts as well as the 
U-value for the windows, including frame are listed.
Line losses have an impact on the transmission loss, but are not defined in 
IESVE. One way to take this into consideration is to adjust the U-values. Since 
this project hasn’t gone into detail with the construction, it has been chosen 
to model the external dimensions, so the area for transmission loss is bigger 
and in that way adjustments have been made to make up for the line losses.
The U-values are in accordance with the requirements from BR10 [SBi 230].

There are not requirements in the same way for the indoor environment 
in the atriums as for the offices. To keep the energy consumption at its 
lowest and take advantage of the possible alternative solutions, which the 
atrium provides, natural ventilation are defined for the atriums instead of 
mechanical cooling in the plugin MacroFlo in IESVE, for further information 
see “Appendix G.1 - Creation of IESVE model”.
The natural ventilation will be investigated further, see chapter “5.3.5 
Natural ventilation”  but for the basic model the openings are defined for 
level 1 and 3 in the facade of the atrium, and for the roof light on level 9 and 
10. The openings constitute 20% of the surface area. A function is assigned 
the openings, which makes the window open gradually, starting when the 
temperature reaches 23 0C and being fully open when the temperature 
reaches 27 0C.

RADIANCEIES
RadianceIES is used to calculate a more realistic consumption for artificial 
light. A dimming profile is defined for the lighting gain, which can be seen 
in table 5.26. The dimming profile adjusts the lighting between full power 
when the illuminance is zero and when the illuminance reasches 500 lux, the 
power goes down to 40 % because the light is needed at a lower capacity. 
The profile is linked to sensors defined in RadianceIES. A sensor is placed for 
each zone. On the detailed levels, the sensor is placed in the middle of the 
facade width, 3 m from the facade. This distance is chosen, because it is seen 
from the daylight investigations, that this distance is approximately the limit 
for 200 lux. The sensors are defined for the workplane. 
In RadianceIES the direct dimensions are taken from the windows as drawn 
in ModelIt. In ModelIt the framing is, however, defined (in this case 30%) 
to be included in the window, whereas it is not in RadianceIES. RadianceIES 
use the dimensions as being all glazed. To adjust this the transmittance 
have to be adjusted accordingly. This have, however, not been done, since 
all the daylight investigations also have been performed without drawing 
the framing so to compare the results from Daysim with IESVE the same 
approach have been taken. 
Another issue related to the definitions of transmittances for the glazings 
in RadianceIES is the fact, that it is only possible to define one value for 
respectively external and internal glazing and the roof light, which means 
that it is not possible to disginguish between the external glazing of the 
facade and the external glazing of the atiums. Therefore the external glazing 
have been defined for the transmittance of the 3-layered glazing type as well 
as the roof light, and then the transmittance of the internal glazing can be 
changed when necessary. This is however an issue in relation to get the most 
realistic results, but the overall output of the results won’t be different so it 
is considered to be acceptable.  
5.3.1 GLASS TYPES
Glass affects several parameters of the thermal comfort and energy 
consumption. Some of the benefits which glass provides, as a high amount 
of daylight, which reduces energy consumption for electrical lighting, can 
also be disadvantages, because problems relating to glare and overheating 
==PAGE:121==
113
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
can occur. The different glazing types, 1-, 2- or 3-layered, including possible 
coatings affect the size of the heat gain and heat loss, temperatures, energy 
etc. The impacts of the different glazing types will be investigated for the 
atriums and the external facades. 
The starting point of our investigations is a basic version of the building in 
T30. The facades are completely glazed, and the framing is assumed to be 
constituting 30% of the window area [Schultz, 2002].
Shading is added, since the energy consumption for cooling would be really 
high, if having an all glazed building. It would also not be realistic in relation 
to glare problems, with workstations near the windows. In order to make a 
more “realistic” basic version, a standard shading is added. 
The shading is defined to be external louvres. The incident radiation to lower 
device is specified to 150 W/m2 and for raising the device 100 W/m2, as it 
gives the best results in relation to energy. 
GLASS TYPES ATRIUM
The two large atriums in the project are special, because the function is 
different. They provide possibility for longer stays and as they are not working 
area, they are not assigned to the same strict requirements for the indoor 
climate as the offices. They function as a passage area in the middle-zone, 
and since they are covering a large part of the facade of the offices, they have 
an impact on the nearest office space. 
Several concepts for the heating of the atriums have been discussed, from 
being an unheated space to heating set points of 9 0C, 15 0C  and 20 0C. In the 
following table 5.27 the values for different set points are listed.

==PAGE:122==
114
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
Overall 3 strategies have been investigated, trying to optimise the energy 
and indoor climate, while respecting the desire to keep the atriums as 
transparent as possible:
(1.1 + 3.1) unheated: 
In the external facade of the atrium the 1-layered glazing type, with the 
low content of iron, will be used and the 3-layered glazing type with a low 
emission coating will be used in the internal facade towards the atriums. The 
placement of these glazing types entails an unheated space in order not to 
waste energy. 
(3.1 + 1.1) heated (case 1): 
This strategy is the opposite of the first one. The 3-layered glass is placed in 
the external facade, and the 1-layered in the internal facade, leaving it most 
wisely to have the atrium heated, since the two zones will influence each 
other, when only having a 1-layered glass separating them, and therefore 
only the heating set points for case 1 of 20 0C are investigated. 
(2.2 + 2.2) Heated(case 1, 2, 3 and unheated): 
The 2-layered glazing type is placed in the external as well as the internal 
facade of the atriums. Heating set points of 20 0C, 15 0C and 9 0C will be 
investigated as well as an unheated situation.
Two zones are chosen for evaluating the indoor climate, a zone in the 
northern atrium and an office zone, adjacent to the atrium. The northern 
atrium is chosen to be representative for the atriums, since the biggest 
temperature variations are seen for this, and this atrium is therefore more 
critical. Furthermore level 4 is chosen to be the representative level for this 
simulation. The placement of the chosen zones can be seen from figure 5.41. 
The energy balance for a room is made up by heat gains and heat losses. 
Heat gains are transferred to the room by external heat gains and internal 
heat gains. Some of the internal heat gains will be the same for the different 
solutions, as they are a result of the impact of the electrical equipment and 
people load which are the same for all the solutions. External heat gain is the 
solar gain, which is affected by the glazing type and the ability to keep the 
heat out. The glazing area and placement also affect the need for electrical 
lighting and hereby the internal heat gain from this. 
Heat is lost by the transmission loss, which the glazing type also affects as 
well as the infiltration. 
Altogether the heat losses and gains affect the heating and cooling load for 
the room, and these are also investigated in the following for the different 
solutions of glazing and combinations. 
On figure 5.42 the heating load for the atrium can be seen. Two of the 
solutions are unheated and therefore do not have any heating load. 
Figure 5.41: Placement of the selected 
zones of level 4 in the northern atrium.
Atrium
zone
Office
zone
==PAGE:123==
115
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
           
Figure 5.42: The heating loads for the different heating set points in the atriums.
Looking at the solutions with heating from 9-20 0C, the heating load for the 
atrium zone varies with the set point temperature as expected. The highest 
load appears for combination 2.2+2.2 with heating set point of 200C. The 
second highest load is for the solution with the same heating set point, but 
with the combination of 3.1+1.1. This combination reduces the heat loss, 
because of the 3-layered glazing in the external facade, and benefit from the 
heat loss from the heated offices through the 1-layered glazing seperating 
the zones. An almost unnoticeable heating load is seen for the glazing 
combination 2.2+2.2 with heating set point of 9 0C, and for the same glazing 
combination an increase is seen for the solution with heating set point of 15 
0C. Compared to the temperature differences from the heating set points 
of  9 0C and 15 0C the increase in the heating load for the solutions with 
heating set points of 20 0C is remarkable. This indicates that the solar gain is 
quite sufficient, to a great extend, for the lowest heating set points, while the 
heating load increases significantly to get the temperature to 20 0C, because 
the solar gain is farther from being sufficient.   
On figure 5.43 the heating and cooling load for the office zone can be seen 
for the different solutions.
         
             
Figure 5.43: The heating and cooling loads for the office zone for the different glazing combinations 
and heating set points. 
==PAGE:124==
116
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
From the graphs, it can be seen that the heating loads are significantly higher 
for the office zone, since higher temperatures have to be obtained all year to 
maintain an acceptable working environment. 
The heating load for the office increases almost equally, the lower the heating 
set point for the atriums is, which makes good sense since the heat loss 
from the office to the atrium increases with the temperature difference, and 
therefore a bigger heating load is needed, when there is a bigger temperature 
difference between the zones. 
The glazing combination, which really sticks out, is the unheated combination 
of 1.1+3.1, where the external glazing is the 1-layered glazing type. This 
combination has the far highest heating load and cooling load, also compared 
to the other unheated combination of 2.2+2.2, which is quite interesting. 
The 1-layered glazing type increases the heat loss to the outside from the 
atrium, which results in lower temperatures in the atrium. This must be 
the reason for the higher heating load, because there is a higher heat loss 
from the office because of the higher temperature difference between the 
zones, even though the 3-layered glazing type has a better U-value than the 
2-layered glazing, and one would not expect that the difference would be 
that noticeable. 
All the different concepts for heating for the combination of 2.2+2.2 have 
the same cooling load. The cooling load is only influenced by the glazing 
combinations and the amount of solar radiation, which gets in and affects the 
temperatures in the atrium, and therefore the cooling load varies depending 
on the 3 different glazing combinations. 
The combinations of 3.1+1.1 have a higher cooling load and even higher the 
combination of 1.1+3.1. The higher cooling load of 3.1+1.1 is again caused 
by the low U-value of the glazing, which “seals” the heat inside the atrium, 
and therefore increases the temperature, which then increases the cooling 
demand for the office. Especially because the two zones only are separated 
by 1-layered glazing, so the heat exchange is increased. The higher cooling 
load for the combination 1.1+3.1 is related to the high U-value, and the 
related high solar heat transmittance of the 1-layered glazing in the external 
facade. This causes high temperatures in the atrium, which affects the cooling 
demand for the office. 
To evaluate the influence the different combinations and heating set points 
have on the indoor climate, the dry resultant temperatures are investigated 
as a parameter. The dry resultant temperature is the mean of the room air 
and mean radiant temperature. The temperatures are investigated for the 
occupied hours only, i.e. from 8 am to 5 pm. 
The atrium has the biggest temperature span, especially for the unheated 
solutions. The percentage of hours within a certain temperature range 
through out the year is investigated in the following. The percentage of hours 
in the different temperature ranges for the atrium appears in the figure 5.44. 
==PAGE:125==
117
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
     
Figure 5.44: Percentages of hours within the different temperature ranges in the atrium. 
The solution with the biggest temperature span in the atrium is the 
combination of 1.1+3.1, which is unheated. This solution has the lowest 
and highest temperatures, because the 1-layered glazing with the highest 
U-value, is placed in the external facade. The solution, which has the smallest 
temperature span divided in the most desired temperatures in the atrium, 
is the combination of 2.2+2.2 with a heating set point temperature of 20 0C. 
Even though the combination of 3.1+1.1 has the same set point temperature, 
this solution has a wider temperature span with temperatures between 
30-40 0C. The only other solution, which has this high temperatures, is the 
unheated combination of 1.1+3.1. The reason for the high temperatures of 
the low U-value and the glazing, which has a low E coating, so when the heat 
gets in, it is difficult for the heat to get out again. 
On the figure 5.45 the distribution of temperatures for the office can be seen.
 
      
Figure 5.45: Percentages of hours within the different temperature ranges in the office.
==PAGE:126==
118
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
The heating for the offices have a set point temperature of 22 0C, so no hours 
below this temperature is seen. The minimum and maximum percentages 
within the temperatures are seen for the two combinations with 1-layer 
and 3-layer glazing. The solutions with the combination of 2.2+2.2 have the 
lowest percentages of hours with a temperature of 22 0C, while having the 
biggest percentages in the higher ranges.
The combinations with 3 - and 1 layer glazing have more hours with the lowest 
temperature and in total more hours with the highest temperature, since 
these combinations have some hours above 25 0C. The wide temperature 
span is caused by the glazing types. The unheated combination of 1.1+3.1, 
with the 1-layered glazing in the external facade of the atrium, causes low 
temperatures in the winter, leading to more colder hours for the office. In the 
summer the 1-layer glazing resists the heat badly, and solar radiation from 
outside and the atrium heats up and affects the office to have warmer hours. 
For the combination of 3.1+1.1, the atrium and office zone affects each other 
more, because only the 1-layered glazing is separating them. This causes a 
heat loss from the office zone in winter, which results in more cold hours 
and a heat gain in the summer, when the atrium gets warm due to warmer 
temperatures for the office. For the office the cooling set point is specified 
to 24.5 0C, so when temperatures above this set point occurs, it must be 
because the cooling system can not provide sufficient cooling. 
According to the guidelines for the indoor environment, the hours with a 
temperature above 26 0C in the cooling season shouldn’t exceed 100 hours 
[DS 469]. Since there is no hours with temperatures above 25 0C, this is 
fulfilled.  
From all the graphs it can be seen that the solutions of the combination 
2.2+2.2 in general have the middle values in relation to temperatures and 
cooling and heating load. The 1- and 3-layered glazings have more “extreme” 
properties in relation to U-value and solar energy transmittance coefficient, 
which causes the combinations to have the more “extreme” results. 
In order to evaluate the solutions from an energy perspective, the cooling 
and heating loads for the atrium and office are added up numerically. This 
is done as an indicator, which can point in the direction of which glazing 
combination creates the highest total load for the investigated zones only. It 
must not be confused with the loads and energy demand on building basis. 
The total heating and cooling load for each combination for both zones, the 
atrium and office zone can be seen in the table 5.29 below.

The highest demand is seen for the combination of 2.2+2.2 with a set point

clearly shows that these two zones have influence on each other, which are 
important to consider to find the best solution. The combination of 2.2+2.2 
with set points of 15 0C and 9 0C and the unheated solution have very similar 
demands, so the solution with heating of 15 0C might be the overall best 
solution. In this case there is an recognisable decrease in the demand for 
cooling/heating, while acceptable temperatures in the atrium are maintained. 
A smaller demand is seen for a heating set point of 9 0C, but it is very small in 
comparison to the relatively big difference in the temperatures and therefore 
environment in winter time. 
ENERGY CONSUMPTION 
The heating and cooling loads give an idea of the most optimal glazing 
combinations and heating set points, but the solutions will be evaluated in 
relation to the energy consumtion, to see how big an influence the different 
solutions have. 
The energy consumption is  supposed to be divided with the heated floor area 
to get the energy consumption described in KWh/m2. Therefore the area of 
the atriums for the unheated solutions should in principle not be included in 
the calculation. All the energy consumptions will, however, be based on the 
same area, in order to get the same relation between the consumptions. The 
used area is 30155 m2, which is the total for the offices and atriums.
The total energy consumption is calculated by the following equation for 
building class 2015:
Etot = (System electricity + lights electricity) · 2.5 + boilers energy · 0.8
The energy factors for building class 2015 can be seen from table 5.29A [SBi 
230, Appendix 6]. System electricity covers chillers energy and energy for 
fans and pumps. Boilers energy includes the hot water consumption.
The energy consumptions can be seen from table 5.30 below.

2.2+2.2 with heating set point of 20 0C, but this solution “only” has the third 
highest energy consumption, while the other solutions of combination with 
2.2+2.2 have energy consumptions, which are very close.
The consumptions for electrical light are the same. According to the daylight 
investigations, see section “5.2.1 Daylight Study - Glass Types”, a noticeable 
difference in the average daylight factor is, however, seen for the different 
glazing combinations, but this isn’t reflected in the energy consumptions for 
the electrical light. As described earlier, it is only the transmittance for the 
internal glazing of the atriums which is changed in RadianceIES, and this is 
apparently not enough to influence the daylight in RadianceIES. 
It can be concluded that the right glazing combination combined with the 
right heating concept have an impact. 
One would think that the unheated solutions would give the lowest energy 
consumption, and it does, if the right glazing types are used as well, otherwise 
the energy consumption is increased. 
It is furthermore concluded that combination 2.2+2.2 with a heating set 
point of 15 0C is the overall best solution.

points and for a version without the atriums. All the consumptions are specified in the unit [KWh/m2/
year].
 
The lowest consumption is actually seen for the version without atriums. 
Savings are seen for the electrical light, because the daylight isn’t reduced 
through more layers of glass and the system electricity, because the cooling 
load has decreased a bit. An increase is seen for the heating load, because the 
heat loss has increased, for the area which was covered by the atriums. The 
increase is in principle bigger than the reduction, but because of the energy 
factors the increase have lower effect than the reduction. The consumptions 
are, however, very close, when having a lower heating set point than 20 0C so 
the difference is not essential. 
GLASS TYPES FACADE
Buildings with a big percentage of glass are very sensitive towards the 
==PAGE:129==
121
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
outdoor environment, and one of the benefits is that they can take advantage 
of the solar gain and natural daylight. There is, however, a very fine balance 
between making it a benefit and not a disadvantage. In summer times it is 
problematic with big glazing areas, as the solar gain causes an overheated 
indoor environment and a bigger demand for cooling, while in wintertime 
the heat from the solar gain is desired in relation to reduce the demand for 
heating. Especially also seen in relation to the fact that glazing often has a 
higher U-value than “normal” walls, and therefore increases the heat loss. 
The energy consumption and the indoor environment are important and 
complex aspects to consider when choosing the right glazing type.  
Two 3-layered glazings are investigated for the facade to see the influence 
on the energy consumption and the indoor environment. One glazing have 
one Low E coating and the other have two, which reduces the heat loss even 
further. The properties of the glazings can be seen in table 5.32.
