It has been chosen, to investigate only two 3-layered glazings, because these 
have the most realistic properties in relation to a low U-value for reducing the 
heat loss and save energy. The question is, however, how the environment 
and the heating and cooling demand are influenced in relation to having one 
or two Low E coatings. 
To evaluate the effect of the glazing types, two zones with the same geometry, 
but different orientations have been chosen to see the temperature 
distribution and heating and cooling loads for the two glazing types. The 
zones are placed on level 9 with external walls turning south and east for the 
first orientation and north and east for the second orientation. 
On figure 5.46 the placement of the two zones can be seen.
Level 9 is chosen, because it is considered to be the worst case scenario, 
where the difference in relation to solar gain for the different glazing types 
and the impact of this, will be most pronounced. Furthermore the two 
orientations are chosen, because they have the most different conditions in 
relation to the solar gain, and therefore the difference in the influence of the 
glazing could be interesting to investigate. 
The zones are placed towards external facade area, and are therefore directly 
influenced by heat losses through the facades and infiltration, and heat gain 
from the solar radiation, which are factors affecting the cooling and heating 
demand. On figure 5.47 the cooling and heating demand are shown for the 
two orientations and the two glazing types.
Figure 5.46: Placement of the selected 
zones, level 9 for evaluation of 
results.

Zone
Southern 
Zone
==PAGE:130==
122
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
    
Figure 5.47: Heating and cooling load for the two glazing types and orientations. 
The northern orientation has a higher heating and cooling load. The higher 
heating demand is caused by the lack of solar gain. It is, however, interesting 
that the north orientated zone has a higher cooling demand, because there 
is a lack of solar gain, and therefore the south should have the highest 
cooling demand. An influencing factor on this must be the solar shading. 
The solar shading is lowered, when the incident radiation reaches 150 W/
m2 and raised when it is 100 W/m2. Therefore it will be relatively often that 
the solar shading is lowered for the southern facade, while not very often for 
the northern facade. Evidently in the summer time this decreases the solar 
gain so much that the northern orientation have a higher cooling demand. 
Running the simulation one more time without the solar shading confirms 
the assumption. The cooling demand is higher for the south oriented zone. 
Glazing 3.1 has the highest heating demand for the northern orientation, 
because the solar gain is lower than for the southern orientation during 
winter time. For the southern orientation glazing 3.1 also causes a higher 
heating demand, so it can be concluded that the extra low emissivity coating 
has an effect in minimising the heat loss. The cooling load is, however, seen 
to be biggest for glazing 3.2, exactly because the glazing reduces the heat 
loss. 
As the next issue, the influence on the temperatures is investigated, where 
the percentage of the occupied hours within certain temperatures is 
investigated. The result can be seen on figure 5.48.
==PAGE:131==

5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
       
Figure 5.48: The percentages of hours distributed on temperatures for the two glazing types and 
orientations. 
According to [DS 469] the hours of temperature above 26 0C in the cooling 
season shouldn not exceed 100, which is fulfilled. 
The biggest percentage of hours is seen with a temperature of 22 0C for both 
glazings and orientations.
Secondly the highest percentage of hours is seen for temperatures from 
24 0C to 25 0C. Glazing 3.1 for both orientations has higher percentages of 
hours of the lowest temperature 22 0C, while it has the lowest percentage of 
hours for the higher temperatures. This is because glazing 3.2 decreases the 
heat loss, so the temperatures increase and result in more warm hours than 
glazing for 3.1. 
It is interesting that the northern orientation has a bit more warm hours 
of 24 0C to 25 0C than the southern orientation. This must be because of 
the solar shading, which reduces the solar radiation in the summer time, so 
much that the northern orientation has more warm hours. 
The distribution of the temperatures is close for the two glazings and 
orientations, so this factor should not be the main concern when choosing 
the glazing type. Differences are, however, seen for the heating and cooling 
demand, which influence the energy consumption. In table 5.33 the total 
heating and cooling demand are added together numerically.

5.3.2 FACADE DESIGN
The design of the facades and glazing areas have been discussed during 
the process. The amount of glazing and placement of this affects as earlier 
mentioned the indoor environment and energy consumption. Having a lot 
of glass improves the daylight and reduces the need for heating, but can 
also lead to problems with glare and an increased need for cooling in the 
summer as well as a big transmission loss. An efficient solar shading can help 
preventing some of these issues as well as decreasing the glazing area during 
replacing some of the insulated  parts. 
The glazing area has been discussed from the beginning of the project T30, but 
there has, however, also from the beginning been a mutual understanding, 
that an all glazed building was not realistic. In the following 3 different design 
approaches will be investigated. At first an all glazed facade, with framing 
constituting 30 %, will be investigated in relation to optimisation of solar 
shading. 
Secondly a facade, where the glazing is decreased and combined with 
insulated parts. For this approach, two concepts for the facades have been 
discussed from the beginning of the project. Design B, with a vertical window 
design, with windows from floor to ceiling and Design C, with a parapet of 700 
mm and windows from the parapet to the ceiling. In section “5.2.5 Daylight 
Study - Design of Facades” a distribution of the glazing areas, decreasing 
upwards for each floor was found, which was used for the two designs and 
daylight investigations for the designs were performed. 
In the last approach a facade will be investigated, where the same glazing 
areas, as used in the second approach will be distributed equally on each level. 
This might be preferable in relation to mass production of the components 
and to shorten the construction period. 
Evaluating the approaches might lead to combinations of these for the most 
optimal designs, and this will be investigated in the end. 
The approaches will be investigated on a zone level with temperature 
distribution and heating and cooling loads and on a building level, where the 
energy consumption will also be investigated. 
==PAGE:133==
125
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
OPTIMISATION OF FACADE WITH SOLAR SHADING
Solar shading can have two purposes: to reduce the glare and to avoid 
overheating, where it either is one common shading or it can be divided 
into two. For this investigation it will be analysed as one shading device. 
Solar shading is a simple and cheap solution for preventing overheating, 
compared to the alternative, which will be solving it through the technical 
installations alone. Overheating can be avoided by solar shading, but it has 
to be controlled for the purpose, since various combinations are possible. 
Solar shading can prevent the big temperature differences in a room, which 
can be caused by solar radiation on sunny days. When blocking the daylight 
it can lead to an increase of the energy consumption used for artificial light 
instead [Littlefair, 2006].
There are two predefined types of solar shading devices in IESVE, louvres and 
shutters, which are not different in terms of performance. Either a predefined 
device is selected as active or not active. For the predefined solar shadings 
there are a few parameters to vary when optimising the shading device for 
the specific building:
	
“Percentage profile group”
	
“Incident radiation to lower device”
	
“Incident radiation to raise device”
	
“Transmission factors at 150 increments”
In the “Percentage profile group”, the timing of the operation of the shading 
device is defined. If the values are greater than 50% the shading device is 
considered in use, which means it is down. When the shading device is below 
50% or set to none, the shading device will only be controlled by the incident 
solar radiation. 
The “Incident radiation to lower device” and the “Incident radiation to raise 
device” gives the time for starting to respectively lowering and raising the 
shading device, which is defined by a level of radiation on the facade. The 
two factors are only in function, when the function in the “Percentage profile 
group” is off as previous described. 
The “Transmission factors at 150 increments” defines the solar altitude at 
a vertical plane, which is perpendicular to the glazed facade, both of these 
factors are both in use when controlling by a profile and the incident radiation 
[Virtual Environment, IES].
Due to the equal performance of the two types of solar shading, as described 
above, louvres are used through the investigations. The analysis will focus at 
the southern zone at level 9, due to high exposure to the sun and thereby it 
will be clear to see the effects of the different types of shading. 
The total energy consumption is calculated by the following equation for 
building class 2015:
Etot = (System electricity + lights electricity) · 2.5 + boilers energy · 0.8
The analysis is divided into steps, taken one by one to see the effects of each 
parameter change.
==PAGE:134==
126
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
STEP 1: INCIDENT RADIATION TO LOWER AND RAISE THE SHADING
Controlling from the incident radiation on the facade, also expressed by the 
irradiation on the facade is the first strategy for solar shading devices to 
be investigated. The solar shading will be activated and therefore lowered, 
when the irradiation exceeds this value, and then again be raised when the 
irradiation is lower than a certain value. According to [SBi 2011-15, 2011] 
the range for closing the shading is 50-800 W/m2, where the standard value 
is 150 W/m2 and for raising the shading the range is 20-500 W/m2, having a 
standard value at 80 W/m2 [SBi 2011-15, 2011].
Solar irradiation on a facade is the sum of the direct solar radiation, the 
diffuse radiation from the sky and the reflected radiation from the external 
surrounding surfaces [Beck et al., 2010]. The illuminance level and light effect 
for direct and diffuse radiation are listed in table 5.35, where the resulting 
solar irradiation is found as well. The diffuse radiation is divided into clear 
sky and overcast sky, where the radiation from clear sky is twice as high, and 
therefore it can be important to distinguish between the two types of diffuse 
radiation when analysing the irradiation.
Illuminance level, 
lux [lm/m2]
Light effect
[lm/W]
Solar irradiation
[W/m2]

854.4

30000

Diffuse - Overcast sky
12000

Table 5.35: The illuminance level and light effect are from [SBi 219, 2008] and the solar irradiation is 
calculated. 
The ‘Basic-Louvres’ have the basic settings as used in the previous IESVE 
investigations. These basic-settings for the transmission factors are 0.65 
percent for an angle of 00, 0.40 for an angle of 150, and 0.2 for an angle of 
300, which is common settings for a simple shading device [Shading Device, 
IES: Table 9]. 
The first step is to test different values for the incident radiation to lower and 
raise the solar shading, to be able to evaluate the preferred combination. 
The combinations for lowering and raising the shading are seen in table 
5.35. Some references of common shading strategies are used for the ‘Basic 
- Louvres’ and ‘Test 5’, when testing the range of irradiation. These values 
are based on the diffuse irradiation, as previously described. Looking at 
the building scale the total energy consumption is calculated for building 
regulations of 2015, using the equation previously described. The total 
energy consumption is based on the heating, cooling, system electricity 
and lights electricity. To further see the relation to the solar shading device 
the southern zone at level 9 has been investigated for the sensible heating, 
sensible cooling, solar gain and lighting gain and listed in table 5.36 as well.

shows an irradiation on the 21st of March at parts of south at 471 W/m2 and 
parts of east and west at 209 W/m2. On 21st of June parts of the facades for 
east, south and west have a maximum irradiation of 313 W/m2.
STEP 2: TRANSMISSION FACTORS
The next step in developing the solar shading is to investigate the difference 
of the transmission factors for the shading. This is done by changing the 
transmission factors at 150 increments. 
The height of the sun on the sky in Denmark is during the year in the range 
from 120 to 580 for a south oriented facade. For east and west, the solar 
angle is in the range from 00 to 280 [SBi 219, 2008]. The basic settings for 
the transmission factors of the solar shading can therefore be neglected for 
the highest inclinations of the sun. In table 5.37 the selected transmission 
factors at 150 increments are defined. The investigated transmission factors 
are mainly found as preferred values in references to investigate the most 
optimal values. The references are listed in the as well. The best combination 
of raising and lowering the solar shading just found is used.

STEP 3: PERCENTAGE PROFILE
The control profile for the solar shading has a great impact, when evaluating 
the efficiency of the solar shading. To give the profile for temperature in 
the room an extra dimension is added to the control system. The room 
temperature can be an indicator of a high amount of solar gain through the 
windows due to overexposure from direct solar irradiation on the facade. The 
==PAGE:137==
129
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
solar shading can therefore be activated by a maximum heating set point for 
the indoor air temperature, where the shading will be lowered to block off 
the unwanted solar radiation. The temperature in the room will therefore be 
a crucial parameter to control the shading device. The modulating profile can 
activate the shading device as an addition to the settings for the irradiation 
on the facade. 
Another parameter to control the solar shading is the illuminance level in 
the room, where the risk of glare from direct sunlight or high lux-levels at the 
work plane or at surfaces within the field of view will be the controlling factor 
for lowering and raising the shading device.
IESVE has some program related limitations, when having a temperature-
depending profile for the entire building, which is a too complex simulation. 
Therefore each zone has to have a defined profile, which makes it more 
complex to define solar shading at bigger models.  At the moment in IESVE this 
would mean that an analysis for each room would have to be done, instead 
of being able to combine it in one common profile. Further improvements 
in the program could be preferred when doing detailed analysis for solar 
shading. 
For these simulations the temperature profiles are simplified to base the 
simulation upon the southern zone for level 9, to be able to compare it 
with the other simulations. The temperature in the room is affected by the 
solar radiation on the facade, and some studies show that the preferred 
temperature in a room is 22 0C as a maximum [Huizenga et al., 2006]. The 
likelihood of high temperatures indoors can be divided into four temperature 
ranges [Littlefair, 2006]:
Not significant: 	
T < 20.50C
Slightly significant: 	
20.5 ≤ T < 22.00C
Medium significant:	 22 ≤  T < 23.50C
Highly significant: 	
T ≥ 23.50C
The temperature controlling profiles are defined by the set point of the 
maximum temperature in the room, which will activate the solar shading. 
Due to the maximum preferred temperatures, as listed above, the profiles 
are tested for shading to be activated, when the temperature is above 220C, 
230C and 240C, as seen in the table 5.39.
Basic - Louvres

ta(32)>24

5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
From the results in table 5.39 it is evaluated that the profile, which will 
activate the solar shading at 220C is preferred. This result is based on an 
decreased solar gain, with only a limited increase of the heating load and 
decrease of cooling load. In general for the values of lighting gain, it seems 
like there are some inconsistencies with the relation between the solar gain 
and lighting gain, when using RadianceIES.
CONTROLLING ILLUMINANCE LEVEL PROFILE
The other control profile for solar shading is dependent on the illuminance 
in the room, preferred at 200 lux in the sensor point in 3 meters depth of 
the room. For this analysis a similar simulation was tested, where the sensor 
point (e1) in same southern zone at level 9 (32) was considered giving a 
profile as follows, ta(32)>(e1,200). Again the complexity of the simulation 
was too much for IESVE, and no simulations were possible. In general it 
would have been a good parameter to investigate due to high risk of glare at 
the workspaces, which would activate the solar shading different from the 
temperature controlled profile.
SUM UP
During the investigation it is seen how big an impact the different solutions 
for solar shading has, when evaluating the energy efficiency of the building. 
The three parameters investigated in steps for controlling the solar shading as 
optimal as possible for the case of T30 gave one preferred combination. This 
combination has an incident radiation to lower the shading device at 150 W/
m2 and an incident radiation to raise the shading device again at 100 W/m2, 
with a transmission factor of 0.65 for increment at 00, 0.40 for increment at 
150 and 0.20 for increment at 300. Finally the controlling temperature profile 
is activating the shading device for temperatures above 220C, which has to 
be specified for each room. Would have been desirable if it could be made 
for the entire building.
FACADE DESIGN B AND C
Two designs for the facade have been in play, as have been described in 
chapter “4. Process in Praxis”. From the beginning the architects have desired 
a design with vertical windows and insulated parts. This division has been 
interesting, as the architects have wanted to interrupt the horizontal lines 
from the front edges of the decks. The concept with a vertical division for a 
facade design, which will be investigated, will be called “B”. 
During the project meetings the architects have, however, expressed their 
concern in relation to the design, because the vertical division does not 
take advantage of the full potential of the glazing areas in relation to the 
distribution of daylight, where a horizontal division, with the same glazing 
area would provide a better daylight distribution, as seen in the chapter 
“5.2.4 Daylight Study - Facade design”. Considering a horizontal division with 
a parapet, however, needed to have a height of 700 mm in order to match in 
aesthetically with the visual dimensions of the construction. A parapet with 
a height of 700 mm and spare unglazed area divided between the windows, 
which have a width of 1500 mm, will be the concept for design ‘C’, which will 
be the second investigated design. 
Concerning the specific glazing areas, the architects desired a continuous 
decreasing  of the glazing areas from the bottom floors to the upper floors. 
==PAGE:139==
131
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
This will be the concept for the two facade designs based on the specific 
distribution of glazing areas found in the section of “5.2.4 Daylight Study - 
Facade design”, where the surrounding buildings and the wanted penetration 
length of the daylight were taking into consideration. In table 5.40 the specific 
glazing areas for each floor is listed. 
The specific facade design for design “B” and “C” and the different levels can 
be seen on figure 5.49.

5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
A small increase in the lighting gain for all zones is  seen for facade B compared 
to C. This is in accordance with the designs, where parts of the glazing areas 
are placed in the upper part of the facade, increasing the daylight distribution. 
The lighting gain is highest for level 4 for the zone oriented towards south, 
which is a bit unusual, but this is due to the opposing building, which is placed 
very close and blocks out the daylight. On level 9 the situation is the opposite, 
because the surrounding buildings are not shadowing, so in this case the 
south oriented zone has a lower lighting gain than the north oriented zone.
The heating and cooling loads can indirectly be derived from the lighting gain, 
because the zones with high lighting gains will be the zones with a high need 
for heating, and the zones with low lighting gain have a higher solar gain, and 
they will therefore have a higher cooling load. The heating and cooling loads 
can be seen on figure 5.51.
     
Figure 5.51: Heating and cooling load for the two facade designs B and C for the northern and southern 
oriented zones for level 4 and 9. 
The heating loads do not show much difference between design B and C. 
The loads for facade C is a little bit higher. Level 9 with the northern oriented 
zones has the highest heating loads. Partly because the northern orientated 
zones lack the solar gain, and level 9 has a higher heat loss through external 
facades, because the zone has some external roof. 
The second highest heating loads are seen for the southern oriented zone on 
level 4, because the opposing building are placed so close to the facade that 
it blocks out the sun. 
The lowest heating loads are seen for level 9 with southern oriented zones, 
because they have a high solar gain.
The difference between the two facade designs B and C is quite small for the 
cooling loads as well, whereas facade B has a bit higher load. The highest 
loads are seen for the south oriented zones, with level 9 having the highest 
load, because the solar gain is bigger. For the northern zones the load values 
are very close, but the load is a little bit bigger for level 9. 
==PAGE:141==
133
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
      
Figure 5.52: The percentages of hours within the temperature ranges for the two facade designs B and 
C for the southern and northern oriented zones on level 4 and 9. 
The results show a quite similar distribution of temperatures between the 
two types of facade designs. 
The south oriented zones on level 9 have the highest amount of hours with a 
temperature above 25 0C, and also the lowest amount of hours of the lowest 
temperature at 22 0C, this is because these zones have the biggest solar gain 
and therefore more warm hours, while the highest amount of cold hours is 
due to the lack of solar gain, as well as an increased heat loss, because this 
level have some external roof. 
For the lowest level, the difference between north and south oriented zones 
is not that big, while the difference increases for level 9. The conditions for 
the two zones on level 4 are very similar, because the opposite building of 
he southern facade is placed very close, and therefore is shadowing. This 
explains why more cold hours are seen for the southern oriented zone on 
level 4 than for the northern oriented zone. 
The results show very similar conditions for the two investigated designs. 
Design B has a higher lighting gain, because the window area is not exploited 
to its full potential, because of the windows from floor to ceiling. On the 
other hand this makes the solar gain a bit higher for design C. In regard to the 
heating and cooling load, design B has the highest cooling load, and design 
C has the highest heating load which is a bit strange since the design of the 
windows increase the solar gain, and therefore the heating load should be 
smaller. The difference in the heating load is not big, but it is there, and must 
be caused by some influence of the design. The floor to ceiling windows 
might spread the heat more in the room, whereas design C directs the heat 
more concentrated further into the room. 
In table 5.41 the heating and cooling load are added up numerically to see 
which design has the highest load.
As seen in the table 5.41 the total heating and cooling load for the two designs, 
B and C, are very close, design B is just 0.22 MWh higher. The difference 
between the designs is more or less non existing, but so far the designs have 
only been evaluated based on the heat balance of certain rooms. Looking at 
the energy consumption for the building will bring a bigger perspective, and 
the various differences might be more pronounced.

DISTRIBUTION OF GLAZING AREA
The previous investigations for the facade design have taken its starting 
point in the glazing areas, found in the daylight investigations, where it from 
a daylight point of view, was most optimal, if the glazing area decreased 
upwards. A situation, where the glazing areas have the same distribution for 
every level will now be investigated, to see if there is any clear differences. 
The average glazing area for the building with design C has been calculated 
to 57.22 %, which will be the glazing area for every level for the “new” design 
called “D”. The glazing area is placed in windows above a parapet, with the 
same height of 700 mm as for design C. The windows have a width of 1.5 m 
and are placed with a distance of 420 mm. An elevation of the facade can be 
seen on figure 5.53. The same levels and zones as previous investigations will 
be evaluated. With design D, the biggest differences will probably be seen for 
the lowest levels, which had a higher glazing area with design C. Figure 5.54 
shows how the heating and cooling load are affected.
     
Figure 5.54: Heating and cooling load for the two facade designs C and D for the northern and southern 
oriented zones for level 4 and 9. 
Figure 5.53: Elevation of facade design 
D.

5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
Level 4 has a lower heating load for design D for both orientations, which 
is a bit unexpected since the glazing area is reduced from 70% to 57.22%, 
and therefore the solar gain is reduced. Reducing the glazing area, however, 
decreases the heat loss through the external facades, which must be the 
explanation, together with the fact that the effect from the solar gain for the 
southern orientation, is quite limited, as the opposing building is placed so 
close, blocking for the sun. 
The heating load has increased for design D for the northern and southern 
orientation on level 9, which must be caused by a small increase in the glazing 
area from 55% to 57.22%, increasing the heat loss. It is a very small increase 
in the glazing area, but must be the reason for the higher heating load. 
The cooling load has decreased for level 4 for both orientations for design 
D, because the glazing area is reduced and thereby the heat from the solar 
radiation. For level 9 the cooling load has increased for design D for both 
orientations. This might be caused by a small increase in the glazing area 
from 55% to 57.22%.

the total loads are highest for design C.

It can be seen that the energy consumption for lighting is lower for design D. 
This is noticeable, since the design for design C is developed from a daylight 
optimisation point of view. It is not a logic outcome, that when decreasing 
the glazing areas on the lowest floors, where the daylight is more limited, 
that the overall consumption for electrical light should be lower. Even though 
reducing the glazing areas on the lowest floors means that the glazing area 
also have been increased on the upper floors to have the same total glazing 
area, the consumption shouldn’t be decreased, because the upper floors 
have more than enough daylight already, compared to the lower floors, as 
seen in section “5.2 Daylight Investigations”.  It is assumed that the reason 
for the difference lies within the plugin af Radiance. How the calculation 
method exactly is done in Radiance, is not known, but there must be some 
issues causing this difference.

boilers for design D, which is interesting since this design had overall lower 
heating and cooling loads. 
Design D, has a 0.2 KWh/m2 higher energy consumption, which is such a small 
difference that it can be discarded. The further investigations will, however, 
be based on design C, since this design is based on the desired concept from 
the architects.   
EVALUATION
It’s difficult to simulate complex solar shading systems in IESVE. The most 
optimal solution was however found to be louvres, which is controlled by the 
irradiation on the facade.
For the investigations of the facade design, the concept of having a horizontal 
distribution of the glass area, design C proved to give the best result. The 
glazing areas for this facade are decreasing floor by floor upwards, and when 
this concept was compared to having an equal glass area for each floor, no 
real differences were seen. 
In the following the optimal solution for solar shading and design C will be 
combined to see, if this might reduce the cooling load and thereby energy 
consumption. 
The heating an cooling loads for design C and design C with solar shading, 
called CS can be seen on figure 5.56.
==PAGE:145==
137
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
         
Figure 5.56: The heating and cooling loads for design C and C with solar shading, design CS.
The heating loads have increased for CS because the solar gain is reduced 
due to the solar shading. The increase in the heating load is, however, 
minor compared to the reduction in the cooling load. The cooling load was 
especially really high for the southern oriented zone on level 9, and when the 
solar shading is added, the load is the lowest. 
The temperature distribution is seen on figure 5.57 for design C and CS.
Figure 5.57: The percentage of hours with the certain temperatures. 
The temperatures have decreased, so there is no hours above 25 0C and 
more hours with the other temperatures. More hours are also seen with 
temperatures of 22 0C, because the solar gain is reduced. Furthermore the 
differences between north and south orientated zones are reduced, so the 
conditions are more uniform for all zones. This is because the solar shading is 
used more often for the south orientations, decreasing the solar gain.

5.3.3 REDUCTION OF GLAZING AREA
The investigated designs have so far been based on glazing areas found 
from daylight investigations, see section “5.2.4 Daylight study - Design of 
Facades”, with a concept of decreasing glazing areas upwards floor by floor. 
From the previous investigation it was seen that there was not any significant 
difference in any aspect of either indoor climate or energy consumption 
between the concept of decreasing glazing areas or equal glazing areas. In 
the following it will be investigated, how the indoor environment and energy 
consumption will be affected when reducing the glazing area by 10% step. 
The percentage of glazing in a glass building is a continuous discussion with 
no precise answer, but now it will be investigated how much change is seen 
in the indoor climate and energy consumption when reducing the glass area 
in steps of 10% from 57.2 %.
To investigate the effect of a lower glazing area, the glazing area of design C is 
reduced 10% on all levels, called design C10. The glazing areas for each floor 
can be seen from table 5.47. The heating and cooling load, can be seen on 
figure 5.58 for design C and C10. 
               
Figure 5.58: Heating and cooling load for the two facade designs C and C10  for the northern and 
southern oriented zones for level 4 and 9. 
As expected the heating and cooling loads have decreased for design C10 for 
all investigated zones. 
The heating loads have decreased, because the heat loss through the external 
facade is reduced. A similar reduction is seen for all the zones, except for the 
southern oriented zone on level 9, which is the zone wiht the lowest heat 
load for design C. When the ‘same’ reduction in the heating load, is not seen 
for this zone it has to be seen in relation to the fact, that this floor benefits 
from the solar gain, so therefore the effect is not as distinct as for the other 
zones, because the reduced solar gain almost equals the reduced heat loss.  
The cooling loads have decreased equally, because the solar gain is reduced. 
The effect on the temperatures from reducing the glazing area can be seen 
on figure 5.59.

so the glazing area is reduced to 37.2 % for the entire building. The design 
is adjusted to the lower glazing area by increasing the height of the parapet 
from 700 mm to 850 mm and then adjusting the distances between the 
windows accordingly to match the glazing area. 
As the lowest possible glazing area, an investigation have been made, where 
the glazing area has been reduced to 30 % for each floor. The building 
regulation [SBi 230, 2014] states that the glazing area should constitute 10 % 
of the floor area, which is still fulfilled, when considering the glazing between 
the offices and atriums as seen in table 5.48. 
The results from the investigations of C20 and C30 in regards to heating and 
cooling load and temperatures is, however, not shown here, since the same 
tendencies are seen as when reducing 10 %. The results can, however, be 
seen in “Appendix G.3 - Results from IESVE”.

5.3.4 RADIANT TEMPERATURE ASYMMETRY
Glass can get warm in the summer and cold in the winter depending on the 
outside temperature. Due to the long wave radiation between surfaces this 
can lead to discomfort for the occupant sitting by the windows, because there 
is differences in the distribution of heat gain and losses on each side of the 
body. This  situation can be described by the radiant temperature asymmetry, 
for further detail see chapter “3.4.2 Solar Radiation”. Investigations show, 
that occupants are less tolerable towards 
cold thermal radiation from a vertical window than warm thermal radiation. 
The radiant temperature asymmetry from cold vertical surfaces will now be 
investigated. According to [DS 474] the radiant temperature asymmetry from 
cold vertical surfaces must not exceed 10 0C. 
In order to find the radiant temperature asymmetry the temperature of the 
glass inside has to be found. This can be done by finding the temperature 
distribution throughout the construction. 
When calculating the temperature through the construction, the thermal 
conductivity is needed for every layer. This value is not known for the cavity 
of argon, between the glass layers. Knowing the total U-value for the window 
the equivalent thermal conductivity for the argon and low emissivity coating 
can be found. This calculation is based on the 3-layered glazing type 3.2 with 
an U-value of 0.6 W/m2K.
In the following table the layers, their thicknesses and thermal conductivities 
are listed. 
Using the following equations the resistance, temperature difference and 
temperatures can be found for each layer:
==PAGE:150==
142
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
                                          
An inside temperature of 22 0C and an outside temperature of -12 0C is 
assumed. Tabel 5.49A shows the temperature through the layers of the 
window.

Table 5.49A: The temperature throughout the window construction.
The inside surface temperature of the window will be 19.34 0C. 
Having found the inside surface temperature, the radiant temperature 
asymmetry can also be found. 
The radiant asymmetry will be calculated for the worst case scenario. The 
worst case scenario will be the vertical facade design with windows from 
floor to ceiling. Caused by the division of the glazing area, where the area is 
decreasing upwards, the widest window will be seen for level 4 with a width 
of approximately 2.3 meters.  The radiant asymmetry will be caulculated for 
a person placed at the center of the window with a distance of 0.6 meters. 
This distance is a guideline for the distance to the occupied zone, when sitting 
next to a big window, according to [DS 469]. 
The angle factors for the surrounding surfaces to the person have to be 
calculated. This is done by using the graphs for angle relation between 
surfaces [Danvak].
The person is assumed to be a plane with the dimensions of height = 1.1m 
and width = 0.6m. The first step is to calculate the angle factor between 
the person and the window. The window is therefore divided in 4 sections, 
rectangles to be able to use one of the graphs. The division and dimensions 
can be seen on figure 5.60. 
Figure 5.60: The rectangles and dimen­
sions, used to find the angle factors. 
==PAGE:151==
143
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
The rectangels of 1 + 2 and 3 + 4 are the same, just mirrored so the angle 
factor is the same for these. In the following the angle factor are found for 
the 2 rectangles.
Rectangle 1 = 2 : 
a = 1.15	
	
b = 2.15	
	
c = 0.6
b/c = 2.15/0.6 = 3.58	 	
	
a/c = 1.15/0.6 = 1.92
F = 0.215
Rectangle 3 = 4:
a = 1.15	
	
b = 0.55	
	
c = 0.6
b/c = 0.55/0.6 = 0.92	 	
	
a/c = 1.15/0.6 = 1.92
F = 0.158
Fwindow = 2 · 0.215 + 2 · 0.158 = 0.746
The angle factor is the relation between the amount of energy radiated from 
one surface to another and the total amount of energy radiated towards a 
hemisphere [Rode et al., 2010]. Therefore the anglefactors on the right side 
of the person have to be 1 added up, so the angle factor can be calculated for 
the remaining part of the right side:
Fremaining = Ftotal - Fwindow = 1 - 0.746 = 0.254
It is open plan offices and therefore the working space isn’t limited by 
surrounding walls. The interior are assumed to be 22 0C, because the heating 
set point are defined for this. The inside temperature of the outer wall 
(insulated parts) are assumed to have the same temperature, even though 
this is a bit optimistic.
The emissivity of a human being is found to approximately 0.9.
The emissivity for the window is 0.94 [Rode et al., 2010]. 
It is difficult to determine the emissivity for the interior, because it is an open 
plan office so the persons are not surrounded by walls. An emissivity of the 
surroundings is assumed to be 0.9, which is the approximately emissivity for 
building materials. 
Then the mean radiant temperature can be found for the external side of the 
person. 
It is assumed the person has a temperature of 30 0C [Rode et al., 2010].
==PAGE:152==
144
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
Since the surroundings on the interior side of the person is assumed to be 22 
0C, this is the mean radiant temperature.
The radiant temperature asymmetry:
∆tpr = 22 0C - 20.023 0C = 1.98 0C
According to [DS 474] the radiant asymmetry by windows and other cold 
vertical surfaces in relation to a small vertical surface 0.6 m above floor can 
not exceed 10 0C , which is fulfilled. The radiant asymmetry is very low, so 
there is no risk of discomfort from high radiant asymmetry. The optimistic 
assumption of the external wall, the insulated parts, having the same surface 
temperature as the inside can be concluded to be acceptable, because even 
though the surface temperature was decreased a few degrees, it would not 
change the radiant asymmetry much, and there would still not be any risk of 
discomfort. 
Using the same approach table 5.50 below lists the radiant asymmetry for 
the other 3-layered glazing, with only one low emissivity coating as well as 
the 2-layered glazing used in the atriums. The lowest temperature occurring 
in the atrium with the heating set point of 15 0C in the occupied hours and 
10 0C outside the occupied hours is 10 0C, so this situation is investigated. 
Furthermore the situation where the 2-layered glazing is used in the external 
facade, is investigated as well.

5.3.5 NATURAL VENTILATION IN ATRIUM
In this section the openings in the atrium, which are used for natural 
ventilation, will be investigated. The purpose for the investigation is to find 
the preferred combination of openings by variating the opening area, opening 
angle of the window and the degree of opening (modulated profile) as the 
only parameters. For analysing the investigations the first parameter to be 
evaluated is the operative temperature, in IESVE defined as the dry resultant 
temperature, for level 4, 6 and 9. It is tried to accomplish a temperature span 
of 200C - 250C, when only investigating the occupied hours 8 am - 5 pm on 
weekdays. 
The investigations for the openings in the atrium are based on the “basic 
model A”, completely glazed, with framing area at 30%, which leads to a 70% 
glazed area. The heating set point in the atrium is 150C, the glazing type in 
the atrium is type 2.2+2.2, and for the office it is type 3.2 including louvres, 
as found in the previous chapter “5.3.2 Facade design”. For the louvres the 
temperature controlled modulated profile is not added due to limitations of 
IESVE, as previous described. 
The settings for the natural ventilation is a 20% opening area with an opening 
angle at 250 and a “degree of opening (modulated profile)” at 250C with 
opening in the range of 40, and therefore they start opening at 230C and fully 
open at 270C. In figure 5.61 the basic model for the atrium is illustrated by 
the percentage of hours within the specified temperature ranges, which will 
be the starting point for the investigations of the openings in the atrium.
Figure 5.61: Percentage of hours within the different temperature ranges in atrium north for the basic 
model at level 4, 6 and 9.
Figure 5.61 shows more than 25 % hours in the temperature range 25 - 
300C, which is indicator for overheating, and will need to be decreased. To 
decrease the overheating hours, the investigations will be divided into the 
three following combination types:
==PAGE:154==
146
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
Type 1: 	
Openings in top and bottom in the facade (level 1+3) and in 
	
	
the roof (level 9+10)
Type 2: 	
Openings in every second level in the facade (1+3+5+7) and in 
	
	
the roof (9+10)
Type 3: 	
Openings in all levels in the facade (1-10) and in the roof 
	
	
(9+10)
For all three combination types the changing factors are the opening area 
in the facade and roof, the opening angle of the windows and the degree 
of opening by a modulated profile. The three combination types will be 
investigated and further optimized separately, by at first finding the optimal 
opening area and then look further at the two other parameters. 
TYPE 1
The first parameter to optimize for the type 1 is the opening area of the 
facade. The opening principle for type 1 is illustrated in figure 5.62. The 
openings in both facade and roof have been tested for an opening area of 
20%, 25% and 30%. In this investigation 30% is seen as the maximal possible, 
since it is considered as a big area. As a further test the percentage opening 
area for the facade and roof is differentiated to respectively 10% facade and 
20% roof, 10% facade and 30% roof as well as 20% facade and 30% roof. The 
heating set point in the atrium is 150C, and therefore there is no temperatures 
below 100C, and the percentage of temperatures between 100C and 150C 
more or less only occurs at level 9. Increasing the opening area results in 
more hours in the range of 250C - 300C than in 300C - 350C, so the overheated 
hours are reduced. The two types as seen in table 5.51, which will be further 
investigated, are the basic model (a) with 20% for both facade and roof, and 
the combination (b) with 20% for the facade and 30% for the roof, as they 
have a higher percentage hours of temperatures in the range from 150C - 
300C for level 4 and 6 compared to tests with other opening combinations. 
To further improve the two solutions from type 1 the opening percentage of 
the windows is investigated with an opening angle at 150 and 200 for both 
(a) and (b) and with a modulating profile at 200C and 220C. When minimizing 
the opening angle the percentage of overheated hours increased with a few 
percent, and when lowering the set point for the modulating profile, the 
percentage of overheated hours was decreased significantly. Further results 
are available in “Appendix G.2 - Natural ventilation”.
For type 1 the preferred combination, called (a.2), has more temperatures in 
the range 150C - 200C compared to 250C - 300C, accomplished by an opening 
at 20%, an opening angle at 250 and a modulating profile at 200C with a 
opening span at 40C, and thereby starts opening at 180C and is fully open at 
22 0C. The solution can be seen in the figure 5.65 for all three types. 
TYPE 2

are two selected combinations (c) and (d), when first investigating the 
optimal opening area. (c) has 30% opening area in both facade and roof, 
where (d) has 30% open in the facade and 20% in the roof. The two selected 
combinations have the highest percentage of hours within the temperature 
range from 150C - 250C, compared to the other combinations and they were 
therefore further investigated. The opening angle and the modulating profile

were again tested for an opening angle at respectively 200 and 150 and the

From the graph in figure 5.65 the combination (2.e) is evaluated as the best 
due to minimal overheating hours in the temperature range 300C - 350C, but 
the temperature range is not the only thing to determine, which is the best 
solution. Since the air velocity is also a significant factor, when designing the 
facade openings in the atrium. Therefore some very simple calculations have 
been made to give an indication, whether there will be risk of very high air 
velocity.
NATURAL VENTILATION
The overall purpose of ventilation in wintertimes is to have good indoor air 
quality, but at the same time avoid discomfort related to draft, and in the 
summertimes the ventilation is mainly used to avoid overheating. For natural 
ventilation the purpose is the same. Different types of natural ventilation is 
used in office buildings; night cooling or in daytimes by natural ventilation. 
There are three overall strategies for natural ventilation; one-side ventilation, 
cross ventilation and buoyancy ventilation [Jensen, 2005]. The buoyancy is 
caused by the density of the external air and internal air, which primarily 
is caused by temperature differences, and thereby the difference in air 
pressure and external wind conditions force the air through the openings in 
the thermal envelope.  In the case of T30 it is mainly the strategy of buoyancy 
ventilation used but the concept of one-side ventilation might influence the 
conditions in the atrium as well.
The maximum air velocity for open space offices is 0.25 m/s for category C, 
which is a moderate expected level [DS/CEN/CR 1752, 2001]. The atrium is 
considered as a recreative area having a higher activity level, and therefore 
higher air velocities can be accepted. The maximum air velocity in the atrium 
is therefore accepted at 0.50 m/s for the purpose of this project.  
As a simple estimation of the differences of air velocities in the atrium an 
average air velocity in level 4, 6 and 9 is calculated from the measured volume 
air flow out in IESVE and the area of the zone. For level 4 and 6 the area is 400 
m2 and for level 9 the atrium area is 160 m2 and the rooflight is 240 m2. The

maximum value at 0.50 m/s, as described earlier. 
Another simplified way to give an indication of the air velocities in the atrium 
is by calculating the central air speed in a certain distance from the opening 
,as well as the penetration length. The central air velocity is defined as the 
center of the inlet air flow in a straight horizontal line from the inlet. The 
central air velocity is given by the following equation [Jensen, 2005]:
vx

the factor is 3.6 for rectangular openings
h

x 	
distance from the opening

the opening in the facade, and in the back of the atrium at 20 m from the 
opening in the facade. These two distances from the facade are investigated 
due to the architectural concept of utilizing the zones next to the internal 
facades for recreative areas such as terrasses. If the air velocities in these 
zones are too high, the terrasses will in all probability not be used.

SUM UP
Based on the previous investigations for both percentage of hours within 
different temperature ranges, and the simple calculations of air velocities in 
the atrium, the preferred solution for the design of openings in the facade 
is type 3 (e.2). Here there is mainly temperatures in the preferred range 
of 150C - 250C and a limited percentage of hours in the range 250C - 350C, 
which range it will be characterized as overheating. For further investigations 
==PAGE:159==
151
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
of the air velocities in the atrium it was only considered as a bit too high 
for type 2 at level 5 and 7, the preferred combination for openings in the 
atrium, is still type 3 (e.2). Again it is important to underline the simplicity 
of these calculations, and they are therefore a limited indication of the exact 
conditions in the atrium. There is a lack of further parameters, such as the 
total impact from several inlets and the related turbulence and mix of air, 
the buoyancy created by the temperature differences in the atrium, and the 
outlets in the roof as well as the external impacts from wind and orientation. 
The simple calculations are only made to give an indication, if there is a risk of 
draft and high air velocities in the atrium. For further detailed investigations 
a CFD analysis would be needed, which is not a part of this thesis. Therefore 
the final solution might not be completely realistic, but it gives an explanation 
and an idea of the concept for the natural ventilation.
5.3.6 FINAL MODEL EVALUATION
Some of the results from the previous investigations have been implemented 
investigation by investigation, but not all. 
A sum up of the outcome of the results will be described in the following, 
and then combined in a final model. 
The most significant results of the models during the investigations will be 
put in relation to the final model, to see the effect on the energy consumption 
for the different approaches. 
The most optimal glazing combination in the atrium was the combination 
of 2.2+2.2. In regard to the heating set point, the best solution was chosen 
to be 15 0C, but having 9 0C or unheated, all had energy consumptions very 
close. 
For the external facade, the best solution was seen for the glazing type 3.2 
with 2 low emissivity coatings. This type reduced the heating load quite a lot, 
while only increasing the cooling load a bit.  
For solar shading, the best solution was seen for louvres with transmission 
factors of 0.65 for 0 degrees increment, 0.4 for 15 degrees increment and 0.2 
for 30 degrees increment. The louvres are controlled by the irradition on the 
surface, and will be lowered at 150 W/m2 and raised at 100 W/m2. 
The facade design of C was seen to be the best solution overall, and savings 
were seen when combining this solution with the solar shading. 
The best conditions in the atriums were seen for having openings on each 
level, with an area of 20 %. Furthermore the openings are attached to a 
profile, so they start to open when the temperature reaches 20 0C and are 
fully opened at 24 0C. 
In the following table 5.56 the difference between the most significant results 
are seen in relation to each other. 
The “starting point” is the 100 % glazed building with solar shading. 
The next results are from the model with facade design C, and then with

Table 5.57: Consumptions for electrical light from DaySim and IESVE. All the consumptions are specified

The table show significant differences between the total energy consumption 
for the results in IESVE, and when using the electrical light consumption as 
found in IESVE. When calculating the total energy consumption for model 
C without solar shading (the biggest difference is seen for this situation) a 
reduction of 12 KWh/m2/year is seen, and thereby the requirements from 
Building Class 2010 is fulfilled. 
Glass is a complex material. As table 5.57 indicates, approaches which 
benefit in one aspect, is a disadvantage in another aspect. The most efficient 
mean for reducing the heating load is to reduce the glazing area, which 
increases the electricity consumption for light. One mean for reducing the 
cooling load is to decrease the glazing area as well, but it can be seen that it is 
more efficient if using solar shading, while combining these two reduces the 
cooling further, but then has the disadvantage of increasing the heating load.
There is different adjustable approaches for reducing the energy consumption, 
but common for all is that the difference is not that pronounced, and that 
other steps have to be taken in order to fulfill any energy performance 
framework. The final model, CS, is still approximately 6 KWh/m2 too high 
according to Building Class 2010. This points in the direction that the amount 
of glass is not the essential factor, if still taking precautious steps as having 
an efficient solar shading and a low U-value for the glass, but that it is more 
important that steps are taken towards energy saving- or energy producing 
actions as solar cells or to improve the efficiency of the building systems. 
The type of glazing also has to be considered, since it also has a direct effect 
on the comfort of the occupants. Radiant temperature asymmetry can be a 
problem with big glazed areas, but as long as the properties of the glazing is 
considered, this problem can be avoided. Having 3-layered glazing types, no 
problems at all were seen in relation to cold temperature asymmetry from 
the windows. 
==PAGE:162==
154
5. TECHNICAL INVESTIGATIONS
5.4 SUB-CONCLUSION
Problematic aspects relating to glass buildings have been investigated within 
daylight, indoor environment and energy consumption. 
During the investigations experiences have been made concerning the 
limitations and possibilities within the different used programs. An important 
factor when building with glass is the increased daylight distribution, and how 
the energy consumption can benefit from this. The consumption for electrical 
light stands for a great share of the total energy consumption. Therefore it is 
not insignificant, how it is simulated, and whether this is in accordance with 
the reality, or to the extent to which this is possible to predict. The energy 
consumption for electrical light has been calculated in DaySim and IESVE, and 
significant differences were seen. In DaySim the levels can be divided into 
zones with different requirements for the level of illuminance and thereby 
the program can simulate a more realistic situation. In IESVE the levels can as 
well be divided into zones, each having a sensor for the level of illuminance, 
but there is a conflict of interest between the wanted division of zones in 
relation to electrical light and in relation to evaluation of indoor environment 
and energy perspectives, because it is only possible to make one common 
division. To accommodate this, programs have to be combined or several 
models in the same program investigating each their aspect have to be made. 
So it’s important to know the limitations and possibilities within the wide 
range of programs to use. In IESVE the plugin Radiance was used to simulate 
the daylight and lighting more precisely but only very small differences were 
seen in the ligting consumption, when the glazing area was changed and 
solar shading added, compared to the differences in the consumption from 
DaySim. Even though the division wasn’t optimal in IESVE it’s still strange 
that no differences are really seen for example for design C with or without 
solar shading. It is important that the program is chosen with focus on the 
subject for the investigation.
The daylight distribution has been evaluated with two programs, and by the 
methods of DF and UDI. DF was investigated in both of the programs Velux 
Daylight Visualizer (VDV) and DaySim, and some inconsistencies were seen 
between the results, but it is difficult to say what the reason for this is. DF 
in VDV is a easy method to get an idea of the daylight conditions and is also 
a good tool to use in the designing phase, while DaySim and UDI provide 
more detailed results. DF is only described for the overcast sky condition, 
and therefore doesn’t describe the daylight conditions for other situations. It 
is kind of a method to be on the safe side, if 2 % Daylight Factor is obtained, 
then lower daylight distributions won’t occur. The disadvantage of the 
method is, that situations with too much light isn’t described, and this causes 
visual discomfort. UDI describes how much time within the useful range (not 
too low or too high) of daylight there is.

6. CONCLUSION
==PAGE:166==
158
6.1 CONCLUSION
Throughout the thesis several aspects of building envelopes with glass have 
been investigated. Glass is an antagonistic material, where the qualities of 
glass also can cause disadvantages. To clarify the problematics and qualities 
when building with glass, interviews were made with the two relevant 
professions of architects and engineers. Through this process as well as 
following a process in praxis a holistic perspective have been acquired. 
It is the transparency of glass, which makes it a desired and attractive material. 
It adds another dimension of space, from room basis, to building basis and 
further to the surrounding context. This bring quality to the building, but 
only if the design is conducted with an eye for the problematic aspects, glass 
can cause. 
Glass in building design is often related to higher energy consumption and 
problematic indoor environment, due to high heating and cooling loads. The 
assumption is often that the more glass, the more these issues are pronounced 
and to a certain extent, the critique is appropriate. Investigations, however, 
show that the effect of reducing the glazing area is not that effective as one 
would expect. 
Another approach for reducing the heating and cooling load is solar shading, 
and in the running between these two approaches the solar shading actually 
seems to have the biggest effect. To have the same influence on the energy 
consumption as an effective solar shading have on an all glazed building, the 
glazing area has to be decreased to approximately 40 %. 
Glass increases the amount of daylight, but more daylight is not necessarily 
equal to better indoor conditions, because it can cause visual discomfort. 
Precautions have to be taken to avoid glare and too big contrasts in the field 
of view. 
Decreasing the glazing area, is one method to reduce the daylight, but a 
more optimal method towards these issues is the use of solar shading, which 
reduces the daylight more equal. 
As seen in IESVE, the use of solar shading proved to be efficient towards the 
energy consumption as well. The disadvantage concerning solar shading is 
just that it jeopardise the quality of the glass, by limiting the transparency 
and the view out. If it is necessary to have solar shading covering the glazing 
most of the time, then what is the point of the glass? So it is a balance 
between maintaining the desired qualities, without jeopardising the indoor 
environment and energy consumption.
The solar shading proved to be efficient in IESVE, but it has to be considered that 
the conditions for the shading isn’t really clarified in IESVE. It is not specified, 
how much of the time the solar shading is in use, which is very relevant for 
evaluation of the effect. Furthermore the development of solar shading in 
IESVE is quite limited, so it is only possible to simulate simple controlling 
systems. Several programs have been in use during the investigations, and 
the limitations and possibilities within these are important to consider in 
relation to the subject of investigation and the outcome. 
==PAGE:167==
159
Investigations have been carried out in relation to glazing types and overall 
concepts for facade designs, and as described, different glazing areas and 
solar shading. Within these parameters it is possible to optimise the solutions, 
which is important to obtain the best solutions. When adding solar shading 
further improvements were seen, but still it was not enough for obtaining 
any energy performance framework, which fulfilled any building class.
 
The consumption for electrical light was investigated in both DaySim and 
IESVE, and with the possibility to detail different lighting zones in DaySim, 
it was possible to calculate a more realistic consumption, which reduced 
the total energy consumption quite a bit. This indicates that it is quite 
important, how the different systems are modelled to get the “right” result 
and consideration and knowledge should be put into this. 
With the different optimisation steps, an energy performance framework 
fulfilling building class 2010 was possible, but there is quite a way to fulfilling 
the building class 2015. More extensive steps towards energy production 
by solar cells or optimisations in the building systems to really save energy 
have to be taken to move the energy consumption further. The study shows, 
that when reasonable precautions are taken as choosing the energy effcient 
glazing type, and using some type of solar shading system, then the glazing 
area is not crucial, and there is a range of freedom in the design of the 
facade. The freedom in the design extends, the more optimised the building 
systems are executed, and here lies the potential for architectural quality, 
which might be compromised in favour of rational energy efficient buildings. 
In extension to this the cooperation between the architects and the engineers 
is of great importance for the final result. The cooperation between the 
architects and engineers in the process of the early stage of T30 was an 
example of a beneficial cooperation. Investigations were made, which 
influenced and adjusted the design accordingly. This aspect is difficult to 
control and measure and make guidelines for but it is important to optimise 
solutions especially for a complex project as glass buildings are. 
==PAGE:168==
